==PAGE:1==
Master Thesis
Experimental Investigation on the Quality of 
Composite Aerospace Components after Milling 
and Drilling
Helene Birkebæk 
August 2018
==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Carbon ﬁbre reinforced polymer (CFRP) composites play an increasingly dominant and signiﬁcant role in
the world of aerospace. The material is incredibly strong, lightweight and corrosion resistant, making it ideal
for surviving the harsh environment an airplane will experience during ﬂight and provide energy eﬃciency.
Composites are composed of individual layers containing carbon ﬁbres, which are uniformly distributed
within a plastic matrix. The layers are stacked together in a mould to obtain their ﬁnal form, however it
is often necessary to perform post-machining operations to obtain dimensional accuracy and a high surface
quality. Milling and drilling are two of the main ﬁnishing procedures, which present numerous challenges
due to the inhomogeneous and abrasive nature of the composite structure. This can be seen in the form of
ﬁbre pull-outs, the separation of individual layers, referred to as delaminations, and intensive tool wear. As
such, evaluating the quality of the composite part after milling and drilling is essential in ensuring correct
machining parameters are utilised along with a high mechanical strength being maintained.
This study concentrated on delaminations introduced in CFRP composites during milling and drilling,
more speciﬁcally the machining operations of slot milling and countersinking were considered. For milling,
the focus was on determining the best machining parameters in order to obtain a high surface ﬁnish and
avoid delaminations. This was done by performing a slot milling operation using a range of spindle speeds
and feed rates, where the inﬂuence of tool wear was also considered. The experiment was conducted using
a four-ﬂute end mill from Unimerco and a Franken Fiber-Cut eight-ﬂute serrated end mill to evaluate the
eﬀect of tool geometry. The quality of the machined part was based on surface roughness and delamination
frequency, where measurements were made in the transverse and longitudinal direction, thus across and
along the milled surface, respectively. For Franken Fiber-Cut an increase in feed rate generally resulted in
a deterioration of surface quality, where the optimum tooling conditions were found at a low feed rate and
using a medium to high speed. For Unimerco, an initial increase in feed rate lead to a lower surface roughness,
however increasing the feed rate further resulted in a lower surface quality. As such, the optimum machining
parameters were found at the intermediate feed rate using a medium to high spindle speed. Common for both
tools was that the spindle speed did not show a general trend on roughness. The tool geometry was found to
have a pronounced eﬀect on the machined surface and a higher roughness and delamination frequency was
found for the four-ﬂute end mill from Unimerco. Measurements made in the transverse direction were found
to more adequately describe the surface roughness, however issues with the contact-based approach were
found in terms of accurately capturing the surface quality of individual plies. This was further conﬁrmed by
optical measurements, where the roughness was found to vary greatly across each composite layer.
The focus for the countersinking experiment was on determining the correlation between delaminations
induced during machining and the mechanical strength. This was done by performing an open hole tensile
and compression test on samples, which had sustained surface delaminations prior to testing. The amount of
damage was categorised using two delamination factors along with the average delamination depth and then
compared to the obtained strength results. For tensile testing there was found to be no correlation between
damage sustained to the surface prior to testing and the mechanical strength, and the same observations
were made in terms of strain. The compression test revealed the same, where the delaminations did not
have an eﬀect on the mechanical strength nor the strain. Two samples containing a honeycomb core were
also evaluated and surface delaminations did not appear to have an eﬀect on the compressive strength. In
conjunction with the mechanical testing, an experiment for determining the void content of the composite
part was conducted due to the adverse eﬀect a high content can have on the strength. The results revealed a
signiﬁcantly low void content, which was in compliance with the 1% limit set by the aerospace industry.

Preface
This master thesis has been submitted as the ﬁnal requirement for obtaining a Msc. degree in Material and
Manufacturing Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The project has a scope equivalent to
32½ ETCS credits and was completed during the time period of the 2nd of January, 2018 to the 2nd of August,
2018. The prolonged study period is due to a 5 ETCS credit course being completed parallel to the master
thesis.
The study was done as a collaboration between the Department of Mechanical Engineering at DTU and
Terma A/S. The milling experiments were done in the Workshop of Building 427, DTU, and the mechanical
tests were performed in the laboratory at Terma, Grenaa.
The project was supervised by professor Leonardo De Chiﬀre from the Department of Mechanical Engineering
at DTU and Christian Bay Alexandersen Manager Q.A. Programs at Terma.
Kongens Lyngby, Denmark, August 2, 2018
Helene Birkebæk

Acknowledgement
This master thesis could not have been completed without the help, support and cheer-leading from a range of
amazing individuals. First I would like to thank Prof. Leonardo De Chiﬀre, who provided essential professional
guidance and always had the time to reply to emails, and Christian Bay Alexandersen for allowing me to
collaborate with Terma and ask an endless amount of questions. The slot milling experiments at DTU could
not have been done without the help from Peter Dausell, who manage to transform my drawings into a
milled pattern on a composite part. Likewise, I would like to thank Mathias Krause Kristensen at Terma
for understanding my ideas with regards to mechanical testing and helping with turning these into the ﬁnal
product seen in the report along with answering about a thousand questions. Well done. Klaus Liltorp also
deserves a special thanks due to the enormous help with the roughness measurements, both contact and
non-contact based. I would like to thank Michael Pedersen at Terma. For his constant support, cheerleading
and encouragement and for his ”nothing is impossible” attitude. Finally, I would like to thank everyone
involved in the project who I have not mentioned already. To the amazing people at Terma who allowed me
to interrupt their daily life and thoughtfully answered all my questions. To the staﬀat DTU Mechanical
Engineering and DTU Compute, who answered a whole range of questions from the theory behind slot milling
to statistical analysis of machining parameters. Thank you.

Chapter 1
Research Scope and Objective
1.1
Scope
This project deals with the manufacturing of aerospace composite components with focus on the milling and
drilling machining operations. It started out with an overall idea of collaborating with Terma, more speciﬁcally
their Quality Assurance Department, and determining where the largest issues arose during production and
how a study could be undertaken to investigate this. As such, the project has undergone a series of iterations
and amendments to arrive at its ﬁnal form, which might become more evident as the reader dives further into
the report. Furthermore, the project has been trial and error in some parts, as based upon the many initial
discussions with Terma and DTU, I had to ﬁgure out which problems appeared most prominent and needed
immediate attention, and the best way to solve them. As such, this project has not only been an introduction
to a range of new theories, test methods and calculations, but also an experience in how to independently
tackle a complex, real-life engineering problem ensuring accurate and appropriate results.
The development of the project can be divided into several stages, culminating in an overall problem formu-
lation and enabling an investigation to begin.
 Stage 1: Discussions with Terma to evaluate, which issues are most critical. Here, the idea of key
characteristics (KCs) were introduced, which are the most vital features on an aeroplane part to get
correct in order to obtain a high quality product. These are speciﬁed by the customers. Based on this,
initial research was done into KC’s, how these were found, the importance of key characteristics, and
which issues might arise due to them.
 Stage 2: A meeting with Terma revealed that problems with machining KCs, especially milling and
drilling, were one of the main issues. After the manufacturing of carbon ﬁbre reinforced polymer (CFRP)
composites, machining operations are required in order to reach its ﬁnal dimensions. This is done in the
form of drilling for assembly purposes and milling for millimetre thickness and shape. Those machining
operations need to be closely evaluated to maintain suﬃcient quality and to remain within the tolerance
requirements. Here, it was found that the manufactured components could be divided into three levels
based on the registered dimensional measurements.
– Level 1: If a component is found at this level, a range of non-conformances have been registered
on the part, thus requires extra attention. Often further measurements are done and collected in
order to detect the most informative parameters with regards to the non-conformance. At this
stage, the main focus is on verifying and controlling the error(s).
– Level 2: A component with very few non-conformances is placed at this level. Thus, statistical
measurements, such as statistical process control (SPC) and key characteristics, can be used to
monitor and control the process.
– Level 3: Level 3 contain components, which are stable and have no signiﬁcant non-conformances.
As such, the focus is on optimizing the amount of SPC and KC data in order to only include the
1
==PAGE:22==
1.2. Objective
Chapter 1
data, which is valuable to Terma. This leads to an optimal production, as time and money can be
saved on collection unnecessary information.
 Stage 3: Terma were found to have particular problems at Level 1 to do with delaminations during slot
milling and countersinking drilling. As such, it was decided that this should be the focus of the study:
To investigate milling and drilling induced delaminations in Carbon Fibre Reinforced Polymer (CFRP)
composites.
Once the overall aim had been ﬁnalised, I had to decide the best way forward in determining the cause of the
delaminations during the two machining procedures. Through several discussions with Terma and supervisor
at DTU, the most vital aspects in terms of slot milling appeared to be the machining parameters and the
void content in the composites. Based on this, I did a design of experiment (DOE) where the spindle speed
and feed rate would be tested at three levels, for two types of tooling conditions, namely a new and worn
tool, using a four-ﬂute end mill from Unimerco and an eight-ﬂute end mill from Franken Fiber-Cut. In order
to validate the tests the surface roughness would be measured, along with conducting a visual inspection. To
check the void content and eliminate this as an issue, a constituent content of composite materials, referred to
as an acid digestion test, were done. For countersinking, which is a postliminary drilling operation, the most
important aspect appeared to be how the delaminations aﬀected the mechanical strength of the composites.
This is due to the operation being done manually and hard to optimize through a change of machining
parameters. To ensure the best tests were chosen, several discussions were conducted with Terma engineers
and an open hole tensile and compression test was found to be most suitable. The compression, tensile and
acid digestion tests were conducted at Terma, whilst the slot milling and the subsequent surface roughness
measurements were done at DTU.
As seen, the project was very dynamic and due to issues with using actual parts from Terma’s F-35 ﬁghter jet
production, several aspects of the project had to be continuously altered, which is especially prevalent in the
methodology section. Here, compromises had to be made with both the tensile and compression test due to
specimen geometry, and with the length of the slots during milling, which was because of available material.
Furthermore, a signiﬁcant part of the project was spent acquiring knowledge in order to make informed
decisions with regards to the best cause of action, which can be seen in the form of the extensive literature
review. All these factors combined made for a very interesting, self-reliant and diﬀerent project, which might
give the report something of a more spontaneous quality, however care was taken with constantly evaluating
each step and ensuring that the end result would be of signiﬁcance for Terma. Furthermore, this could not
have been done without the exceptional help from Terma and DTU supervisor.
1.2
Objective
The overall aim of this report is to investigate milling and countersinking induced delaminations on composite
parts from Terma’s F-35 ﬁghter jet production. This can be further divided into two sub-categories based on
the machining operations and their individual objective.
For slot milling the aim is to determine the best machining parameters to obtain the highest surface quality
comparing two diﬀerent end mills. This is achieved by conducting slot milling experiments on an F-35 com-
posite part from Terma at various spindle speeds and feed rates, where the eﬀect of tool wear is also taken
into account. The surface quality is evaluated based on the surface roughness and delamination measurements
using various evaluation methods. Through a quantiﬁcation of the amount of machining damage experienced
during milling a conclusion can be reached on the most appropriate feed rate and spindle speed for each end
mill. Furthermore, a comparative analysis can determine which tool leads to the highest surface quality.
For countersinking the aim is to determine whether surface delamination has an impact on the mechan-
ical performance of Terma’s F-35 parts. The objective is obtained by conducting open hole tensile and
compression tests on various CFRP composite samples, which have sustained surface damage prior to testing.
Furthermore, a Constituent Content of Composite Parts experiment will also be undertaken to investigate
the hypothesis that a high void content could have an inﬂuence on the mechanical properties. The results
2
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 1
1.2. Objective
will be interpreted graphically, visually and statistically, which will determine the impact of delaminations
on the mechanical strength.
The report consists of ten chapters, where the ﬁrst one was focused on the research scope and objective just
introduced. Chapter two is an introduction to composite materials, the manufacturing process along with
the subsequent machining operations and the associated challenges. Next, a review of the current ﬁeld of
knowledge with regards to manufacturing and machining of composite parts is presented, where particular
focus is placed on slot milling and the ﬁnishing drilling operation of countersinking. Chapter four focuses on
the experimental investigation utilised to accomplish the stated objectives for slot milling and countersinking.
The latter is concerned with open hole tensile and compression testing along with a constituent content
of composite materials experiment. The slot milling results are presented in chapter ﬁve, which is followed
by a discussion in the subsequent chapter. Chapter seven is focused on a statistical analysis of the surface
roughness results obtained in the slot milling experiment. In chapter eight, the results for the open hole
tensile and compression tests are presented along with constituent content of composite materials. These were
collected to evaluate the eﬀect of delaminations sustained during countersinking. Chapter nine is a discussion
of the results. Chapter 10 presents the conclusion, which includes recommendation for best machining practice
during slot milling, and whether surface delaminations introduced during countersinking is a serious issue.
This chapter also includes a discussion of future work.
3
==PAGE:24==
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2
Introduction
High-performing composite materials are increasingly becoming one of the most important aspects of the
aviation industry due to their excellent speciﬁc strength, resistance to corrosion, and light weight, which is
essential in achieving higher speeds. The F-35 Lightning II, which is a multi-role stealth ﬁghter jet from
Lockheed Martin, is an excellent example of the vital role of composites. Here, 35 % of the structural weight
and most of the visible surface on the ﬁghter comprise of composites (Sloan, 2009). The material often consists
of strong, stiﬀﬁbres embedded in a tough resin matrix, where wood and bone are two naturally occurring
composites. For the aerospace industry, great dependence have been placed upon carbon ﬁbre reinforced
polymer (CFRP) composites, as these have been found to have superior physical properties.
The production of composite parts can be divided into two steps, where the ﬁrst is manufacturing, and
the second is machining to meet the dimensional requirements for assembly. At Terma, the manufacturing
starts with removing the pre-impregnated composite material, which is sheets of ﬁbre infused with resin,
from the freezer, where its kept to control material lifetime. The composite parts consist of individual layers,
plies, which are cut from the defrosted roll, and transferred to a clean room. The clean room is used in
order to minimise the eﬀect of foreign particles in the layers. The layers are then placed onto a mould, which
has the desired shape of the ﬁnal product. Once the layers are correctly positioned with regards to the
orientation of the ﬁbres, and the thickness of the part, a vacuum is placed over the mould, also called a tool.
The vacuum is needed to draw out the air to ensure all trapped gas is removed. Next the tool is moved to the
autoclave for curing. Out of the furnace, the composite part is separated from the tool and sent for machining
(Alexandersen, 2018). Figure 2.0.1 illustrates the process chain of manufacturing a composite part.
Figure 2.0.1: Process chain for manufacturing of composite parts (Mohr, 2015)
5
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2
The parts are moulded to near net-shape geometry, but due to tolerance and surface ﬁnish requirements
ﬁnal machining operations are often required. At Terma, two of the main operations are slot milling and
countersinking, where the former is performed to achieve the correct thickness and dimensions of the part
(Alexandersen, 2018). Countersinking is a ﬁnishing drilling operation performed so the fastener is completely
level with the surface to eliminate aerodynamic drag. However, both these procedures present a challenge
as the CFRP composites are inhomogeneous and abrasive in nature, thus diﬃcult to machine. Damage is
often observed in the form of delaminations, which is the separation of individual plies, ﬁbre pull-outs and
disintegration of the matrix. Delamination have been found to be a particular problem in the top layer due
to only be supported by the layer below, whereas layers within the part are fully supported on both sides.
Furthermore, machining and the resulting damage can also cause changes in the mechanical and chemical
properties of the individual constituents of the part (Urban, 2001). This highlights the critical importance
of the surface condition on the performance of composites, and the need to continuously assess the quality
to ensure complete compliance with dimensional and mechanical strength requirements. One of the main
aspects of ensuring a high surface quality is the use of correct machining parameters and tool geometry,
which can be evaluated through surface roughness measurements and visual inspections. In terms of assessing
the mechanical strength the use of tensile and compression tests are essential as these are two of the main
forces the aircraft will experience during ﬂight. The presented challenges of machining composite parts
are considerably diﬀerent to the ones encountered during milling and drilling of metals, where extensive
experimental work have been done. This necessitates the need for further research into the eﬀect of surface
damage sustained during milling and drilling on the surface quality and mechanical strength of composite
parts.
6
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 3
Literature Review
This chapter will give an understanding of the existing body of knowledge on manufacturing and machining of
composite parts, along with some of the available quality assurance testing methods. This is used to validate
the framework for the study, the particular approach taken and the selection of methods. The chapter is
divided into four sections, where the initial part is focused on the manufacturing of aerospace composite parts.
Next, a basic study on the machining of composites is given, as this forms the basis for the two subsequent
sections, namely slot milling and countersinking. The former will be discussed ﬁrst, which will be split into two
parts, each focusing on separate aspects of surface quality assurance, i.e. delaminations and surface roughness.
This includes an evaluation of available testing methods to assess the surface quality. In the last section,
countersinking is considered, with particular attention on surface damage, in the form of delamination, and its
eﬀect on strength. This serves as a foundation for the next part, where focus is on establishing a link between
surface damage and strength through mechanical testing. More speciﬁcally, open hole tensile and compres-
sion testing methods will be evaluated. The literature review will reach a conclusion at the end of this chapter.
The aerospace industry is constantly looking to optimize the performance of aircraft carriers through the
development of high-performing structural materials. This is why composite materials are of particular inter-
est. They exhibit an exceptional strength and high stiﬀness-to-density ratio along with fatigue and corrosion
resistance. Composites are either engineered or naturally occurring materials consisting of two or more vastly
diﬀerent constituents, each with distinct physical and chemically properties. Generally, composites are made
of a reinforcement embedded in a matrix, where the former provides the strength and stiﬀness. The matrix
binds and keeps the reinforcement aligned as well as protecting against abrasions and the environment. The
composite materials can be categorized based on the matrix, where the primary ones are metal, ceramic,
carbon and polymer matrix composites. In the aerospace industry carbon-ﬁbre-reinforced matrices based
on thermosetting polymers, such as epoxy or polyester resins, are mainly used. These consist of stiﬀand
strong carbon ﬁbres in a polymer matrix, which is tough but neither particularly stiﬀnor strong. Thus, by
combining the properties of the matrix and the reinforcement, a new, superior material can be manufactured
(Masuelli, 2013). At Terma, Carbon Reinforced Polymer Composites, so called CFRP composites are used,
where the polymer is of the epoxy type. As such, the following literature study will mainly focus on this
(Alexandersen, 2018).
3.1
Manufacturing of Composite Parts
In order to manufacture the ﬁnal product, namely the composite material, both the polymer matrix and
carbon ﬁbre reinforcement must be produced. In the following sections a short introduction to the fabrication
methods for each constituent will be given. This is followed by a description of the production of the composite
part, including the lay-up and stacking sequence, along with the moulding process.
7
==PAGE:28==
3.1. Manufacturing of Composite Parts
Chapter 3
3.1.1
Polymers
Polymers can be divided into two groups, namely thermosets and thermoplastic, which is based on their
chemical structure. The former contains a high amount of cross-linked monomers, enabling them to withstand
high temperatures. Furthermore, the thermosets have a low viscosity and good adhesion to carbon ﬁbres
making them ideal for composite parts. As an epoxy resin is a thermoset polymer and the one utilised at
Terma, the focus will only be on this type. In order to select the correct epoxy resin, the environment in which
it will be used is a very important factor, along with tensile strength, strain and modulus, durability in service,
and glass transition temperature (Tg). The Tg of a material deﬁnes when it goes from a rigid amorphous
state, which is below Tg, to a more ﬂexible state, found above the glass transition temperature. For aerospace
applications, the material needs to be in the state below Tg, as only rigid composites are useful, thus increasing
Tg is of interest (Dodiuk and Goodman, 2014). The epoxy resin consists of three elements, namely a base
resin, curatives and modiﬁers (Masuelli, 2013). A thermoset resin is described as a cross-linking of monomers,
where in the case of epoxy resins, this happens through the reaction of an epoxy group. The presence of a
functional group in the shape of a ring, consisting of one oxygen atom and two carbon atoms, deﬁnes the
molecule as an epoxy. The variation of epoxy resins stem from the wide range of base molecules the functional
group can be attached to. The resins are produced from base molecules containing an unsaturated carbon-
carbon bond, and two processes can then be used to convert this bond into the distinctive three membered ring.
The type of base resins are generally split into two groups: Those used for high-temperature applications and
those used for low-temperature applications. When selecting which base resin to use an important factor to
consider is the glass transition temperature Tg. As mentioned previously, a high Tg is desirable for aerospace
applications, as this means the component can withstand higher temperatures without an alteration of the
chemical and physical properties of the resin. The Tg strongly depends on the curing schedule. Low tem-
perature cures, i.e. at room temperature, will result in a low Tg, whereas if the component is cured at an
elevated temperature a much higher Tg can be achieved. One way of accomplishing this is through the use
of an autoclave during the curing process. An important factor to consider, is moisture absorption as the Tg
is signiﬁcantly lowered by this aspect. Another important factor to examine is the viscosity of the resin, as a
solid or semi-solid resin is undesirable for the wet lay-up process. In order to use the composites for aerospace
applications, they must cure to yield inﬂexible structures, which can be built into the cured resin in several
ways: incorporating aromatic groups into the chemical formula, increasing the number of epoxy groups, thus
the number of reactive sites, or reducing the distance between the aforementioned groups. There are three
main classes of base resins used for composite applications, which are phenolic glycidyl ethers, aromatic
glycidyl amines and cycloaliphatics. aromatic glycidyl amine is frequently used for aerospace applications
due to its excellent high temperature properties (Pascault and Williams, 2010).
3.1.2
Carbon Fibres
The most commonly used carbon ﬁbres are polyacrylonitrile ﬁbres (PAN), pitch resins, and rayon, which are
all created from diﬀerent precursors, but the method of manufacturing is the same (Huang, 2009), (Masuelli,
2013). Initially, a stabilisation process is conducted by heating the carbon ﬁbres within a temperature range
of (200 −300)◦C for 1 h, causing the ﬁbres to attract oxygen atoms from the air, resulting in a rearrangement
of the atoms. As such, this is a conversion of the initial linear atomic bonding of the ﬁbres to a more stable
ladder bonding conﬁguration. This type of bonding is more stable as the destruction of the principle bond
involves a scisson of two or more bonds, whereas for linear polymers, there is only one central bond. Next,
is a carbonizing process, which is where the ﬁnal form of the ﬁbre is created. During this procedure, the
carbon ﬁbres are placed in a furnace, which has a temperature range of (1000 −3000)◦C, for several minutes.
The furnace contains a mixture of gases excluding oxygen, which prevents the ﬁbres from burning at high
temperatures. During the carbonizing, the ﬁbres start to lose their non-carbon atoms, mainly seen in the
form of water vapour, ammonia, hydrogen and nitrogen. A few carbon atoms will remain and form tightly
bonded carbon crystals, and align parallel to the axis of the ﬁbre, and the ﬁnal product has been produced.
The mechanical properties of the ﬁbres are greatly improved by increasing the crystallinity and orientation.
This can best be achieved by starting with a highly orientated precursor and maintaining the orientation
throughout the manufacturing process. Carbonizing leaves the ﬁbres with a less than ideal surface for bonding,
thus an oxidisation step is added, where the oxygen provides better chemical bonding properties. This can
8
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 3
3.1. Manufacturing of Composite Parts
be achieved by immersing the ﬁbres in various gasses or liquids. Subsequently, the ﬁbres are coated, usually
done with either epoxy, polyester or nylon, and wound into large threads. The ﬁnal ﬁbre has a length, which
is much greater than its diameter, where the length-diameter ratio can vary greatly for various types of ﬁbres
(Huang, 2009). At Terma continuous ﬁbres are employed in the composites, which have a long aspect ratios
and a preferred orientation. This is in contrast to discontinuous ﬁbres, which are short, and orientated in
radom directions. Examples of continuous ﬁbres are illustrated in Figure 3.1.1, where the focus in this report
will be on the ﬁrst two, namely uni-directional and woven.
Figure 3.1.1: Types of continuous ﬁbres used in carbon/epoxy composites (Maio, 2017)
3.1.3
Composites
The two constituents of the composite have now been introduced, but in order to fabricate the ﬁnal product
called a laminate, where individual sheets are referred to as a ply, the two must be joined together. Several
methods are available for this such as pultrusion, where the ﬁbres are pulled through the resin, which is
heated in a die so the material can cure, ﬁlament winding, involving the ﬁbres being pulled through a resin
and then onto a mandrel, and pre-impregnated tapes, pre-pregs. The latter is utilized at Terma, and will
thus be explained in more detail. In the pre-preg formation process, the ﬁbres are impregnated with resin, as
in the case of pultrusion, however the resin is only partial cured, and is delivered to Terma as one continuous
ply on a roll, also called a tape, and must be completely cured after the laminate has been formed. Figure
3.1.2 illustrates the production of the pre-preg tape(Callister, 2007).
Figure 3.1.2: The production of pre-preg sheets (Callister, 2007)
9
==PAGE:30==
3.1. Manufacturing of Composite Parts
Chapter 3
3.1.3.1
Lay-up and Stacking Sequence
The pre-preg tapes are used for forming the ﬁnal composite part, and once the pre-pregs have acclimatised,
the next step is to mould the composite by using a predetermined form, complying with the requirements of
the air-plane part. Initially, the sheets are cut into the correct shape, and a release agent is placed onto the
mould. The stacking of the individual plies to form a laminate is essential in ensuring the correct capabilities
along with the thickness of the part, and is speciﬁed before hand. Prior to the plies being stacked according
to the speciﬁed lay-up sequence, a few layers of sacriﬁcial plies are placed at the bottom of the mould and
covered with a tracer layer. This is done to ensure the following machining processes does not damage the
actual composites, and to further control thickness of the parts. The laying-up process must be done in a
clean room, which are free of any contaminants that might damage the composite, and have a controlled
temperature and pressure.
A single ply, or a lay-up in which all of the layers are stacked in the same orientation, is referred to as
a lamina, whereas when the plies are stacked at various orientations with respect to each other, it is called a
laminate. The latter is also referred to as a multi-directional laminate. Unidirectional plies orientated in the
0◦are extremely strong and stiﬀin that direction, however are weak in the 90◦orientation, as such stacking
the plies at various orientations increases the strength and stiﬀness of the laminate. The third option is a
woven fabric, which is produced by the interlacing of warp (0◦) ﬁbres and weft (90◦) ﬁbres in a regular pattern
or weave style, where each ply can then be stacked at diﬀerent orientations. Furthermore, a variety of weaves
exists such as plain, twill and crowsfoot, all illustrated in Figure 3.1.3 along with UD and multi-directional
laminates (Campbell, 2010).
Figure 3.1.3: (a) Uni-directional (Campbell, 2010) (b) multi-directional (c) Woven (Administration, 2016)
As indicated in Figure 3.1.3, each ply is labelled according to its ply orientation, and is based on a global axis,
as shown in Figure 3.1.4 for a multi-directional laminate. The x-axis is parallel with the 0◦orientated plies,
the y-axis is perpendicular to the ﬁbres, thus at 90◦and the z-axis is normal to the plane of the laminate. The
stacking sequence is enclosed in square brackets, where the distinct layers or groups of layers are separated
by a slash (on Technology Enhanced Learning (NPTEL), 2018).
10
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 3
3.1. Manufacturing of Composite Parts
Figure 3.1.4: (a) Stacking sequence (Engineering, 2017)(b) Orientation of ﬁbres according to a global axis
(Maio, 2017)
For woven laminates, or fabrics, the warp direction is used to indicate the ply angle and the orientation of
the warp and weft direction is given inside the square brackets, as shown in Figure 3.1.5. Normal procedure
is to separate the two orientations with a slash, thus in the case of a weave two orientations are often given;
one for the warp direction and one for weft.
Figure 3.1.5: Orientation of woven fabric according to warp direction
In some cases the laminates can also contain a honeycomb core, which is added to increase strength and
stiﬀness. At Terma a special type called a SynCore is sometimes employed, and this assembly can be seen as
a type of laminated composite. The outer layers consist of CFRP composite material, which is strong, thin
and hard, where the core is softer, lighter and weaker. By combining these two, a stronger, stiﬀer and more
lightweight structure can be achieved in some cases. Furthermore, the addition of a core also reduces the cost
of the laminate (Henkel, 2017).
3.1.3.2
Composite Moulding
The ﬁnal step in the manufacturing of composite part is moulding, and various methods are available, however
in the aerospace industry autoclaving is the preferred method. Once, the pre-preg material have been placed
onto the mould in the correct stacking sequence as described in Section 3.1.3.1, the plies are covered with a
release ﬁlm, breather ﬁlm and ﬁnally a vacuum bag. The latter is used to draw out all the air, where after the
entire mould is placed in the autoclave for curing. The removal of air is referred to as demoulding. The part
is placed under continuous vacuum during curing to extract trapped gasses from the laminate. To ensure all
11
==PAGE:32==
3.2. Introduction to Machining of Composite Parts
Chapter 3
trapped air has been removed between the individual plies, the laminate is covered with a vacuum bag after
3-4 ply, removing all excess air, following a cycle of demoulding(Masuelli, 2013).
One of the disadvantage of using pre-impregnated material, is that it has to be kept in the freezer to
stop the resin from expiring. Furthermore, the process of autoclaving is labour intensive as it involves manu-
ally laying up the individual sheets of material, bagging the tool, and performing several demoulding cycles
to remove excess air. The lay-up sequence for autoclaving can be seen in Figure 3.1.6 (Barbero, 2010).
Figure 3.1.6: Lay-up sequence for autoclaving (Barbero, 2010)
Once the component is out of the autoclave and completely cured, it is transferred to a testing suit, where
non-destructive testing is carried out. Following this, the component enters the CNC machining center,
where milling and drilling are done. Next, a coordinate measuring machine (CMM) is used to measure all
relevant physical geometrics of the component, which is done on all the components, thus a 100% inspection
is conducted at Terma. Lastly, the component enters the paint shop.
3.2
Introduction to Machining of Composite Parts
In this section a basic introduction will be given to orthogonal cutting of composite parts as this forms
the basis of all machining operations. As such, an understanding of material removal must be reached in
order to further investigate milling and countersinking, which will be done in two separate, subsequent sections.
The manufacturing process described in Section 3.1 produces a composite part to near net-shape, how-
ever machining is still needed to bring the component to its ﬁnal dimensions. At Terma, the main machining
operations are milling, such as edge trimming and slotting, along with drilling, both completed using a CNC
machine. Further machining, such as countersinking of the drilled holes, is done manually. The procedure is
complicated by the fact that CFRP composites are heterogeneous materials with anisotropic properties. As
such, the material behaviour during machining is inhomogeneous and depends on ﬁbre and matrix properties,
the ﬁbre orientation and the relative volume of the matrix and ﬁbres (Namburi, 2017). During machining,
the tool will encounter both the softer matrix and the harder reinforcement, which has a diﬀerent response
to the cutting tool. Studies have found that the machining of composites is highly dependent on the chips
generated during cutting and the direction of the ﬁbres with respect to the machining direction (Namburi,
2017).
Koplev et al. (1983) did one of the ﬁrst studies on composite machining, conducting a series of tests to
study the surface after machining and the generated chips. The experiments revealed that the ﬁbre orienta-
tion was a signiﬁcant factor when assessing surface quality. The smoothest surface was obtained when the
ﬁbres were parallel to the cutting direction, whereas ﬁbres perpendicular to the cutting direction obtained
12
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 3
3.2. Introduction to Machining of Composite Parts
a rougher surface. The chip formation also varied depending on the ﬁbre orientation with respect to the
cutting direction. When machining perpendicular to the ﬁbre orientation, two distinct eﬀects were observed
near the tool tip. As the tool moved forward, it exerted a pressure, which caused fracture and created a chip.
Concurrently, there was a downward pressure on the composite below the tool, which lead to the production
of cracks. A diﬀerent observation was made when machining parallel to the ﬁbres. Although, the tool also
exerted a pressure on the material and creating chips, a crack was also observed in front of the tool, thus
indicating that this crack ran further than the current chip. The study also found that surface roughness was
more pronounced at high cutting speeds and lower feed rates, which could be due to the build-up of heat as
a result of poor thermal conductivity .
Another study on the formation of chips during orthogonal cutting of composites was conducted by Arola
et al. (1995a). Here, it was found that although the formation features of the chips varied greatly depending
on the direction of the ﬁbres, all were created from a series of brittle fractures and were discontinuous in
nature. The orientation of the ﬁbres in the each ply was found to be the main inﬂuence of chip formation, and
three distinct cutting mechanisms during the edge trimming were established, each related to a speciﬁc ﬁbre
direction. In the 0◦orientation, the chips formed through failure along the interface between the ﬁbre and
matrix, and cantilever bending and fracture perpendicular to the ﬁbre direction was observed. For positive
ﬁbre orientations between 15◦and 75◦, the chips formed due to compression induced shear perpendicular to
the ﬁbre axis and shearing between the ﬁbre-matrix interface along the ﬁbre direction during the chip forma-
tion. For ﬁbre orientations greater than 75◦the formation of chips was mainly due to compression-induced
fracture perpendicular to the ﬁbres and interlaminar shear fracture along the ﬁbre/matrix interface. In the
90◦direction the chips were formed through intra-laminar deformation as a result of compression loading,
and for negatively orientated ﬁbres it was due to shearing. The characteristics of chip formation due to ﬁbre
orientation is illustrated in Figure 3.2.1.
Figure 3.2.1: Cutting mechanisms in orthogonal cutting (Arola et al., 1995c)
Although, the mechanisms observed in Figure 3.2.1 are for uni-directional (UD) plies, the cutting mechanisms
for 0◦and 45◦ﬁbre orientations in multi-directional laminates were found to be identical to their UD
counterparts, as seen in a subsequent study by Arola et al. (1995b). The mechanisms for uni-directional chip
formation in 90◦and - 45◦plies were slightly diﬀerent to the ones observed in multi-directional laminates. In
the latter, less extensive damage occurred when machining the plies, which is due to the support of adjacent
layers. However, for ﬁbres in the -45◦direction more damage was observed through shear failure along the
ﬁbre/matrix interface, with fracture perpendicular to the ﬁbre direction in comparison to ﬁbres in the 90◦
direction.
13
==PAGE:34==
3.3. Slot Milling
Chapter 3
3.3
Slot Milling
The ﬁrst part of this section gives an introduction to the slot milling machining operation, and the subsequent
section focuses on the evaluation of surface quality after the procedure. This has been split into two parts,
namely delaminations and surface roughness, where each section follows a similar structure: How to classify
and measure the type of damage, and the eﬀect of machining parameters, tool wear, ﬁbre orientation along
with the composite structure.
A variety of milling operations are available, but the ones most frequently encountered at Terma are slotting,
and edge trimming, as illustrated in Figure 3.3.1. Currently, slot milling is done to remove any excess material
of a part in order to reach the correct dimensions. This is followed by an edge trimming procedure to obtain
the desired surface quality, as the former operation can result in surface damage in the form of delaminations
and protruding ﬁbres.
Figure 3.3.1: (a) Edge trimming (Can, 2017) and (b) Slot milling (Performance, 2017)
Edge trimming is generally used to achieve contour shape accuracy, where slot milling is used to generate
ﬁtting surfaces (Li et al., 2017). In this study, the focus is on slot milling, as this is the initial machining
operation the F-35 parts will encounter. Furthermore, a large portion of parts outside the F-35 production
will only encounter this process, as the addition of the edge trimming operation adds both time and cost to
the process, however are in some cases unavoidable. There are two methods of slot milling, namely climb and
conventional, also referred to as down and up milling, which depends on the motion of the tool with respect
to the feed direction. For conventional, the rotational motion of the tool is in the opposite direction to the
feed direction, whereas for climb the rotation of the tool is in the same direction as the feed. Figure 3.3.2
illustrates both methods.
Figure 3.3.2: Illustration of conventional and climb milling (Zeng et al., 2016)
In conventional milling the chip thickness will start from zero and then increase as the cutting continues,
whereas for climb the chips start from maximum size and then gradually decreases as milling ensues. The
14
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 3
3.3. Slot Milling
forces generated during conventional are low, but will cause the workpiece to be lifted from the surface, thus
a good ﬁxture is needed to secure the part. In climb, the engaging forces are high, and will press the part
down into the holding surface. Further, in conventional the chips are generated in front of the tool, which
can lead to an uneven surface, and re-cutting of the chips, whereas for climb the chips will fall behind the
cutter and leave a cleaner cutting surface (Miller, 2014). Based on this, climb appears to achieve a much
better surface quality, and it reduces the load from the cutting edge leading to a longer tool life. However,
conventional milling is lower impact during the initial cutting, where the chip thickness is zero, and is also
preferred when the depth of cut varies with more than 20 % (Miller, 2014). Furthermore, studies by Urban
(2001) and Rasmussen (2018), have indicated that although climb is often preferred for metals, the opposite
is true for composite materials. Here, conventional provides a much higher surface quality in terms of a lower
delamination depth and surface roughness. This could be due to the matrix and ﬁbre accumulating inside
the ﬂute of the end mill during climb, which obstructs the cutting and leads to surface damage. Conventional
milling is the recommendation for any composites containing carbon ﬁbres, as stated by Richardson (2011) in
an article on cutters for composites, which is due to less delaminations being generated with this milling op-
eration. However, a solid and appropriate ﬁxture is essential in ensuring the quality is high (Engineering, 2010).
There are two ﬁbre direction angles involved in the milling of CFRP composites, namely the ﬁbre ori-
entation angle (φ), and the ﬁbre cutting angle (θ), which are illustrated in Figures 3.3.3(a) and 3.3.3(b),
respectively. The former is measured between the feed direction and the ﬁbre orientation, whereas the ﬁbre
cutting angle will change continuously during milling, due to the rotary motion of the tool, as shown in
3.3.3(c). The variation of the two angles has an enormous eﬀect on the surface quality, which is due to
diﬀerent ﬁbre orientations resulting in a varying surface roughness (Hintze et al., 2011).
Figure 3.3.3: (a) Orientation angle, φ, (b) Cutting angle, θ, (c)Change of cutting angle during milling Hintze
et al. (2011)
Slot milling can be prone to stress concentration and top layer delamination, which results in severe mechanical
failures. Therefore, the process parameters must be chosen properly to avoid any unnecessary damage,
excessive cutting force and tool wear (Hintze and Brugmann, 2018). As such, a discussion of the inﬂuence of
15
==PAGE:36==
3.3. Slot Milling
Chapter 3
tool geometry and wear, along with machining parameters and the eﬀect of stacking sequence is essential in
understanding how to achieve a high surface quality and is the focus in the next sections.
3.3.1
Surface Quality
The abrasive and inhomogeneous nature of the composite part introduces surface damage during machining
operations. As the machined surface quality determines the reliability, wear resistance and service life of
the parts, ensuring the quality of the ﬁnal part is essential. The two main aspects of surface quality are
delaminations and the surface roughness. Although, similar types of machining conditions are needed to
obtain good surface quality in terms of reduced delaminations and roughness, a section has been done for
each type. This is to illustrate the diﬀerent methods by which they are identiﬁed and how both play a role
in contributing to the overall surface quality of the ﬁnished part. There is some overlap in terms of the
best machining practise, which should hopefully help to emphasise the important of selecting the correct
parameters.
3.3.1.1
Delamination
Delamination in the top layers of composite parts occur due to the cutting action of the milling tool, as
shown in a study by Hintze et al. (2011). Here, it was found that when the tool was unable to cut the
ﬁbres, this would cause them to bend outwards, deﬂecting away from the plane of the laminate, and lead
to severe delaminations. Furthermore, the protruding bundles of ﬁbres resulted in much deeper top layer
delaminations in comparison to separate ﬁbres protruding from the edge. If the protruding ﬁbre is long, it
can easily intertwine with the cutter tooth, resulting in the ﬁbre being ripped from the surface layer causing
a deep delamination. This observation was conﬁrmed in a study by Liu et al. (2017) who investigated the
surface quality of CFRP composites when using a staggered end mill. The investigation also revealed that the
tear direction was generally along the surface ﬁbre direction. Colligan and Ramulu (1992) undertook a study
investigating edge trimming of unidirectional CFRP composites with various ﬁbre orientations to identify
the diﬀerent delamination types. Three types of delaminations were established as illustrated in Figure 3.3.4.
Figure 3.3.4: The three diﬀerent types of delaminations as identiﬁed by Colligan and Ramulu (1992)
Type I is identiﬁed as where the ﬁbres have broken inwards away from the trimmed edge leaving an exposed
area where the surface ply is missing. This is also referred to as ﬁbre pull-out, as the ﬁbres are being pulled
16
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 3
3.3. Slot Milling
from the machined edge leaving a gap. Type II delaminations consist of uncut ﬁbres, which protrude from the
edge, and can be separated from the below ply some distance into the material. This type of delamination is
often found with the uncut ﬁbres attached to the matrix, as reported by Li et al. (2017). Type III is where
loose ﬁbres are partially attached to the edge, causing a ”fuzzy” appearance. Often a combination of Type I
and Type II is encountered in which the ﬁbres or ﬁbre bundles protrude from the edge accompanied by damage
of the surface, as seen in the middle illustration on Figure 3.3.4. In a study by Hintze et al. (2011), where
the occurrence and propagation of delamination during the machining of CFRPs were investigated, Type I
delaminations were found to occur during machining as a result of poor support of the top laminate layer and
lead to severe damage. Contrastingly, Type II delaminations, characterized by ﬁbres that protrude beyond
the trimmed edge, were found not to result in perceptible damage to the surface. While Type II delaminations
can easily rectiﬁed, correcting Type I break-outs is critical, as reported by K¨anig and Graß (1989). The
experimental investigation by Hintze et al. (2011) also revealed the existence of two delamination eﬀects
when milling composite parts with diﬀerent ﬁbre orientations: Generation of delamination and propagation
of delamination, as presented in Figure 3.3.5. Generation of delamination occurs in the ﬁbre cutting angle
range of 0◦< φ <90◦of the edge, where the ﬁbres avoid the cutting tool, which leads to delaminations. The
propagation is when a certain ﬁbre has initially been delaminated, and propagates with progressing feed rate.
Based on this, further studies were conducted by Hintze and Brugmann (2018) to investigate the relation
between minimum delamination depth, ﬁbre orientation and the occurrence of uncut ﬁbres. Here, it was
shown that protruding ﬁbres can be used to identify delaminations, as these are always found together. So,
although the delamination can be hard to identify as the protruding ﬁbres might mask the separation from
the ply below, this study clearly illustrates that the ﬁbres in themselves are enough to indicate delamination.
Figure 3.3.5: Generation and propagation of delamination according to ﬁbre orientation (Hintze et al., 2011)
Tool Wear
Tool wear is a signiﬁcant factor in terms of delaminations and can result in severe surface damage if a dull
end mill is used during machining. This is due to wear leading to a rounder cutting edge resulting in a larger
radius, thus a higher generation of damage along the milled edge. Furthermore, the bigger cutting edge radius
also results in higher machining forces, thus deﬁned cutting of the ﬁbres becomes more diﬃcult as found
in a study by Hintze et al. (2011). The inﬂuence of tool wear on delaminations during milling had been
observed in previous studies by Hohensee (1992) and Rummenh¨oller (1996), who investigated the machining
of ﬁbre reinforced plastics by milling and laser cutting. Here, tool wear was identiﬁed as a decisive factor, and
ﬁbres in the top layer were revealed to be more prone to delaminations. Ferreira et al. (1999) investigated
the correlation between an increase in tool wear and cutting forces. It was demonstrated that the feed force
increased signiﬁcantly with increasing wear, which was found by Karpat et al. (2012) to be accompanied by
a greater degree of delamination.
Tool Geometry
The geometry of the tool is another decisive factor in terms of surface damage. Davim and Reis (2005)
investigated the eﬀect of tool geometry on delamination of CFRP composites. Here, a two-ﬂute end mill was
17
==PAGE:38==
3.3. Slot Milling
Chapter 3
compared to a six-ﬂute tool using a variety of spindle speed and feed rate combinations. It was found that the
two-ﬂute end mill produced less damage on the material than the six-ﬂute mill. Furthermore, delaminations
were shown to increase slightly with feed rate for both end mills. For the six-ﬂute mill an increase in cutting
velocity also lead to a higher delamination frequency. This is in contrast to an experiment conducted by
Kili¸ckap et al. (2015), where the eﬀect of tool geometry and machining parameters were investigated during
milling of CFRPs using a three-ﬂute and four-ﬂute end mill. Here, the delamination factor were found to
decrease with increasing number of ﬂutes. The diﬀerent tools were also shown to cause separate types of
delamination, where the three-ﬂute end mill produced Type III delaminations, whereas Type I-II/II uncut
ﬁbres were seen for the four-ﬂute mill. As the latter type of delaminations are easier to rectify, the best
results were obtained for the four-end mill. In a study by Kalla et al. (2010) it was found that a tool with a
helix angle diﬀerent from 0◦produces an axial force perpendicular to the surface being trimmed, and leads
to a higher frequency of delaminations in the form of protruding ﬁbres. A similar observation was made
by Hagino and Inoue (2013), where the eﬀect on ﬁbre orientation and helix angle when end milling CFRP
composites were studied. Three diﬀerent types of end mills were utilized with helix angles of 0◦, 30◦and 45◦,
respectively. The former was found to give the best results in terms of surface delaminations irrespective
of the ﬁbre orientation. A Duboust et al. (2016) investigated three diﬀerent milling tools, namely two PCV
three-ﬂute tools with diﬀerent diameters and a 12-ﬂute CVD burr-tool, during wet and dry milling of CFRPs.
The number of ﬂutes played a signiﬁcant role in the amount of surface damage, where the tool with the high-
est number of ﬂutes produced the best surface quality, which was also due to the burred surface of the end mill.
Machining Parameters
The delamination frequency is likewise inﬂuenced by machining parameters, such as feed rate, feed per tooth
and cutting speed. Delaminations tend to increase with increasing feed rate and decreasing speed, as found
by Sheikh-Ahmad et al. (2012). In a study by Colligan and Ramulu (1992) delamination in surface plies of
graphite/epoxy composites caused by the edge trimming process were investigated. The surface roughness
and delamination frequency was seen to be negatively aﬀected by increasing feed per tooth. This was con-
ﬁrmed in a slot-milling experiment conducted by Davim and Reis (2005). Here, the feed rate was found to
be the main factor determining the delamination frequency, where an increase resulted in a higher rate of
surface damage. The spindle speed has also been found to inﬂuence the amount of delaminations as found
by Pecat et al. (2012). In this study, the inﬂuence of milling process parameters on the surface integrity
of CFRP were studied, where an increase in speed lead to visible ﬁbre bending close to the surface, thus
Type II delaminations. The explanation given was that a high spindle speed worsen the damage factor and
increased the plastic deformation rate. The same observations were made by Kili¸ckap et al. (2015). Here, an
experimental investigation of end milling CFRP parts were undertaken, and the delamination factor was seen
to increase with increasing feed rate, but also with cutting speed. In a study on the inﬂuence of machining
parameters and ﬁbre cutting angles during milling of CFRP parts by Liu et al. (2017), the speed was also
found to be signiﬁcant. An increase in cutting speed was found to result in a higher amount of delaminations.
This was explained as being due an increase in friction between the tool and chip resulting in a raise of cutting
temperatures. This leads to matrix smearing, debonding of the matrix and ﬁbres, leading to delaminations.
The experiments also revealed that an increase in the feed per tooth resulted in a higher amount of ﬁbres
being cut, which lead to more machining defects. As such, a higher number of ﬂutes will lead to a higher
surface quality, because the total feed rate will be divided among a higher amount of cutting edges leading
to a lower feed per tooth. Khairusshima et al. (2017) concluded that a lower feed rate leads to a more stable
machining process, resulting in less delaminations as found during an investigation on machining of CFRPs.
Caggiano (2018) reviewed the machining operations available for ﬁbre reinforced polymer composites, and
found that the decrease in feed rate leading to less surface damage, was due to smaller chips per tooth being
generated.Thus, lower cutting forces and less impact on the cutting edge.
Composite Structure
The type and amount of delamination also depends on the structure of the composite, thus a diﬀerence can
be detected between a weave and a multi-directional laminate. In a study investigating the inﬂuence of a
woven composite structure on delaminations by Hintze et al. (2015) it was found that the undulation of the
woven yarn, which results in a varying thickness of the top resin layer, leads to delamination of diﬀerent types
and magnitudes. Damage was found to occur at neutral or negative undulation angles, φz, where the weaved
18
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 3
3.3. Slot Milling
structure along with the angle is illustrated in Figure 3.3.6. At positive undulation angles the increasingly
supportive eﬀect from the top resin layer prevents further crack propagation, which can be seen in the form
of a higher amount of the matrix being present above the ﬁll yarn, shown in Figure 3.3.6. Several experiments
were conducted using a variety of tool diameters and helix angles and the same conclusion was reached. Based
on this, it was concluded that the eﬀect of geometry was less signiﬁcant than the lay-up of the laminate.
These ﬁndings were conﬁrmed by Li et al. (2017), where the machined edges of woven laminates were found
to be very particular with ﬁbre pull-out, uniform burrs, attached ﬁbres and resin along with surface damage.
Here, it was found that the length of the protruding ﬁbres increased with increasing feed rate at a constant
speed. It decreased with increasing speed when the feed rate was ﬁxed. The reason given was that an increase
in speed leads to a higher rate of plastic deformation, which results in higher cutting forces. Thus, the ﬁbres
were forced to fracture, lowering the length of the uncut ﬁbres. Contrastingly, a rise in feed rate means the
material removal per tooth will also increase, making it easier for ﬁbre bundles to avoid the cutting tool,
leaving a large amount of uncut ﬁbers on the edge.
Figure 3.3.6: Structure of a plain weave with the undulation angle,φz, illustrated on the right Hintze et al.
(2015)
Delaminations can be diﬃcult to quantify, but various methods have been presented by diﬀerent authors.
Colligan and Ramulu (1992) used the delamination width along with the frequency at which they occurred to
evaluate the eﬀects of ﬁbre orientation, tool geometry and cutting conditions. Here, the width was measured
using an optical microscope, and a minimum delamination width of 1mm was adopted, although the threshold
value for the aerospace industry is 2.5mm. In a study by Davim and Reis (2005) the eﬀect of cutting parameters
on observed delaminations during slot milling was investigated. Here, the delamination width was also used
as an indication of damage, as illustrated in Figure 3.3.4, and based on this, the delamination factor, Fd,
was found by dividing the maximum width of the slot by the nominal width. This method was found to
be appropriate when Type I delaminations was identiﬁed as these leads to a larger width. Although, a
combination of Type I/II could also make use of this method, it might exclude the damaging eﬀect of
protruding ﬁbres, which would not be taken into account using this method. In the same study mentioned
previously by Li et al. (2017), who investigated the occurrence of burrs during slot milling of multi-directional
and plain woven CFRP, the length of the protruding ﬁbres were measured using an optical microscope. In
every instance a visual inspection should be made, which is often more giving then trying to quantify the
delaminations, as stated by Can (2017).
3.3.1.2
Surface Roughness
The components of surface texture are waviness, roughness, form and lay. Waviness stems from the individual
machine, an imbalance in the grinding wheel, thus may result from vibrations, chatter or work deﬂections
19
==PAGE:40==
3.3. Slot Milling
Chapter 3
and strains in the material. Form is the general shape of the surface, and will be a straight line for a ﬂat
surface or an arc for a circular component, where form errors are often due to the part not being held ﬁrmly
resulting in sliding. Lay is the directional characteristic of a machine part. This leaves the roughness, when
analysing the surface quality. Roughness only derives from the manufacturing process, thus is a result of
the machining rather than the machine as in the case of waviness. These characteristics are never found in
isolation and all surfaces are a combination of all elements, however an analyse of each one needs to be done
separately, as discussed subsequently (Dagnall, 2011).
Roughness measurements are often done by drawing a stylus across the machined surface at a constant
speed for a set distance. The measured proﬁle is a result of scanning the actual surface of the sample with
the probe, mechanically ﬁltering the proﬁle due to the tip and, in some cases, the skid, which can be placed
in front, behind or next to the stylus, with the set-up illustrated in Figure 3.3.7. From this an electrical signal
is obtained and can be plotted on a graph(Schuetz, 2012).
Figure 3.3.7: Roughness measurement equipment with and without a skid (Magazine, 2004)
The roughness proﬁle is extracted from the primary proﬁle, where the latter is found by removing the nominal
form, which is the intended form of the component, from the measured proﬁle using a best-ﬁt least-square
method. The roughness proﬁle can be obtained by applying certain ﬁlters, which suppresses long-wave, λc, and
short-wave λs, components. λs was introduced to address the diﬀerences between the measuring instruments
being used, mainly referring to the radius of the stylus. A 5 µm stylus sampling every 1 µm will have less
wavelength information than one with a 2 µm stylus sampling every 0.25 µm. The guidelines recommend
λs to be ﬁve times the sampling length. The long wavelength component, λc, is used to determine which
wavelengths belong to roughness and which to waviness. By selecting the correct value the waviness of the
proﬁle can be suppressed, resulting in the actual roughness of the sample. The sampling length over which
the measurements are done is equal to the cut-oﬀwavelength. General practise is to have the total evaluation
length as ﬁve times λc. The ISO 4287:1998 (1998) provides solid guidelines on how to best select the correct
cut-oﬀ, based on the stylus used and the surface to be measured Dagnall (2011). When using a skidded stylus,
this will act as a mechanical ﬁlter, taking out much of the general form of the component, and be seen as a
coarse ﬁltering Liltorp (2018). As such, instruments using skids can be used for measuring roughness parame-
ters only, as stated in ISO 3274:1996 (1996), which also includes a section with regards to skidded instruments,
however provides the same recommendations in terms of cut-oﬀﬁlters as ISO 4287:1998 (1998). Thus, in
order to obtain the roughness proﬁle, regardless of the measuring instrument used, it is necessary to use a
λc proﬁle ﬁlter to get the roughness proﬁle. Furthermore, if a skid is employed, its radius in the direction of
the trace should be no less than 50 times the nominal cut-oﬀwavelength used. (Kuzinovski and Tomov, 2003).
In order to quantify the surface roughness and compare various machining conditions, roughness param-
eters are used. The most common ones are Ra, Rt and R10z, which describes the are arithmetic average
20
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 3
3.3. Slot Milling
height (Ra), peak to valley height (Rt) and ten-point average height (Rz), respectively. These values are
calculated based on ISO 4287:1998 (1998) (Dagnall, 2011). However, the roughness parameters described
in ISO ISO 4287:1998 (1998), were built for homogeneous materials, such as metals, whereas composites
are heterogeneous. Furthermore, studies have found that the Ra roughness parameter does not capture the
roughness suﬃciently while other parameters such as R10z gives a better indication of the machined surface
Urban (2001). R10z is the average distance between the ﬁve highest peaks and ﬁve lowest valleys within each
cut-oﬀlength. As such, this will take into account the more extreme features of the surface, which is why
several studies have found that in order to adequately describe the surface roughness of a composite material,
the parameter R10z should be employed. Ra averages all peaks and valleys of the roughness proﬁle, and
neutralises extreme features such as deep valleys, resulting in these having no signiﬁcant impact on the ﬁnal
results, thus will not give an accurate representation of the roughness (Free, 2015). However, as industrial
requirements are still often based on Ra values this can be used to ensure the measurements are below the
acceptable limit of 3.2 µm as set by the aerospace industry (Li et al., 2017). The roughness parameters are
illustrated in Figure 3.3.8
Figure 3.3.8: Illustration of the roughness parameter Ra and R10z (Gadelmawla et al., 2002)
Another issue in terms of surface roughness measurements of composites being based on conventional materials
is the ﬁlter used for the long wavelength component, λc. The ﬁlter itself is a Gaussian ﬁlter, as it produces
a good ﬁt with low distortion. However, in a study by Rimpault (2016) it was shown that using a linear
Gaussian ﬁlter on composite materials can lead to various artefacts, such as artiﬁcial peaks, edge distortion and
misﬁltered proﬁles. The main issue though, stems from the diﬀerent ﬁbre orientations, where some produces
very sharp peaks or valleys, such as the -45◦plies. Instead, it has been proposed that ISO 13565-1:1996 (1998)
is used, which is particularly useful for honing surfaces, where the proﬁles consist of deep valleys. Even if
those deep valleys are random, they are numerous and narrower. Furthermore, the roughness parameters
generated from such proﬁles are less impacted by the measurement position. The ISO 13565-1:1996 (1998) is
particularly useful for measuring transverse directions of multidirectional laminates as these often contain a
range of ﬁbre orientations including the 45◦plies.
Transverse and Longitudinal Measurements
The surface topography can be done in two directions, namely transverse and longitudinal, and is an important
aspect to consider. Conventionally, these have been done across the feed direction, thus transverse, in order to
assess the largest roughness variations. However, this is for homogeneous materials such as metals, and several
studies have shown that this procedure cannot be directly transferred to composites (Urban, 2001), (Sheikh-
Ahmad et al., 2012) and Gara and Tsoumarev (2016b). Instead the longitudinal direction has been employed,
21
==PAGE:42==
3.3. Slot Milling
Chapter 3
where the two measurement directions are exempliﬁed in Figure 3.3.9. Here, the longitudinal measurements
are highlighted for the 0◦orientated ply, and the transverse direction can be seen to go through several
diﬀerent orientated plies.
Figure 3.3.9: Longitudinal and transverse measurement direction for roughness evaluation of CFRPs (Duboust
et al., 2017)
Due to measurements in the transverse direction being done across several plies, the roughness measurements
are often found to be higher in this direction, as illustrated in Figure 3.3.10. The proﬁles are taken from a
study by Duboust et al. (2017) who investigated the surface roughness of CFRP composites using an optical
method. Here, the eﬀect of certain ﬁbre orientations having a higher roughness is exempliﬁed, which is seen
in the form of peaks and valleys for the transverse direction. However, the measurements made in the 0◦
also shows peaks and valleys being present, indicating that this is unavoidable when measuring roughness on
composite materials.
Figure 3.3.10: Roughness proﬁles for the longitudinal and transverse direction, left and right, respectively
(Duboust et al., 2017)
Early studies indicated that the transverse direction did not contain any information with regards to cutting
parameters. In a study by Ramulu et al. (1993), it was found that for multi-directional laminates the surface
was periodic and non-Gaussian in the transverse direction, but non-periodic and Gaussian in the longitudinal
direction. At ﬁbre orientations of 0◦and 45◦the surface roughness in the longitudinal direction was found
to be at its lowest, whereas the highest was found for -45◦plies revealed as deep valleys on the proﬁle, and
in the 90◦direction seen as peaks. In the transverse direction the surface roughness did not seem to depend
on the ﬁbre orientation with respect to the cutting direction. Furthermore, it was found that in order to
adequately describe the surface roughness of a composite material, the parameter would have to include
three characteristics: First, the parameter must quantify the maximal roughness heights, which will describe
the depth of the ﬁbre pull-out region and the thickness of matrix smearing. Second, the strata distributions
of the surface roughness proﬁles have to be determined. Third, the periodic irregularities seen in most of
the machined surfaces must also be described to determine the spacing between the deep valleys caused by
ﬁbre pull-out. No such parameter exists, but instead a combination of R10z and Ra was suggested. These
22
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 3
3.3. Slot Milling
observations were conﬁrmed in a study by Gara and Tsoumarev (2016a), where the surface roughness during
slot milling of a woven laminate was investigate. It was found that the transverse roughness did not depend on
cutting conditions, but on tool geometry. For the longitudinal direction, the roughness was found to depend
on tool geometry and cutting conditions, and to be much lower for up milling in comparison to down milling.
This is in contrast to experiments by El-Hofy et al. (2011), where the factors eﬀecting surface quality in
slot milling was investigated. Here, the transverse direction was used, which yielded good results, as it gave
an indication of the overall roughness. Further studies by El-Hofy et al. (2018) revealed similar roughness
tendencies in the transverse and longitudinal direction when investigating the eﬀect of tool temperature in
slotting of CFRP composites. This is in contrast to other ﬁndings, where the transverse direction was shown
to have a much higher roughness, due to the diﬀerent ﬁbre orientations contributing in distinct patterns to
the overall surface quality. El-Hofy et al. (2018) found this to be because the longitudinal direction were
more aﬀected by striations left by the tool, while in transverse direction the proﬁle was aﬀected by ply
orientation. In an investigation by Boudelier et al. (2011), where process parameters during machining of
CFRP composites were optimised, the roughness was also measured in the transverse direction. This was
found to be more representative of the overall roughness of the part due to measuring across several plies,
thus obtaining higher roughness values. This is due to the ﬁbre orientation responding diﬀerently during
machining leading to a range of surface values. In an investigation on the eﬀect of edge trimming CFRP
composites samples by Can (2017), the transverse direction was found to give a good indication of the best
machining parameters. In this study, an ANOVA analysis was conducted to ﬁnd the best combination of
spindle speed and feed rate, which yielded similar results in terms of achieving optimum surface roughness
for the transverse and longitudinal direction. It was found that in order to achieve the minimum arithmetic
average roughness the same machining conditions were found to be ideal for the longitudinal and transverse
direction.
Machining Parameters
Ensuring the correct machining parameters are employed is essential in avoiding a high surface roughness.
Puw and Hocheng (1993) investigated the eﬀect of ﬁbre orientation angles on the cutting force value and
surface quality during machining of CFRP composite. Here, it was found that cutting speed had a major
inﬂuence on the surface roughness, where a higher speed lead to better surface quality. This is in contrast
to the experiments conducted by Davim and Reis (2005), where the cutting speed was found to be less
signiﬁcant.The feed rate was, however, had a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the surface roughness, where an increase
in feed rate lead to higher surface roughness values. In a study by Gara and Tsoumarev (2016a), where the
eﬀect of tool geometry on surface roughness of CFRP composites were investigated, it was found that an
increase in feed rate at a constant spindle speed lead to a higher surface roughness. This was also the case
for a raise in spindle speed. This observation was also made by Li et al. (2017), who investigated slot milling
of multi-directional and woven laminates, and found with an increase of cutting speed and feed rate, more
smearing of the matrix and cavities were observed on the machined surface. A higher cutting speed leads
to more friction between the tool and chip resulting in higher cutting forces, thus leading to more surface
damage. The deterioration in surface quality at higher feed rates could be due to the increased strain rate on
the material promoting fractures of the ﬁbres and matrix. In a study by Anil et al. (2017) an investigation
on the eﬀect of spindle speed on the surface quality of CFRP composites were done. Here, low cutting speeds
were found to fracture chips more readily, which lead to a rougher surface, and adhesive wear of the tool. As
the speed increased, the machining time was reduced, and the amount of chip fracture decreased resulting
in a better surface quality. The studies all reached the conclusion that the roughness value increases with
a raise in feed rate. This is due to a lower feed rate generating smaller chips per tooth, resulting in lower
cutting forces and reducing the impact on the cutting edge (Caggiano, 2018). Increasing the feed rate will
therefore cause the average chip thickness to increase, and consequently the forces required for chip removal.
This has been shown by Koplev et al. (1983) to lead to a higher surface roughness, and is exempliﬁed in
Equation 3.3.1 (Urban, 2001).
aeﬀ= ae
Vf
Vc
(3.3.1)
where aeﬀis the eﬀective chip thickness, ae is radial depth of cut, Vf is the feed rate and Vc = π·d·N is the
cutting speed. Here, d is the diameter of the cutting tool and N is the spindle speed (RPM). Equation 3.3.1
illustrates that if the speed is kept constant, but the feed rate is increased, the eﬀective chip thickness will
23
==PAGE:44==
3.3. Slot Milling
Chapter 3
increase, resulting in higher cutting forces, and lower surface roughness. Although, an increase in speed will
generate a higher amount of heat, it will also produce a lower chip thickness at a ﬁxed feed rate.
However, a few studies have proved that a higher feed rate can lead to better surface quality as found
by El-Hofy et al. (2011), where factors aﬀecting the surface integrity during slot milling of CFRP laminates
were investigated. Here, statistical analysis showed that the combination of lower cutting speeds and higher
feed rates is preferable in terms of improving surface roughness, where feed rate was the signiﬁcant factor.
This was explained in terms of the observed temperatures during slot milling. Although, the cutting tem-
perature increased with increasing speed, this was not observed for an increase in feed rate. Here, a raise in
feed led to the cutting temperatures being reduced, which was attributed to the lower contact time between
the tool and machined surface. However, any further increase in feed rate lead to higher temperatures due
to tool wear becoming more prominent. This observation was conﬁrmed by Duboust et al. (2017), where
an the increase in feed rate during milling of CFRP composites were found to give a higher surface quality.
This was only until a certain feed rate, where any further increase lead to a higher surface roughness. The
observation was also made by Khairusshima et al. (2012) who studied tool wear and surface roughness when
milling CFRP composites using chilled air. Here, it was found that although higher spindle speeds and lower
feed rates lead to less tool wear, in order to obtain a better surface quality the machining should be done
at higher feed rates and cutting speeds. In a study by Vinayagamoorthy et al. (2016), where an analysis
of machining damage on CFRPs were studied, a medium-level feed rate and high spindle speed were found
to produce the best results. This was conﬁrmed by J. Delahaigue and Lebrun (2017), who examined the
inﬂuence of machining parameters on natural ﬁbre composites. Here, a mean increase in feed rate lead to
a higher surface roughness, however a higher surface quality was observed for the intermediate feed rate
and spindle speed. Singh et al. (2016) did an experimental study on the machining of CFRP composites,
and made the same observation with regards to the intermediate feed rate resulting in the best surface quality.
As seen by the literature review with regards to the inﬂuence of cutting parameters on surface rough-
ness contradicting observations have been made through several independent studies. The general agreement
is that an overall increase in feed rate leads to a higher surface roughness, which is due to larger chips being
generated, resulting in higher cutting forces, and a higher impact on the cutting edge. The increase in feed rate
