also generates heat between the tool and the work material, resulting in higher tool wear. This culminates in a
lower surface quality due to matrix smearing, cavities being formed along with protruding ﬁbres. In addition,
the increase in feed per tooth, causes a signiﬁcant rise in the feed force, which leads to friction and results
in a lower surface quality due to the heat generated (Khairusshima et al., 2012), (Can, 2017). Contrastingly,
a few studies have revealed that although an overall increase in feed rate leads to higher surface roughness,
the best results in terms of surface quality was observed at the intermediate stage. This can explained in
terms of the thermal damage encountered at high and low feed rates during machining of CFRP composites.
An initial raise in feed rate can lead to a reduction of cutting temperatures due to a less tool-surface contact
time, resulting in less ﬁbres escaping, thus a lower surface roughness. Raising the feed rate further results in
a lower surface quality mainly due to tool wear raising the cutting temperature, along with the production
of a large chip thickness El-Hofy et al. (2011), Duboust et al. (2016). More complicated contradictions stem
from results obtained on changing the spindle speed. Some found an increase in speed resulting in a better
surface quality where other studies revealed the exact opposite. Wang et al. (2015) found an explanation for
the opposing ﬁndings in an experimental investigation of the correlation between machining parameters and
surface roughness of CFRP composites. In this study, the speed was not to showing any clear trends and a
medium spindle speed were found to obtain the best results in terms of surface quality. A slower spindle speed
means the material removal of the cutting edge is large, and the milling temperature is low. This results in
less softening of the matrix material, making the cutting of the composite more diﬃcult resulting in a higher
surface roughness. When the spindle speed is higher, the generated temperatures during milling will lead
to excessive softening of the matrix, resulting in more protruding ﬁbres, thus a worse surface quality. It is
evident that in order to obtain a good surface quality the interaction between spindle speed and feed rate
must be considered. Various combinations will result in diﬀerent outcomes in terms of surface quality, which
is also dependent on the geometry of the tool. This was also the conclusion reached by Alhashemi (2013) in
an experiment where diﬀerent machining conditions were investigated for CFRP composites, and conﬁrmed
experimentally by M. Slamani et al. (2018) In the latter the inﬂuence of machining parameters on surface
24
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 3
3.3. Slot Milling
quality during high speed edge trimming of carbon ﬁber reinforced polymers was considered. Here, ANOVA
results of the preliminary tests show also that the interaction eﬀect of the cutting velocity and feed rate had
the highest contribution to the surface roughness.
Fibre Orientation
In a study by Hocheng et al. (1993), it was observed that no delaminations were formed at 0◦ﬁbre orientation
angle. At 45◦and 90◦shear and tensile mode type failure occurred of the carbon ﬁbres. El-Hofy et al. (2011)
considered the factors eﬀecting the surface quality during slot milling of CFRP composites. In this study,
low surface quality was observed in the form of uneven surfaces at 45◦orientated plies while those in the
90◦and 135◦direction suﬀered matrix cracking and ﬁbre pull-out due to high cutting forces and softening of
the resin. The best surface was found when ﬁbres were parallel to the cutting direction. Pecat et al. (2012)
presented a study in which slot milling was performed on uni-directional CFRP composites to investigate
the eﬀect of ply orientation on surface roughness. A smooth surface was observed for ply orientations at 0◦
and 45◦, but serious damage was observed at 90◦and 135◦. Chatelain et al. (2012) looked at the eﬀect of ply
orientation on the trimming process of multi-layered quasi-isotropic CFRP laminates. There was shown to
be a signiﬁcant correlation between the roughness proﬁle and the ply orientation, independent of the cutting
parameters, revealing the importance of ply orientation and stacking sequence during milling operations. The
highest surface roughness was found at 135◦, with similar and lower values being observed at 0◦and 45◦. In
an experiment by Palanikumar (2016), where the milling of polymer matrix composites were investigated, it
was observed that surface roughness value were low for ﬁbres orientated at an angle of 30◦or less. Higher
angles saw a rapidly increase in surface damage. Ghafarizadeh et al. (2016) looked at the eﬀect of milling
parameters and ﬁbre orientation angle on cutting temperatures, machining forces and surface roughness of
CFRP composite was investigated. This lead to the conclusion that to achieve the best surface ﬁnish a low
cutting speed should be utilised at ﬁbre orientation angles of 0◦and 90◦. This was conﬁrmed in an experiment
by Duboust et al. (2016) where an optical method for measuring the surface roughness of CFRP composites
were utilised. Here, ﬁbre orientation of 0◦, 45◦and 90◦presented the lowest surface roughness, where the
highest was found at 135◦. The higher surface quality in the 0◦and 45◦ply orientation, was also observed by
Gara and Tsoumarev (2016b) who studied the eﬀect of ﬁbre direction on surface roughness. In most cases,
the worst surface roughness can be observed in the 135◦ﬁbre orientation, and the best appear to be achieved
when machining ﬁbres in the 0◦direction. The contradicting observations made with regards to the other
ply directions could be due to the inﬂuence of other machining parameters such as the rake angle of the
tool, stacking sequence and general tool geometry. Concerning a woven composite structure, a study was
done by Li et al. (2017), where the surface integrity of a woven and multi-direction CFRP laminate was
compared after slot milling. Less surface damage was found on the plain woven laminate in comparison to
diﬀerent types of multi-directional composites. However, inﬂuential factors such as spindle speed, feed rate,
tool geometry and wear also has an impact on the observed results.
Tool Geometry
The geometry of the end mill tool and its eﬀect on surface roughness was investigated by Davim and Reis
(2005) where a higher surface roughness was found for the six-ﬂute end mill tool with a neutral helix angle
in comparison to a two-ﬂute mill with a helix angle of 30◦. This observation was conﬁrmed by Caggiano
(2018) during an investigation machining CFRP laminates, where tools with varying helix angles were studied.
To reduce tool wear and improve machined surface integrity, inclination milling with high helix angle end
mills, in which the resultant cutting force is parallel to the CFRP surface, was proposed. However, in a
study by Hagino and Inoue (2013), who investigated the eﬀect of the helix angle on cutting characteristics
during milling of CFRP laminates, it was found that an end mill tool with 0◦helix angle presented the best
results in terms of surface roughness, independent of the ﬁbre orientation. Jenarthanan and Jeyapaul (2015)
studied the optimal tooling and machining conditions for low surface roughness and found that a low ﬁbre
orientation angle, low helix angle with a medium spindle speed and low feed rate were optimum parameters.
In a theoretical and experimental study of slotting CFRP material with a segmented helix tool by Gara and
Tsoumarev (2016b), the segmentation of the tool was found to provide a higher surface quality in comparison
to a standard burr tool. Furthermore, the serration on the ﬂutes lead to less damage on the resin and ﬁbres.
Kili¸ckap et al. (2015) conducted an experimental investigation on the inﬂuence of cutting parameters and
tool parameters on surface roughness. This revealed that the Ra roughness values decreased when a higher
25
==PAGE:46==
3.3. Slot Milling
Chapter 3
number of ﬂutes on the cutting tool was utilized, which conﬁrmed the studies by Erkan et al. (2013). Here, a
mathematical mode was done for predicting the damage factor in end milling of composites, and the surface
roughness was found to decrease with increasing number of ﬂutes. This was attributed to a decreasing feed
per tooth due to the participation of more cutting edges in a revolution. This was contradicted by Duboust
et al. (2017) during a study on the surface roughness of machined carbon composites, where a comparison
was made between a burr tool with twelve ﬂutes and a four-ﬂute helical end mill. Here, the twelve-ﬂute tool
was found to increase the surface roughness, leading to the conclusion that a higher number of ﬂutes could
lower the surface quality.
Tool Wear
Tool wear can seriously aﬀect the surface quality of a machined part, which is inﬂuenced by several factors.
The wear of the active cutting edge of the tool, will lead to changes in the geometry, thus aﬀecting tool perfor-
mance. The increase in radius of the tool results in larger cutting forces, which generates higher temperatures
during machining, culminating in matrix burning, protruding ﬁbres and cavities along with pits being formed
(Can, 2017). Furthermore, a reduction in a sharp tool edge, means the tool is more likely to slipping over the
ﬁbres rather than cutting them, resulting in a higher surface roughness. These observations were conﬁrmed
in a study by Sheikh-Ahmad et al. (2017) where a new tool life criterion for delamination free milling was
considered. Here, the cause of tool wear leading to a higher roughness was due to wear of the active cutting
edge, leading to a larger tool diameter, thus adversely aﬀecting tool performance and the surface quality.
Inadequate tool sharpness and accelerated rates of wear was also found to produce machining problems such
as delamination, pitting, and burning of the matrix. This resulted in an increase in tool forces, and the heat
generated at the machining surface. The wear was found to be strongly inﬂuenced by parameters such as
spindle speed and feed rate, where an increase in the latter had a severe negative eﬀect on tool wear, resulting
in lower surface quality. These ﬁndings were contradicted by Delahaigue et al. (2017), who investigated the
inﬂuence of cutting temperature on the tensile strength of CFRP composites. Here, a lower surface roughness
was found for the worn tool both for conventional and climb milling, which was due to thermal damage caused
by the wear of the tool. This lead to the degraded matrix spreading out over the machined surface, creating a
more ﬂat and thus less rough surface. This observation was further conﬁrmed using a light optical microscope.
The surface roughness is not always a suﬃcient parameter on its own to describe the machining quality
in CFRP since subsurface damages may occur, such as intra-laminar delaminations, ﬁbre splitting and matrix
cracking. This observation was conﬁrmed in a study by Azmi et al. (2013) who investigated the machinability
of polymer composites during end milling. Although, a general trend was observed in terms of increase in
feed rate leading to a higher roughness, the results also revealed large variations. It was found that care had
to be taken when measuring the surface roughness of ﬁbrous composite materials, due to the presence of
fractured ﬁbres, ﬁbre protrusions, delamination damage along with matrix failure, leading to variation in
measurements. Other studies have reached a similar conclusion (Ramulu et al., 1993), (Ghidossi et al., 2004).
The diﬃculties with surface roughness as a sole identiﬁer of surface quality was further explored by Duboust
et al. (2017). Here, a comparison between contact based surface proﬁlometers, and optical methods were
studied for measuring the roughness of CFRP composites. The optical system works by focus variation, taking
multiple images at varying vertical distances and using optical sharpness to create a full high resolution 3D
surface image, which is then used for further roughness calculations. There were found to be large variations
in the roughness values when using a proﬁlometer, which depended upon the ﬁbre orientation, position and
angle of the stylus especially when measuring parallel to the ﬁbre orientation. It was also found that the stylus
path may not lie directly parallel with the ﬁbres, or pass over multiple ply layers. It can be diﬃcult to detect
which layer is being measured as each layer can vary slightly in thickness, and cannot always be detected
visually. As such, the transverse direction can be easier to measure as all measurements will be made across
the same amount of plies, as long as the stylus is placed at roughly the same start position each time. The
study also found that using an optical system allowed for accurate roughness calculation of individual plies,
which was due to better visibility of the composite structure. Furthermore, using this type of system enables
areal roughness parameters to be calculated such as Sa, which corresponds to the Ra parameter described
earlier. For calculating Ra the absolute value of the peak or valley is used, so it does not distinguish between
peaks or troughs. Similarly, the Sa parameter is described as the arithmetic mean of the absolute of the
ordinary values over a deﬁnition area. The concepts of sampling and evaluation areas are still deﬁned, as in
26
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 3
3.3. Slot Milling
the case of a line measurement, but the default is one sampling area per evaluation area. Thus, the roughness
parameters are calculated based on the measured surface without being segmented into smaller areas, as
described previously for the Ra parameter. The research also showed that the optical system, including the
use of areal roughness parameters, can increase the accuracy of roughness measurement for machined ﬁbrous
composite surfaces and is less sensitive to measurement position than the stylus. In the investigation by
Duboust et al. (2017) it was further found that the areal roughness values, Sa, were more useful in measuring
the surface texture due to a better repeatability and a larger characterisation area. Using an optical method
it is important to consider the magniﬁcation used to obtain the images for further measurements. Studies
have shown that an increase in roughness can be found when increasing the magniﬁcation. Generally, a
higher magniﬁcation will provide a more reliable result in terms of surface roughness since more details in the
texture of the sample can be detected. Thus, the lower magniﬁcation scans tend to underestimate both peak
and valley measurements in comparison to the highest magniﬁcation scan. Nevertheless, roughness can be
useful in combination with visual inspections such as microscopical evaluations and non-destructive testing
method (Voß, 2017).
3.3.1.3
Statistical Analysis of Surface Roughness
A two-way analysis of variance (ANOVA) with replicates can be used to measure the signiﬁcant eﬀect of two
factors, i.e. feed rate and spindle speed, along with their interaction, on a dependent variable, the surface
roughness. As such, this can be used to validate the experimental result by attaching a statistical meaning
to each of the factors involved. In an analysis of variance the variability between groups are compared to the
variability within each group, thus how much natural variation is there in the measurements. Furthermore,
the analysis will also provide insight into whether the interaction between the two parameters are relevant. If
there is a strong interaction correlation between them, then the two factors should be assessed together, as
one will aﬀect the other. Here, an interaction plot is often relevant, which illustrates the relationship between
the two factors and how they depend on each other. An important observation is that the interaction term
can only be accounted for if the ANOVA is conducted with replicates, meaning that each group contains more
than one result. If a two-way ANOVA without replicates is done, the interaction term cannot be accounted for
because the degrees of freedom (df) would be zero as df = observations- groups = 0. when there are only one
observation per group, thus 1-1=0. This model assumes no interaction between the factors (Veaux et al., 2004).
Before an ANOVA can be conducted certain assumptions must be met in order for it to be valid. Firstly,
the residuals should stem from a normal distribution, referred to as the normality, secondly the variance
between the samples should be equal, called homogeneity, and lastly the samples should be independent of
each other. The residuals are the diﬀerence between the actual value and the value predicted by the model,
thus the error, and its these that need to follow a normal distribution not the response variable. This is
due to the y variable, the response, also being inﬂuenced by the independent variables, the x value, which
the error is not. The requirement can be stated as: ϵ =i.i.d. N(0,σ2), where the ϵ is the error term, thus
the residual, and i.i.d states that each of these should be independent and identically distributed. To verify
this, a normal plot can be created, also referred to as a Q-Q plot, for the residuals, which should follow a
straight line if stemming from a normal distribution. The normality requirement is not fatal, and often the
analysis can be continued without normality if the sample size is large enough (Veaux et al., 2004), (Møller,
2018). Homogeneity, on the other hand, is important, and can be assessed by plotting the residuals against
the ﬁtted values, which are the ones predicted by the model. Here, a random pattern should be followed,
which illustrates equal variance across the groups. The random pattern is shown as the residuals not being
systematically high or low. So, the residuals should be centered on zero throughout the range of ﬁtted values.
In other words, the model is correct on average for all ﬁtted values. Furthermore, random errors are assumed
to produce residuals that are normally distributed, thus should fall in a symmetrical pattern and have a
constant spread throughout the range (Møller, 2018), (Stone et al., 2012). A residual plot should also be made
against each independent variable, namely the feed rate and spindle speed, to ensure that any variance not
accounted for by the model is diﬀerent for each level of a factor. A random scatter indicates that the model
is ﬁtting, however any pronounced curvature could indicate a systematic contribution from the factor, which
is not accounted for by the model (Anderson-Cook et al., 2018). The residual plots can be further veriﬁed
by conducting a Levene’s test. This will check for equal variance amongst the groups. The null hypothesis
27
==PAGE:48==
3.4. Countersinking
Chapter 3
for the test is that variance is equal across the groups normally using a signiﬁcance level of α = 0.05, thus if
the p-value is larger than 0.05, the residuals are homogeneous, and variance is equal across groups. A note
should be made that due to the robustness of the ANOVA test if the sample sizes are equal, this requirement
can, if needs be, be ignored and the test continued even if the assumption is not satisﬁed(Veaux et al., 2004).
In order to carry out the ANOVA analysis, a null hypothesis must be set up, which in this case consist
of three statements: that the means of observations grouped by one factor are the same; that the means of
observations grouped by the other factor are the same; and that there is no interaction between the two
factors. The interaction test tells you whether the eﬀects of one factor depend on the other factor. Put in
another way: With two factors, there are two hypotheses tests (excluding the interaction). Each of those
hypotheses asks whether the mean of the response variable, which is the surface roughness, is the same for
each of the treatment levels, and can be written as:
yijk = µτj + γk + ωjk + ϵijk
(3.3.2)
where y is the response variable, the surface roughness, µ is the average, τj the spindle speed, γk the feed
rate, ωjk is the interaction term of level j of the speed and level k of the feed, with ϵijk being the error, the
residual. The ANOVA analysis is often conducted using a statistical software program, which will display
a results table once the test is complete. This will contain information with regards to the sum of squares,
degrees of freedom, mean square, an F-value and a p-value. The ﬁrst parameter is related to the total variance
of the observations, the second is sample variance, the F-value is related to the statistic used to calculate
if a relationship exists between the factors based on the F distribution and the p-value is used to conclude
on any signiﬁcance. If this value is below the signiﬁcance level set by alpha, and often found to be 0.05, the
factor has an statistical signiﬁcance. This means the null hypothesis, as stated previously, can be rejected
(Veaux et al., 2004).
3.4
Countersinking
In this section an analysis and discussion on countersinking will be done, which is a ﬁnishing drilling operation
done after the initial hole has been machined. The ﬁrst part will introduce the technical aspect of the ma-
chining operation, which is followed by a section on delaminations. This will include methods of quantifying
the extent of the damage for a comparison to be done between samples having sustained varying degrees of
delamination. The last section is on the correlation between mechanical strength and delamination, which is
the main idea behind the countersink study. This will serve as an introduction to why these experiments are
needed and as a basis for the subsequent chapter on the theory behind mechanical testing.
The assembly of two diﬀerent composite parts or a joining of a part to the main body can be done in
two ways: Using countersunk or protruding head fasteners and rivets, as illustrated in Figure 3.4.1. The
former provide aerodynamic beneﬁts, which a protruding fastener does not, and is thus of particular interest
when assembling aircraft parts. Countersinking is a ﬁnishing operation, which is conducted after drilling
and is often done manually as found at Terma. Here, a conical recess is made at the top of the hole in
order to account for the fasteners needing to be parallel with the surface of the part. Furthermore, coaxiality
and concentricity has to established between the hole and the countersink (Fern´andez-P´ereza et al., 2017).
Delaminations are easily introduced in the vicinity of the countersunk hole, which could lead to a decrease
in strength, stiﬀness and the eﬀectiveness of the fastener to hold the two parts together, thus seriously aﬀect
the safety of the aircraft. Other defects, which can occur during drilling are chip-out of ﬁbres and the matrix,
which is due to a blunt drill not cutting the ﬁbres properly, whereby the rotation of the tool causes the ﬁbres
to be pulled out along with the matrix. This is mainly an occurrence in uni-directional laminates. Matrix
heat is the result of the drilling thrust or torque being too high, which generates excessive heat, and can lead
to matrix damage. However, the most prevalent defect is delaminations (Fern´andez-P´ereza et al., 2017).
28
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
Figure 3.4.1: Protruding and countersunk rivet (Magazine, 2018)
3.4.1
Surface Delamination
Delamination implies the separation of layers that form the material and may severely aﬀect the structural
integrity and long term reliability of the component. The introduction of delaminations occur as a result of
the inhomogeneous nature, anisotropic and abrasive characteristics of composites, as was also found with
milling. The excessive transverse cutting forces seen during drilling, and countersinking is another reason
for delaminations and ﬁbre fracture occurrence (Fern´andez-P´ereza et al., 2017), as illustrated in Figure
3.4.2. Furthermore, an imbalance between the ﬁbre volume and resin content can also be another source
of delaminations. If the composite contains inadequate amount of resin, the individual plies will separate
more easily during machining operations. The surface damage is enhanced by tool wear, inadequate cutting
speeds and feeds or improper tool geometry. For these reasons, such machining damages are not permitted on
primary structural components, while some repairable delamination may be tolerated on secondary parts. In
terms of machining parameters, tool geometry and wear, most studies have been done on drilling the hole and
not on the countersunk part. With drilling the essential aspect is to ensure a sharp tool (Fern´andez-P´ereza
et al., 2017) .
Figure 3.4.2: Damage experience during drilling and countersinking (Butler-Smith et al., 2015)
Drilling operations are associated with a thrust force, which acts normal to the ply and tends to separate the
layers in a laminate either by inter-laminate cracking or intra-laminate cracking. The former is an occurrence
between separate plies and the latter is inside one ply. Delamination during drilling can occur in two ways,
namely by peeling the top layer oﬀthe laminate or pushing out the bottom ply near the exit, as illustrated
in Figure 3.4.3. It should be noted that in countersinking only peel-up delamination will occur, as no exit is
made at the bottom of the composite(Dur˜a et al., 2014). The peel-up phenomenon is caused by the cutting
force pushing the abraded and cut materials to the ﬂute surface. Initially, the cutting edge of the drill will
abrade the laminate, but as the drill moves downward, it tends to pull the abraded material along the ﬂute,
29
==PAGE:50==
3.4. Countersinking
Chapter 3
which leads to the material moving upwards before eﬀectively being cut. This in turns creates an upwards
force, which can separate the top ply from the one below, thus leading to delamination. This peeling force is
a function of tool geometry and friction between tool and workpiece. The push-out delaminations are due to
compressive thrust forces, which the edge of the drill always exerts on the laminate. Here, the bottom ply
will break away from the one above, which means the interlaminar bonds are broken. As the drill approaches
the end of the laminate, the uncut thickness becomes smaller and the resistance to deformation decreases.
At some point before the laminate is totally penetrated by the drill, the loading exceeds the interlaminar
bond strength and delamination occurs (Dur˜a et al., 2014), (Fern´andez-P´ereza et al., 2017).
Figure 3.4.3: Illustration of push-out and peel-up delamination types when drilling (Uusitalo, 2013)
Tool wear is one of the main contributing factors leading to delaminations. In drilling CFRP composites, the
predominant wear mechanisms are abrasion, which is due to the nature of the rough carbon ﬁbres leading to
debris in the cutting region, and micro-chipping, stemming from the inhomogeneity of the material causing
varying forces at the cutting edge (Dur˜a et al., 2014). The amount of debris can be minimised by using a hard
tool, which can be achieved through a diamond coating. Furthermore, by using tools with a high toughness
the ﬂuctuating forces can also be reduced. In a study by Fern´andez-P´ereza et al. (2017), it was reported that
wear takes place at the corner of the cutting edge of the tool, which is where higher cutting forces are found
during drilling. Furthermore, the predominant mode of wear was reported as chipping, which is characterized
by ﬂakes due to presence of highly abrasive particles and the ﬂuctuation of forces. This combination produce
stress concentrations, leading to a weakening of the edge, thus resulting in delaminations. Eventually, after
some level of wear, the sharp edge is lost and the cutting geometry of the tool is modiﬁed. This illustrates
that a sharp cutting tool along with correct machining parameters are essential in achieving a high surface
quality for countersunk holes.
A study by Xu et al. (2016) found that the most prominent type of surface damage encountered dur-
ing countersinking were surface and interlaminar delaminations, tearing and protruding ﬁbres. Experiments
showed that these negatively aﬀect the structural integrity of the composite part. Furthermore, delamination
were recorded to occur when the drilling thrust exceeded a threshold value, which is termed the critical thrust
force. Caggiano (2018) investigated the machinability of CFRPs, and established that in order to achieve a
high surface quality, thus avoiding delaminations, certain machining parameters provided the best results. It
was shown that feed rate had the greatest inﬂuence on thrust force, push-out delamination, and diameter of
the hole. Here, lower feed rates reduced the thrust force and push-out delaminations, as seen in Figure 3.4.3,
however higher feed rates resulted in holes closer to the nominal diameter. The spindle speed was found to
be one of the major determinants of the circularity of the drilled hole. Neither spindle speed nor feed rate
had any visible inﬂuence on peel-up delamination within the tested range. In a study by Davima et al. (2017)
the eﬀect of cutting parameters on speciﬁc cutting pressure, delamination and cutting power in carbon ﬁbre
reinforced plastics were investigated. Here, the feed rate was found to have the most signiﬁcant inﬂuence on
thrust force conﬁrming the observations made by Caggiano (2018).
30
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
3.4.1.1
Delamination Factor
To compare the eﬀect of surface damage on the mechanical strength on composites, the delamination must
be quantiﬁed. Diﬀerent techniques have been used by researchers to assess the surface damage as a result
of drilling. Initially, the delaminations are evaluated using methods such as X-ray, optical micrograph, ultra-
sonic C-scan, digital photograph, acoustic emission and laser based imaging technique. These methods are
used to measure the extent of the delamination in terms of area, shape, orientation on the laminate and to
evaluate the frequency of occurrence. This data can then be used to calculate a single number in order to
more eﬀectively compare the extent of the damage between samples. Here, the delamination factor and the
adjusted delamination factor are the two most common parameters. Others have been introduced such as
the equivalent delamination factor, reﬁned equivalent delamination factor and minimum delamination factor
Dur˜ao et al. (2008), Davim et al. (2007). In this study, the focus will be on the ﬁrst two as these appear to
be most appropriate.
Chen (1997) ﬁrst proposed the concept of a delamination factor, namely the conventional Fd factor, to
easily compare and evaluate drilling damage. It can be expressed as a ratio of the maximum diameter to the
nominal hole diameter:
Fd = Dmax
D
(3.4.1)
as illustrated in Figures 3.4.4. The conventional delamination factor presents good results when the damaged
area follows a regular pattern, as often found in glass ﬁbre reinforced polymer (GFRP) composites. However,
when drilling CFRP composites, the delaminations are often in a irregular pattern, containing breaks and
cracks in the vicinity of the drilled hole. This leads to a delamination factor, which is not representative
of the delaminated area and its magnitude. Thus, a combination of measuring the crack and the damaged
area seems more suitable for obtaining a better visualisation of the damage after drilling. Based on this, an
adjusted delamination factor was introduced by Davim et al. (2007):
Fda = αDmax
D
+ β Amax
A
(3.4.2)
Fda = αFd + βF2
d
(3.4.3)
where the ﬁrst part of the Equation 3.4.2 represents the conventional delamination factor, and the second
part takes into account the damaged area. Here, Amax is the delaminated area related to Dmax and A is the
drilled area hole related to D as shown in Figure 3.4.4. In Equation 3.4.3 the conventional delamination factor,
Fd, has simply been inserted to give a simpler expression. The parameters α and β are used as weights in
Equation 3.4.3. The latter is the ratio of the damage area (Ad) to the area corresponding to Dmax subtracting
the nominal hole area, A. α is given as α = 1 −β. Thus, Equation 3.4.3 can now be written as:
Fda = Fd +
