In order to evaluate the damaged area the visual inspection methods introduced initially must be utilized,
where diﬀerent techniques can be used to analyse and capture the damaged area. In the study by Davim et al.
(2007), which saw the introduction of the corrected delamination factor, the area was calculated using ImageJ
where appropriate parameters were selected in terms of brightness, threshold, and image enhancement in
order to only having the damaged area in the image. Based on this the area was calculated.
3.4.1.2
Correlation Between Surface Delamination and Strength
The main body of work on the correlation between delamination and its eﬀect on strength is mainly focused on
sub-surface damage, and not so much surface delaminations, which indicates the need for further studies. As
such, this section will serve as a short introduction to the existing knowledge with regards to the connection
between delaminations and strength and to stipulate the need for the mechanical experiments introduced in
the subsequent sections.
In a study by Davima et al. (2017) a correlation between feed rate and mechanical loss by the bearing
strength was identiﬁed, where larger values of feed rate had an adverse eﬀect on the damage extension, thus
leading to mechanical loss. This initially lead to the indication that delaminations must also result in a loss
of bearing strength. However, when studying the diﬀerent tool geometries it was found that the ones leading
to a larger delamination zone, also lead to a higher bearing strength. This was found to be because of the
damage around the hole acting as a stress relief factor increasing the force-carrying capacity of the plate.
Thus, a higher amount of delaminations might not lead to lower strength. This observation is conﬁrmed
in ASTM D-5766M-02a (2007) and ASTM D-6484M-04 (2015), stating delaminations can blunt the stress
concentration due to the hole, increasing the load-carrying capacity of the specimen and the calculated tensile
and compressive strength.
Clarke (1997) investigated the eﬀect of artiﬁcially damaged, real damaged, and undamaged specimens. Here,
it was recorded that the undamaged specimens did not show any greater tensile strength than the damaged
specimens, however the former were able to absorb more energy before ﬁnal failure, meaning that a greater
strain to failure was observed for these. It was further recorded that delaminations did not reduce the tensile
strength of the specimens. In a study by Reis et al. (2009), where the eﬀect of interlayer delamination
behaviour of composites were investigated, the delaminations were found to have a negligible inﬂuence on
the fatigue strength for tensile cycle loadings. However, the damage did lead to a signiﬁcant reduction in
strength for fatigue loadings. An investigation by Ambu et al. (2005) found that the fatigue strength beneﬁted
from delamination by decreasing the stress concentration at the hole suppressing ﬁbre failure. The increase
in strength was also found in a study by O’Higgins et al. (2010) drew a comparison of open hole tensile
characteristics of GFRP and CFRP composites. Here, it was found that damage sustained prior to testing
had a direct positive eﬀect on the open hole tensile strength of the CFRP laminates. The given explanation
was intra-laminar damage at the the hole provides stress relief, which is due to a change in hole geometry
leading to a reduction in stress concentration, hence increasing the notched strength. Larger damage zones
typically provide more stress relief and hence greater notched strength. Conversely interlaminar damage, if
extensive, reduces the notched strength as individual uncoupled plies are free to fail by the fracture mode of
least resistance. In a study by Liu et al. (2018) a numerical analysis of the inﬂuence of deviation from nominal
countersinking hole geometries was investigated. This was done by inserting a delaminated ply below a few
layers of damage free CFRP plies. Here, it was found that when there is a delamination zone around the
countersunk area, the critical load causing further delamination growth is much smaller in comparison to other
cases. Through a comparison of an ideal hole to one containing delaminations, it was shown that the latter
failed under a small load during a tensile test in comparison to the former. Khechai et al. (2018) investigated
the strength degradation of samples containing diﬀerent shaped and sized holes were investigated. It was
shown that a larger hole diameter, whilst keeping the dimensions of all samples the same, lead to a reduction
in the tensile strength of the laminate. Although, none of the samples contained any delaminations prior to
testing, a comparison can be drawn between a larger hole diameter and a sample containing a delaminated
hole, as the diameter will extend out into the damaged area.
In case of tensile strength, contrary results are given in literature. E and Tagliaferri (2001) investigated
32
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
the eﬀect of machining parameters on damage zone extension and mechanical properties of GFRP. The
tensile strength remained constant with increasing damage whereas a clear decrease in bearing strength was
reported. In contrast, the results on woven GFRP published by Rao et al. (2008) and Parameshwaranpillai
et al. (2011) indicated that an increase in delamination severity lead to a a reduction of the tensile strength.
Dur˜ao et al. (2012) studied the eﬀect of delaminations on the tensile strength by performing an open hole ten-
sile test. Here, it was found that the delamination had to reach a certain level of severity before a reduction in
strength could be detected. Furthermore, three point bending tests were also performed on carbon-epoxy lami-
nates, which revealed no correlation between delaminations sustained prior to testing and the ﬂexural strength.
An experimental investigation was conducted by Rhodes et al. (1976) to determine the eﬀect of low im-
pact damage on the compressive strength of CFRP laminates. Fourteen panels for two diﬀerent load levels
were tested, and eight of the panels were damage by impact, which was done by ﬁring a aluminium projectile
at the pieces to simulate far-away debris. For the laminates designed with the lowest load levels, the impact
did not have an eﬀect on the compressive strength, however for the remaining panels, failed between 50 and
58 percent of the design load due to impact damage. Furthermore, limited damage that was not visually
detectable reduced ultimate strength as much as extensive visible damage. In contrast to these studies ASTM
D-6484M-04 (2015), which parts of the compressive test was based upon, states that the delaminations in
the vicinity of the hole might not lead to a reduction in strength. On the contrary, this might increase the
strength, which is due to longitudinal splitting and delamination diminishing the stress concentration caused
by the hole. This in turn increases the load carrying capacity of the specimen, thus the calculated strength.
There appear to be a signiﬁcant negative impact on strength due to sub-surface delaminations, which can be
introduced due to peel-up during drilling, however little literature could be found on surface delaminations
and their eﬀect on compressive strength. Although, delamination does not always lead to a reduction in
strength, several studies have shown the negative eﬀects of the damage in terms of load carrying capacity of
composites (Khechai et al., 2018), (Reis et al., 2009).
3.4.2
Mechanical Testing
A wide variety of forces and structural stresses act on an aircraft, where tension can be seen when the engine
pulls the aircraft forward, but air resistance tries to hold it back, and as a result the fuselage stretches. On the
other hand, compression is the stress that tends to shorten or squeeze aircraft parts. The bending of the wings
is a combination of the two stresses, where tension will be experienced on the top, and compression on the
bottom of the wing. As such, testing the open hole tension and compression strength is critical in ensuring the
aircraft is able to carry the intended load and safely continue ﬂying for its estimated lifetime. Experimental
testing is essential to implementing composite structures in the aerospace industry. It is necessary in order to
describe and verify the mechanical properties, where the current available predictive mathematical methods
are inadequate or diﬃcult to implement at design level (World, 2005). In this section the ﬁrst part focuses
on the open hole tensile (OHT) test, which includes a discussion of the mechanics behind the method, along
with an evaluation of the outcome in form of a stress-strain analysis and ultimate strength. The subsequent
section on open hole compression (OHC) testing follows the same general structure.
3.4.2.1
Open Hole Tension
Engineering structures are subjected to a variety of loads, such as tension, which makes testing necessary in
order to compare diﬀerent materials, lay-up systems and give an insight into the basic mechanical properties.
Furthermore, the test can be useful in deciding whether damage sustained prior to testing has an eﬀect
on the outcome. The addition of a hole in the structure can lead to a reduction in strength due to stress
concentrations around the hole, and is thus a limiting factor. As such, the open hole tensile test is important
in determining the maximum allowable stress in a design (Hallett et al., 2009b).
Tensile testing works by placing the sample in between two grips pulling at opposite sides of the sam-
ple at a set speed. The extension along with the load, which is required to continue pulling the sample, is
continuously recorded. Once failure occurs the test is stopped. From this data the stress, σe, and strain,ϵe
33
==PAGE:54==
3.4. Countersinking
Chapter 3
can be calculated using the following equations (Callister, 2007):
σe = P
A0
(3.4.5)
ϵe = δ
L0
(3.4.6)
where P is the load in Newtons, A0 is the gross cross-sectional area disregarding the hole as stated in ASTM
D-5766M-02a (2007) for tensile testing. The gross cross-sectional area is the width of the sample multiplied
by the thickness, δ is the extension, and L0 is the original length of the test sample. Equation 3.4.6 normalises
the data by taking into account the dimensions of the specimens, thus allowing a comparison between samples
with diﬀerent geometries (Maloney, 2006). There is a need to normalise specimens of varying lengths when
calculating the strain, which is due to longer samples reaching a higher elongation length in comparison to
a shorter sample. The longer the specimen, the lower the stress required to obtain an equal deformation,
which is due to stress being proportional to strain not to deformation. Furthermore, although this is by
deﬁnition the engineering strain, which can deviate from the true strain, for small strains below 0.1, the
same results are obtained (Maloney, 2006). The strain is normally measured by attaching an extensometer
to the specimen, which will then record the change in length during testing. The displacement of the top
conical grip can also be used to calculate the strain, by taking the movement as the extension of the specimen,
referred to as the crosshead movement. This is measured by the digital position encoder that is included as
part of the testing equipment. The former method is less prone to errors, thus will present a more accurate
result in terms of strain. The latter measurements are especially aﬀected by movement of the specimen as
ultimate failure is reached, thus at the maximum applied load (Instron, 2011), (Berggreen, 2018). In a study
by Ugiansky and Payer (1979) it was shown that at any load, the specimen displacement is only a fraction of
the crosshead displacement with the remaining movement being due to the load train and the testing machine.
As yielding is occurring the displacement will mainly occur in the specimen and not the machine. However,
several studies have contradicted this observation, such as one by Perkins (2005), who investigated measuring
the tensile strength at high strain rates. Here, a comparison between measurements using an extensometer
and the crosshead movement was made and revealed that both methods produced satisfying results. In a
study by Kumara et al. (2014), where the use of miniature tensile specimens for measurement of mechanical
properties were investigated, the crosshead movement was also utilised for strain measurements. Reliable
results were obtained using this method, however some scattering of the data was observed in some cases.
Ou et al. (2017) investigated the eﬀects of gauge length and strain rate on tensile behaviour of Kevlar single
ﬁlament and yarn. The crosshead movement was found to be a representative measure, and used for strain
calculations. Although, the uniform strain up to maximum load should not be aﬀected by the diﬀerence in
specimen length, a few studies have indicated that this is incorrect. In an investigation by Legerlotz et al.
(2010), where the inﬂuence of specimen dimensions on material properties of tendon fascicles was studied,
there was found to be a dependency on strain in terms of specimen length, where a longer sample lead to
a reduction in strain. Although, this was not done on CFRP composites a comparison can be drawn across
the complexity of both materials. A way of accounting for a diﬀerence in length between samples is by using
the gauge length instead of the original length of the sample in Equation 3.4.6. This does assumes that each
specimen will experience the same movement in the grips during the measurements, but would take into
account any embedded machining errors(Berggreen, 2018). Han et al. (2009) studied the eﬀect of specimen
dimensions on obtained tensile properties of sheet metals, and although this was done on metals rather than
composites, the same principles should hold true in the elastic region as both materials exhibit a linear
relationship. Here, strain was found to be the only mechanical property depending slightly on geometry,
however due to variations seen for specimens of the same dimensions the following was concluded: both the
specimen geometry and the way that the strain was measured have an eﬀect on the measured value. The
stress calculations does not appear to be aﬀected by specimen size, and is thus more representative of strength
in comparison to maximum load, as concluded by Han et al. (2009). The next step is to plot stress versus
strain, as this is how the ultimate tensile strength of the material is found. This describes the maximum load
the specimen can endure before failure, and is the maximum on the aforementioned curve. It is an intensive
property; therefore its value does not depend on the size of the test specimen.
In terms of failure in tensile testing, there are two situations to consider, namely where the matrix fails ﬁrst,
and where the ﬁbre fails ﬁrst, where each of these will generate a slightly diﬀerent response in terms of the
34
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
stress-strain curve, although the general shape is the same (Pilling, 2018). The former is most commonly
found for polymer matrices such as epoxies, whereas the later is mainly for metal matrices. This is due to the
metal displaying plastic deformation behaviour, thus being able to strain further in comparison to the ﬁbres,
whereas the epoxy matrices are found to be more brittle in comparison. The brittleness of the epoxy arises
due to the behaviour of densely cross-linked polymers, which is seen in the form of matrix intra-laminar ply
cracking, which precedes the ﬁbre rupture. Early cracking promotes development of critical failure modes
such as inter-ply delamination, and will be discussed in a subsequent part of the section (Turk et al., 2017).
Since the composite used in this study is a carbon reinforced composite with an epoxy matrix, the focus will
only be on the one former. Three standard stress-strain responses can be observed for CFRP composites,
however the latter case is for specimens under constant load, in contrast to the constant strain rate used
in this experimental investigation. As such, this will not be discussed further. The remaining two cases are
illustrated in Figures 3.4.5(a) and (b), where the curve for each individual component along with the entire
composite can be found. In case (a) both the ﬁbre and matrix are linear elastic until failure, but the matrix
will fail at a considerably lower strain than the ﬁbre, as seen in the ﬁgure as well. Strain is the important
factor in determining the failure strength of the composite when testing parallel to the ﬁbres because both the
ﬁbre and the matrix will experience the same strain. This failure is referred to as elastic brittle. Failure will
occur suddenly without any drop or increase in strain. In case (b) the tensile test is done under constant strain
rate, also referred to as strain controlled. If part of the composite breaks, such as the matrix, as illustrated
in case (a), the strain rate does not change at that instance, but the ﬁbres will continue to stretch by the
same strain. As such, the stress in the ﬁbres remain the same, however the load along with the stress will
fall across the whole composite, as illustrated by the drop in stress on the graph. The ﬁbres will continue to
resist the strain being imposed upon the matrix and will do so until sudden failure (Pilling, 2018).
Figure 3.4.5: a) Standard stress-strain curve for CFRP laminate and b) Under constant strain-rate (Pilling,
2018)
Although case (a) displays an ideal curve, this is often found to be the failure mode as seen in studies by
Kwon and Huh (2016), Treasurer (2006) and Clarke (1997), whereas case (b) is only observed in some cases
as found by Schreiber et al. (2017) and Dany Arnoldo Hernandeza (2017). Following the each test the failure
mode for the specimen is recorded according to ASTM D-5766M-02a (2007), as illustrated in Figure 3.4.6.
35
==PAGE:56==
3.4. Countersinking
Chapter 3
Figure 3.4.6: Accepted failure modes according to ASTM D-5766M-02a (2007)
Tensile loading of a notched specimen leads to a variety of damage types, which can cause ultimate failure
of the sample or simply result in a redistribution of stress around the hole. The sub-critical damage, which
develops at relatively low load levels prior to ultimate failure has been identiﬁed as being signiﬁcant for
determining the specimen strength and ﬁnal breakdown mode Garcea et al. (2017). Prior to this, the loading
of the specimen can lead to matrix cracking, ﬁbre-matrix splitting, delamination, and ﬁbre breakage, as
illustrated in Figure 3.4.7. The prediction of when and where the failure will occur is diﬃcult as this is based
on the material, lay-up and stacking sequence. Generally, the ﬁrst mode to occur is matrix cracking (Wisnom,
2012), (Hallett et al., 2009a). This starts at the free edge of the hole, where splits are occurring within the
plies. The splits form in the ﬁbre direction, typically tangential to the hole, except for the 90◦direction ply,
where the cracking is found to be more considerable. In the initial stages, the splits are isolated and only
occur around the edge of the hole, however as the load is increased, inter-laminar delamination starts, which is
where the splits join between adjacent layers. The stresses between the layers typically stem from the lay-up
and stacking sequence, and is most often seen when stacking 90◦and 0◦plies together or having a 90◦core.
Furthermore, when stacking 90◦and 45◦plies together, a delamination triangle can form between the layers,
and the length of the splits will increase with applied load (Treasurer, 2006). Although, the delamination
tend to happen around the notch, it can also appear at the edges of the specimen due to the free edge eﬀect
Garcea et al. (2017). The initial stage is followed by the critical event of the delamination at the hole joining
the specimen edge, which is where the delamination passes through the thickness of the sample. This occurs
in the 90◦ply through a crack, and will continue to run along the 90◦/45◦interface until the 0◦ply is reached.
At this point the growth of the delamination can no longer continue through the thickness of the specimen as
full width cracking has happened, and the hole and edge delaminations have connected. As such, the growth
continues along the length of the specimen causing major delaminations and leading to a signiﬁcant drop in
the load. The given description of sub-critical damage is relevant for most specimens, however this is not the
cause of failure for all, as this also geometry, thus a variation in geometric parameters also lead to a variation
in the rate of damage dependent(Wisnom, 2012), (Hallett et al., 2009a). Damage develops as the load on the
sample is increased, which eventually causes the breakage of ﬁbres. This is when ﬁbres are stressed beyond
their load carrying capabilities and fracture happens. The amount of damage, which has taken place, when
the ﬁbre failure stress is exceed is a governing factor on the strength, but also the failure mode of a given
specimen Garcea et al. (2017). The mechanisms are illustrated in Figure 3.4.7. Studies have shown matrix
cracking to be insigniﬁcant in terms of leading to failure during tensile testing in the ﬁbre orientation. This
is due to most of the load being carried in the ﬁbres. However, matrix cracking can play a signiﬁcant role in
properties heavily inﬂuenced by the matrix, such as compression strength or transverse modulus. Further,
delamination can result from the local strain concentrations caused by matrix cracking, which can result in
a reduction of strength and stiﬀness (Smith, 2001).
36
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
Figure 3.4.7: Failure mechanisms for tensile loading (Garcea et al., 2017)
The lay-up is a main factor inﬂuencing the ultimate failure of the composite, which describes how many layers
of each orientation is included in the laminate, and relates to reaching a speciﬁc strength and stiﬀness. The
diﬀerent orientations each provide the laminate with speciﬁc properties. The 0◦orientated ply can be added
for longitudinal strength, the 45◦ply for rotational and bending stiﬀness, the 90◦ply is for transverse strength,
where the plies all contribute to the general stiﬀness of the composite.Thus, by changing the number of plies
in the diﬀerent directions the properties of the composite can be altered. Furthermore, studies have shown
that the damage mode also changes with lay-up. The stacking sequence, which describes how each individual
ply is arranged, is also important in determining the strength of the composite (Callister, 2007). Here, high
inter-laminar stresses and delaminations can result from an inadequate stacking sequence (Herakovich, 1989).
As seen the stacking sequence and ply orientation have a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the failure modes and the
strength of the composite. In a study by Yan et al. (1998), the strength of the laminate was observed to
increase due to the addition of 0◦plies. This was attributed to a stress concentration from ﬁbre-matrix
splitting. Park (2001) investigated the eﬀect of stacking sequence and clamping force on pinned and bolted
joints, and found that by having a lay-up sequence consisting of 90◦plies at the surface the delamination
strength increased. The eﬀect of stacking sequence on strength was conﬁrmed in a study by Tay et al. (2005),
who examined the open-hole tensile strength of laminates and found the pattern of damage progression to
depend on the stacking sequence for every ply. Starikov and Schon (2005) found that both the tensile and
compressive strength of bolted joints improved when increasing the number of 0◦. Furthermore, the tensile
strength was found to exceeded the compressive strength. In a study by Achard et al. (2014), it was stated
that for composite specimens under tensile loading the structural and ultimate failures are related to the state
of damage of the 0◦plies. The other plies at diﬀerent orientations are often already damaged, however without
loss of stiﬀness. This was in contradiction to a previous study by Hallett et al. (2009b), who investigated the
scaling eﬀects of notched samples during tensile testing. Here, all the plies had an inﬂuence on the type of
failure mode and the ultimate load level irrespective of their orientation. However, studies have indicated
that open hole tensile strength shows less sensitivity to stacking sequence, which is due to failure mainly
occurring due to stress concentrations around the hole. The inter-laminar stresses can be reduced by avoiding
90◦plies on the centreline.
The geometry of the sample is another aspect to consider when conducting a tensile test of notched components.
Especially, the specimen width to hole diameter (W/D) ratio appears to be signiﬁcant in terms of strength.
In ASTM D-5766M-02a (2007) standard, which describes the open hole composite tensile testing procedure,
the ratio is recommended to be kept at 6. This is because there is a correlation between the size of the hole
and the distance from the free edge. When the distance from the edge of the hole to the edge of the sample is
small, thus a small W/D ratio, the stress at the hole is large compared to the remote stress. However, when the
plate is wide compared to the hole diameter, the stress eﬀect of the hole is reduced (Portanova and E.Masters,
37
==PAGE:58==
3.4. Countersinking
Chapter 3
1995). Thus, the purpose of having a good width to diameter ratio is to avoid any interaction between the
stress concentration at the hole edge and the specimen edge. In a study by Portanova and E.Masters (1995),
where standard test methods for open hole tension testing of textile composites were investigated, only a
slight increase in strength was observed for increasing the width-to-diameter ratio. Contrastingly, in study by
Sen et al. (2010) it was shown that as the edge distance-to-hole diameter (E/D) increased so did the strength
of the composite, which was also seen for an increase in the width-to-diameter (W/D) ratio. This theory was
also investigated by Wisnom et al. (2009) where a ratio of 5 was used, hence lower than the speciﬁed 6 by the
ASTM standard. By using linear elastic assumptions, this was found to be suﬃcient in allowing the stress
at the free edge to be equal to the far ﬁeld applied stress. However, even with a rather high ratio, previous
investigations have found that the damage at the hole edge and the specimen edge join up, which then leads
to failure. Hence, it could be expected that increasing this distance over which the damage has to join up
before it can propagate would increase the strength of a specimen. Nilsson et al. (2009) studied the eﬀect of
varying the hole diameter and width of specimens, whilst keeping the ratio the same. Here, it was found that
as the diameter increased, the strength decreased, although the ratio was maintained at 6 for all tests. This
illustrates that a key aspect of tensile testing is not only keeping the ratio of the samples constant in terms of
the width-to-diameter aspect, but actually maintaining the same width and hole diameter for each specimen.
This has been further conﬁrmed in studies by O’Higgins et al. (2010), where the strength of an open hole
sample decreased with increasing hole diameter. A possible explanation for this was put forth by Whitney
and Nuismer (1974), who showed that for open hole laminates with varying diameters, but all with the same
stress concentration factor, the stress droped oﬀmore steeply moving away from the hole, for smaller holes. It
was reasoned that this increased the probability of having a ﬂaw in the highly stressed region around a larger
hole, resulting in a lower average strength for this laminate. Recently, in an investigation by Khechai et al.
(2018), various width to diameter ratios were tested in order to assess the eﬀect of this. Here, it was shown
that at ratios higher than w/D = 25 mm/7.5 mm = 3.3, the open hole was completely isolated by splits in
the 0◦plies with respect to the loading direction, and behaving as if there was no hole. The thickness of the
sample is another geometric parameter to consider, as a study by Coats and Harris (1999) showed. Here,
the eﬀects of laminate thickness were examined, and for thick laminates, pre-failure damage near the notch
only occurred in the outer plies. As such, the greater the laminate thickness, the less eﬀect damage had on
the stress distribution prior to failure. Again, these studies illustrate the importance in maintaining similar
geometries in terms of specimen width and thickness along with hole diameter. In a study by M.R.Wisnom
and J.W.Atkinson (1997), the reduction in strength of unidirectional ﬁbre epoxy with increasing gauge length
was investigated. The conclusion was that gauge length did not appear to have any signiﬁcant contribution
to the tensile strength of the tested specimens. Ata¸s et al. (2017) studied the eﬀect of changing the specimen
length, width and thickness during tensile testing. Here, there were found to be no eﬀect of changing the
sample geometry in terms of stiﬀness and strength for both studies. This was further conﬁrmed by Ou et al.
(2017), where the eﬀects of gauge length and strain on the tensile behaviour of a single ﬁlament and yarn was
investigated. The gauge length was found to have no signiﬁcant eﬀect, however a distinct drop in uniform
elongation was observed for thin sheet specimens with increasing gauge length to width ratio.
3.4.2.2
Open Hole Compression
The tensile strength of composites mainly stem from the high tensile strength of the carbon ﬁbres of the
material. In compression, however, the matrix and the interface between the matrix and ﬁbres are the most
important factors as the former provide lateral support for the ﬁbres, thus preventing column buckling. As
with tensile loading, the introduction of a hole in the composite reduces the strength, and these become critical
regions under loading. However, where improvements have been made to the tensile properties resulting in a
doubling of the strength, compressive strength have not improved. Thus, some carbon ﬁbre-epoxy composites
have compressive strength 40 % lower than that of the tensile strength, (Leopold et al., 2017). This would
indicate that compressive strength is a better measure to use during the design phase to ensure that the parts
are able to withstand the required loads. Furthermore, due to the brittle nature of the CFRP composites, as
introduced in Section 3.4.2.1, this leads to a sudden, and brittle failure, with complex damage mechanism,
which can be hard to predict (Smith, 1994) (Ahn et al., 2013).
In compression testing the specimen is placed between two parallel plates and pressure is applied such
38
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
that the specimen is compressed, rather than pulled apart as in tension. Equations 3.4.6 and 3.4.5 can still be
applied to compression as stated in ASTM D-6484M-04 (2015), albeit with negative values for the extension, δ,
and the load, P, but the theory behind is still the same Callister (2007). In terms of the resulting stress-strain
response stemming from the test, this is slightly diﬀerent to the one presented for tensile, which is due to
the compressive behaviour being strongly inﬂuence by the matrix. Figure 3.4.8(a) illustrates a standard
stress-strain curve for carbon/epoxy composites evaluated at room temperature, as found in a study on the
compressive strength of composites by Torabizadeh (2013). Here, a slightly non-linear response begins to
develop after the initial start of the test, until ﬁnal failure occurs catastrophically, as indicated by the sudden
drop. The non-linearity can be explained in terms of the interface bonding of the matrix and ﬁbres, where
defects can arise during the manufacturing process, which are then incorporated into the specimen during
curing. This will inﬂuence the matrix capability of supporting the ﬁbres during compression. The curvature
becomes more pronounced as the strain increases, which highlights the role of the matrix as that of support
to the ﬁbres. In some cases signiﬁcant non-linearity can also be observed just before maximum load, which is
associated with the plastic deformation of the epoxy matrix. Figure 3.4.8 (b) shows a stress-strain response
of a laminate with an insert similar to parts containing a honeycomb core, which is the case for some of the
parts produced by Terma, as introduced in Section 3.1.3.1 . The curve was obtained in a study by Lemanski
et al. (2013) where coupons were fabricated with inserts. The samples comprised of straight plies on the outer
surfaces with central blocks of discontinuous and continuous wavy plies. Initially, an elastic response was
observed, but due to the onset and propagation of shear damage in the cohesive layer between the insert and
the other layers non-linearity started to show until peak load, as indicated by the number 1. Following this,
the insert started to separate from the other plies, and global deformation was observed, which lead to the
reduction in load and then a subsequent increase in load, as indicated by the number 2 on Figure 3.4.8(b).
This eventually resulted in micro-buckling in one of the straight layers surrounding the insert, number 3 on
the graph, which lead to a further reduction in strength. Although, the loss of strength arose due to the
compressive microbuckle-type failure, which was found to occur in the straight layers around the insert, this
was only possible after delamination. This failure is therefore delamination driven.
Figure 3.4.8: (a) Standard stress-strain curve during compression for CFRP (Torabizadeh, 2013), and (b)
Part containing insert (Lemanski et al., 2013)
In terms of the failure strain studies have noticed the importance of stacking sequence and ply thickness,
as observed by Amacher et al. (2014). In this study thin ply composites were investigated through an
experimental and numerical characterisation. The thinner composites exhibited a much delayed damage
growth and showed a quasi-brittle failure instead of extensive delamination and transverse cracking patterns
as observed for thicker specimens. This observation was conﬁrmed in a study by Leopold et al. (2017). In
this study, it was found that a decrease in the ply thickness of the individual layers lead to higher a value of
failure strain and strength. This was found to be because of smaller delaminations being present in thinner
plies, although a larger amount was detected as these were spread out over a wider range of plies. This was
compared with composites having the same overall thickness, but thicker, thus less individual plies, where
the strength was found to be much lower. In terms of failure modes, the same notation can be used as for
tensile, as seen in Figure 3.4.6, where Figure 3.4.9 illustrates the three most commonly observed failures in
compression.
39
==PAGE:60==
3.4. Countersinking
Chapter 3
Figure 3.4.9: Typical compression failure modes: (a) Shear, (b) axial splitting and (c) kink zones and fracture
(de Paiva et al., 2003)
In order to understand compressive failure in multi-directional laminates, it is necessary to develop an
understanding of the underlying mechanisms. This is best done by observing a uni-directional ply with a 0◦
orientation, as this carries the load in multi-directional plies Callister (2007). As such, the initial part of this
section will be focused on the theory of micro-buckling, thereafter multi-directional laminate compression
will be discussed. One of the ﬁrst models for predicting the compressive strength of composite laminates was
developed by Rosen (1964), which was based on microbuckling. He proposed two classes of microbuckling,
namely an extension mode with out-out phase buckling and a shear mode with in-phase buckling. The former
is valid for lower volume fractions, and is when the ﬁbres act independently of each other. Most modern
composites have a relative high ﬁbre volume fraction, thus in-phase buckling is observed, which is where the
ﬁbres interact with each other, as shown in Figure 3.4.10(a). The in-phase buckling leads to formation of
kink-bands if load is continuously applied.
Figure 3.4.10: (a) In-phase microbuckling, (b) Formation of kink-bands and (c) Shear failure (Leopold et al.,
2017)
Two separate studies by Moran et al. (1995) and Moran and Shih (1998) investigated the buckling leading
to kinking in ﬁbrous materials and related this to composites, as the two have similar compressive failure
mechanisms. Here, three stages of failure was identiﬁed, namely incipient or developing kinking, transient
kinking and ﬁnally steady state kinking. The ﬁrst stage is initiated on a small scale and is characterised by
local plastic shearing and buckling of the carbon ﬁbres. Next, in the transient stage, the kinked areas grow
and coalesce leading to the formation of a single kink band across the sample. Following this, a steady state
is reached, which is where the band starts to broaden laterally. The geometry of a kink band is illustrated in
Figure 3.4.10(b). For composites, these stages are hard to distinguish, because the fracture happens quite
rapidly, where the initial kinking stage occurs when the stress of the matrix shearing reaches a critical value.
An analytical model developed by Budiansky and Fleck (1998) describes the initiation and propagation of
the kink bands having an orientation angle β, width ω, and an inclination angle α of the ﬁbres, as shown
40
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
in Figures 3.4.10(b) and 3.4.10(c). In a study by Pimenta et al. (2009) et al. the initiation of microbuckling
and the associated kinking was investigated. The latter was described as a shear driven ﬁbre failure where a
distinct shearing angle was observed, and was found to be initiated by ﬁbre failure. This occurs when the
matrix is suﬃciently strong, and the interface between the matrix and ﬁbres are strong enough to prevent
microbuckling and kinking. The presence of defects along the ﬁbre and at the laminate surface deteriorates
the strength of the ﬁbres and can lead ﬁbre fracture.
Failure in the compression of composites laminates with an open hole is due to the initiation and growth
of a microbuckle starting at the edge of the hole. This starting point of compressive failure is due to the
free edge eﬀect, where due to the high peel stresses, delamination between two layers start at the edges
(Tsampas, 2013). In terms of failure mechanisms in compression of multi-directional laminates the research
is still ongoing, and is not yet entirely understood. However, three failure modes can be distinguished, which
are illustrated in Figure 3.4.11, and found as :
 Fibre fracture (ﬁbre failure)
 Delamination, occurring between the plies, thus interlaminar failure
 Ply splitting (matrix cracking or shearing), which occurs within the plies, thus intralaminar failure
Figure 3.4.11: Compression failure in the form of ply splitting, delamination and ﬁbre fracture (Greenhalgh,
2009)
As shown through the literature review so far, several studies found that failure is controlled by micro-buckling
in the 0◦plies, which is followed by delamination and then the formation of a kink-band. As such, the com-
pressive failure at the hole is partly a result of delamination. Delamination is the separation of individual
plies from each other and can occur when there is a change in ply orientation with regards to the adjacent
layers. The interlaminar stresses cause plies or groups of plies in a laminate to separate from each other,
leading to delamination. This initiates and propagates in the matrix layers intervening the reinforcement
plies of the composite system, limiting their life and performance Dikshit et al. (2017).
The stacking sequence has a pronounced eﬀect on the failure of composites during compression testing,
as veriﬁed in a study by Ratwani and Kan (1982). Here, a series of experiments were conducted on notched
16- ply graphite/epoxy laminates to understand damage propagation and failure modes in diﬀerent layup
conﬁguration composite laminates. The failure mode was found to change depending on the stacking se-
quence, which can be attributed to the stress redistribution within the laminate. Wang et al. (2004) drew
a comparison between experimental and simulated results for open hole compression and found that the
compressive strength of a lay-up with subsurface 0◦plies is higher than that of a unidirectional lay-up,
which is due to the higher stability of the load bearing ﬁbres. Thus, stacking sequence and good support
of the 0◦layer is essential in achieving high compressive strength. In a study by Wisnom et al. (2009) the
compression strength of various sized composite specimens samples were tested. It was shown that for a
quasi-isotropic laminate consisting of 0◦and 45◦plies, there are two mechanisms, which can lead to failure.
Failure is initiated in the 45◦ply by matrix shearing, whereas ﬁbre instability is the cause of failure in the
41
==PAGE:62==
3.4. Countersinking
Chapter 3
0◦ply. Furthermore, three types of failure modes were observed, namely brittle, push-out and delaminations.
This can be explained in terms of the point at which the ﬁbre failure occurs during the damage development.
In all cases, the failure was initiated in the top ply, orientated at 45◦, where the crack then moved down
the laminate by delaminations and further matrix cracking until the 45◦/0◦interface was reached. If ﬁbre
failure occurs before signiﬁcant damage, a brittle type failure is seen. If greater damage occurs ﬁrst, the
failure is pull-out, whilst complete delamination is seen if the stress does not reach a level suﬃcient to cause
ﬁbre failure. This observation was also made by Greenhalgh (2009), where compression of unidirectional and
multi-directional laminates were investigated. Here, it was found that once the load-bearing 0◦ﬁbres have
failed, the 45◦layers soon fail afterwards. However, unlike in tension, these layers are constrained by the
surrounding plies under compressive loading.
In a study by Pinnell et al. (1993), who conducted a fractographic analysis of quasi-isotropic laminates
loaded in compression, several interesting observations were made. Experimental results from [0/45/90/- 45]s
specimens, showed the delaminations were generally located at the 0◦/45◦and 45◦/90◦interfaces. Extensive
damage was observed in the ([0/45/90/- 45]) laminate with a majority of the delaminations occurring at
the 0◦/45◦and 45◦/90◦interfaces. One large trans-laminar fracture, at an angle to the specimen’s thickness,
was observed, which consisted of ﬁbre fractures occurring in the 0◦plies, as well as matrix fracture and
ﬁbre-matrix debonding in the oﬀ-axis plies. The 0◦plies sustained ﬁbre fracture while the oﬀ-axis plies failed
via matrix cracking and/or ﬁbre-matrix debonding, thus delaminations were observed at the 0◦/45◦and
45◦/90◦interfaces. This is illustrated in Figure 3.4.12, where arrow 1 indicates ﬁbre fracture in the 0◦ply
and arrow 2 illustrates ﬁbre/matrix debonding, and delaminations.
Figure 3.4.12: Compressive failure of ([0/45/90/-45]s) laminate with arrows indicating (1) ﬁbre fractures and
(2) ﬁbre/matrix debonding (Pinnell et al., 1993)
In another study by Wiggenraad and Ubels (1997), who investigated numerous lay-ups and their eﬀect on
delaminations after compression testing, made the same observations in regards to the 0◦/90◦interface and
delaminations. However, in a complex stacking sequence, it was found that due to the many ply angle changes,
thus jump in stiﬀness, this resulted in a larger number of smaller delaminations. The widest ones were located
along the two 90◦degree plies. With regards to the quasi-isotropic laminates, delamination in the 45◦/-45◦
ply interface was also characterized by resin-poor and resin-rich zones due to manufacturing.
A phenomenon observed in epoxy laminates is matrix cracking or ply splitting, and can be seen in the
form of cracks in the individual layers. The factors which dictate the development and the extent of the
ply splitting are the interfacial ﬁbre/matrix strength, the strength and stiﬀness of the matrix as well as the
presence of other defects particularly delamination. Ply splitting can occur when the matrix fractures parallel
to the ﬁbre direction, in the laminate plane or in shear, as illustrated in Figure 3.4.13 Tsampas (2013).
42
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
Figure 3.4.13: Ply splitting in (a) longitudinal (b) and (c) transverse and (d) shear direction (Tsampas, 2013)
Furthermore, the ply splits can occur in three diﬀerent modes, which is dependent on the orientation of the
fracturing plies: Transverse cracking, which is the splitting of 90◦plies, oﬀ-axis splitting in the ± 45◦plies,
and longitudinal splitting of the 0◦plies. The latter is usually dominated by intralaminar shear fracture and
can promote the formation of other damage mechanisms such as delamination. There does seem to be some
discrepancy as to which mechanism accompanies ply splitting. Delamination is in most cases induced by
ply splits, but can also occur after or simultaneously with kinking. Essentially, delamination separates the
laminate into two or more sub-laminates which can buckle out-of-plane and consequently lead to multiple
delaminations across the width of the specimen. Usually, depending on the load application, multiple de-
laminations lead to global fracture and collapse of the laminate. In some cases during compression testing,
buckling of the laminate will occur, which will induce ﬂexural loads onto the already applied compression
load. This bending moment will then induce delaminations, which will reduce the support of the load bearing
ply. Even though delamination reduces the load bearing capability of the axial plies, little or no ﬁbre fracture
occurs at this failure sequence since delaminations dominate across the specimen width (Tsampas, 2013),
(Greenhalgh, 2009).
As seen from the above discussion failure of composites is often due to a combination of fracture modes. The
load-bearing 0◦plies fail in compression and exhibit the features associated with micro-buckling, where a
typical example of compressive failure can be observed in Figure 3.4.14. However, the other layers, which
are oﬀ-axis, might fail due to other mechanisms, such as shear. The collapse of the laminated is usually
characterised by delamination between the diﬀerent layers and ply splitting within the plies. Furthermore,
monitoring of the compressive failure process in composites is diﬃcult because failure is quite sudden without
much warning. This dynamic and sudden nature of the ﬁnal failure makes it diﬃcult to identify the micro-
scopic failure sequence (Hahn et al., 1986). It is thus important to evaluate all damages during compression
testing, and make an assessment based upon this.
Figure 3.4.14: Typical compression failure as indicated by the in-plane crack (Greenhalgh, 2009)
43
==PAGE:64==
3.4. Countersinking
Chapter 3
Although, the initiation of kink-bands is initiated at ﬂaws or defects, where a hole can be classiﬁed as either,
other factors also play an important role in fracture. As seen from previous discussions, the lay-up and
stacking play a critical role in failure during compression, however so does specimen geometry. In studies
by Lee and Soutis (2009), it was found that the sample and layer thickness also inﬂuence the compressive
strength, where strength was seen to increase with a decrease in dimensions and ply thickness. The open
hole compressive failure was found to depend on interlaminar toughness, where a high toughness leads to a
relatively slow brittle failure, whereas a weaker interlaminar interface results in a sudden failure. Wisnom
et al. (2009) investigated the eﬀect of scaling composite samples on the compressive strength was investigated.
In order to conduct a thorough test, the width to diameter and length to diameter ratios were kept constant
at 5, whilst varying the length, width and hole diameter. The conclusion of the study was that increasing
the width or the hole diameter of the sample would result in a reduction of the strength although the ratios
were maintained at 5. The compressive strength of samples without a hole was found to be limited by the
stress concentrations developing at the end of the tabs, and manufacturing defects rather than the specimen
size. However, for the open-hole compression strength, the reduction in strength was only due to a change in
hole size rather than specimen thickness or volume increase. Khechai et al. (2018) found that with increasing
hole diameter to specimen width ratio, compressive strength decreases, thus in order to make a comparison
between specimens, maintaining the same width to diameter ratio is essential.
Macrobuckling is another common phenomenon observed during compression testing, which is where failure
occurs due to buckling of the sample and not of the ﬁbres. This is not an indication of the strength of the
material, but rather of the sample geometry and is to be avoided. This type of buckling is mainly an issue
with long and thin specimens, which is often the required geometry of the available standards describing an
open hole compression test, Treasurer (2006). As such, various ﬁxtures have been designed, which all serve
to constrain the sample suﬃciently to avoid macrobuckling. The ﬁxtures all vary in the requirements of the
sample size, loading type and implementation. One of the most commonly used ﬁxtures is described in the
ASTM D-6484M-04 (2015) standard, and is the one used for the minority of compression samples in this
study. The ﬁxture was designed by Boeing, and completely encases the specimen to ensure that it will not
fail by buckling. However, in order for the test samples to ﬁt inside this particular ﬁxture a certain thickness
have to be obtained. This can be diﬃcult with samples stemming from actual parts, as the geometry is ﬁxed
and cannot easily be changed. Various other test methods do exist for shorter samples with and without a
ﬁxture, such as NASA’s short block compression test, as reported by Chaterjee et al. (1993). Here, the tested
section is unsupported, and the method of load transfer is end-loading. Furthermore, the report also revealed
that the short block compression test, with an average coeﬃcient of variance of 5.1 %, demonstrated the
best reproducibility of the top three methods studied. Another advantage of this method is the requirement
of far less material than in the case of using the Boeing ﬁxture, where the samples are much larger. As
such, the short block method may be used eﬀectively in a materials development program when quantities of
material are limited and relative moduli measurements are suﬃcient to assess trends in the data. The main
problem associated with the short block test is end-crushing. This is where the ends of the specimen are
crushed leading to premature failure, and does not give an indication of true strength. Thus, in using this
test it is vital to examine the samples after testing to ensure this phenomenon has not occurred. Furthermore,
adequately thick specimens need to be used (Chaterjee et al., 1993).
The support of the sample is not the only important parameter when it comes to compression testing,
where also sample alignment, non-uniform loading and quality of the sample can inﬂuence the results. For
the latter controlling the moisture is essential in obtaining good results as several studies have indicated that
this can have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the strength both in terms of tensile and compression. The absorption
is due to the epoxy resin in the composite material, where a high moisture content leads to a degradation in
the structural performance of the composite materials. This is due to the adverse eﬀect of moisture on the
physical and chemical properties of the epoxy matrix, which also serves to decrease the the glass-transition
temperature, Tg, leading to a poor performance in high temperature environments. The change in moisture
content can induce swelling in the composite material, which leads to residual stresses in addition to the
thermal ones incurred during the curing procedure. As such, controlling the humidity and temperature of
the room and sample is essential when conducting mechanical tests (Aktas and Altan, 2002).
44
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 3
3.4. Countersinking
3.4.3
Constituent Content of Composite Materials
During the manufacturing of composites, the carbon ﬁbres are saturated with epoxy resin, which is a proce-
dure highly dependent on the orientation and structure of the ﬁbres. Voids can be formed throughout the
composite during manufacturing, and is hard to avoid. A high fraction of ﬁbre reinforcements, thus a low
void content, is desirable as this typically leads to better mechanical properties. For the aerospace industry
a requirement of maximum 1% void content is usually speciﬁed, although certain studies have found that 2.5
% is low enough (Liu et al., 2006). As such, determining the void content is needed to ensure that the correct
properties have been obtained during production, which can be done with constituents content of composite
materials based on ASTM D3171-15 (2004), also referred to as an acid digestion test.
The acid digestion test is a destructive test, which can be used for calculation of the reinforcement or
matrix content, both by weight and volume along with the percent void volume. This is done by physically
removing the matrix through acid digestion using an aqueous mixture of sulphuric acid and hydrogen peroxide
for an epoxy matrix, thus only leaving the ﬁbres. Archimedes principle (Nave, 2000) is used to determine the
density of the samples prior to testing, where the samples are weighed in air and then in water, as shown in
Equation 3.4.7.
Density of sample
Density of water (ﬂuid) =
weight of sample
weight of sample in water
(3.4.7)
The density of the ﬂuid, normally distilled water can change slightly with temperature.
Based on the tests, the ﬁbre volume, matrix volume and ﬁnally the void content can be calculated (ASTM
D3171-15, 2004):
Vf = (Mf)
Mi
· ρc
ρf
· 100
(3.4.8)
Vf is the volume percent of ﬁbre content, MF is the ﬁnal mass of the sample, Mi is the initial mass. ρc is the
density of the specimen and ρf is the density of the carbon ﬁbres, which has a theoretical value of 1.78 g/cm3.
The volume content of the epoxy matrix is calculated using a similar equation to that of the ﬁbres:
Vm = (Mi −Mf)
Mi
) · ρc
ρm
· 100
(3.4.9)
where Vm is the matrix content in volume percent and ρm is the density of the epoxy matrix with a theoretical
value of 1.21 g/cm3..
The void content in the composite can be calculated based on Equation 3.4.8 and 3.4.9:
Vv = 100 −(Vf + Vm)
(3.4.10)
The sulphuric acid used in the test exhibits aggressive oxidizing properties, and is thus capable of oxidizing
a variety of substance, such as an epoxy matrix. When the acid is added to the specimen, the digestion will
turn the solution dark, which is due to the rapid dehydration of the organic constituents in the epoxy matrix.
The addition of the hydrogen peroxide serves to enhance the oxidizing potential of the hot sulphuric acid,
which results in complete digestion of the matrix. As seen, the test is volatile, and toxic fumes are produced
during the chemical of the matrix, which can be a dangerous process (Carr, 2017).
The test is essential in verifying the ﬁbre and matrix content of the composite part to eliminate the er-
ror of voids resulting in delaminations. The favourable properties of the composite part is achieved through a
speciﬁc mixture of carbon ﬁbres and epoxy materials, however composites are inherently heterogeneous and
anisotropic materials and defects, such as voids, can easily arise from the fabrication process. This can be due
to a number of factors such as entrapment of air in resin rich areas during the construction of the pre-preg
sheets, moisture absorption during storage, inadequate pressure and temperature during curing, tearing in
the vacuum bag in the curing process and resin bleed out. The latter occurs during the curing process in the
autoclave, when the part is under vacuum, and have been observed at Terma. In terms of curing pressure
several studies have found that an increase in pressure leads to a reduction in void formation. Prior to the
curing process the parts are placed in a vacuum bagging system, which works by physically transporting the
45
==PAGE:66==
3.5. Conclusion on Literature Review
Chapter 3
voids out of the system through vacuum lines, which is then further assisted by the pressure in the autoclave.
(Liu and Chen, 2016).
If the matrix content is lower than expected, there will be a weaker interface strength due to inadequate
adhesion , which results in surface delaminations and lower strength. Furthermore, the mutual abrasion of the
carbon ﬁbres can result in fractures, also seen in the form of delaminations when milling and countersinking
(Saidpour and Tehranchi, 2001). Voids lead to a reduction in mechanical properties such as the interlaminar
shear strength, tensile and compressive strength, along with fatigue resistance. Studies have shown that the
shear strength is sensitive to a void concentration as low as 1%, while the compressive strength is eﬀected at
5% or higher. Thus, a low void content maximises the composites mechanical strengths, and ensures strength
consistency. The extent of the eﬀect is dependent upon a number of parameters, such as void content, void
distribution and void shape. This was conﬁrmed in a study by Zhuang and Talreja (2014) who investigated
the eﬀects of voids on delaminations in uni-directional composites. Here, it was found that the mechanical
properties such as interlaminar shear strength, fatigue resistance and compressive strength decreased with
increasing void content. Furthermore, manufacturing induced defects such as voids were found to inﬂuence
the structural integrity, which lead to unstable growth of delaminations. The results also illustrated that
the size, shape and position of the voids have speciﬁc inﬂuence, which might not be adequately captured by
the void content calculations. However, the test is still valid as it gives an indication of the severity of the
porosity of the part, and whether further tests are needed.
3.5
Conclusion on Literature Review
In this literature review several important aspects have been considered. Initially, an introduction to the
manufacturing process were given, which gave an overview of the diﬃculties associated with machining com-
posites due to their complex nature. This is mainly due to the diﬀerent orientations of each individual ply,
which makes up a composite part. Further complexity is added when each layer consist of a weave. These
observations were validated in the subsequent chapter on the machining of composites. Here, it was found
that cutting in diﬀerent ﬁbre orientations will lead to contrasting end results in terms of surface quality. This
chapter also gave an introduction to the diﬀerence between uni-directional and multi-directional laminates
when machining operations are performed. The latter has support from surrounding layers with diﬀerent
orientated ﬁbres, which leads to less extensive damage in some cases. Next, slot milling was considered, where
two machining methods were found to exist, namely conventional and climb. The former was found to present
better results in terms of surface quality, which was in contrast to the machining of metals. An explanation
for this could be due to the matrix and ﬁbres building up inside the ﬂute during climb milling, resulting in a
rougher surface. However, little literature existed on the actual mechanisms behind this, only that conven-
tional presented a better result, as conﬁrmed by experiments at Terma. The section also included a review
on delaminations and how to classify these. Several parameters were found to have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on
surface damage, such as machining parameters, tool geometry and wear. It was found that an increase in feed
rate lead to more delaminations, however in terms of speed a trend could not be established. Tool wear was
found to result in worse delaminations, as conﬁrmed by several studies, and is due to a bigger cutting edge
resulting in higher forces during machining, leading to more damage. Furthermore, more heat is generated,
which generally results in the deterioration of the surface. In terms of tool geometry, a conclusion was less
clear. Most studies found a neutral helix angle to achieve the best results, however with regards to the number
of ﬂutes several contradicting observations were made. Initial studies found a higher number of ﬂutes lead to
more delaminations, but more recent experiments showed that a lower amount of ﬂutes lead to a raise in the
feed per tooth, resulting in more delaminations. Next, the surface roughness of composite parts during slot
milling was investigated. Here, it was found that the roughness could be measured in two directions, namely
transverse and longitudinal, where early studies indicated that only the latter could be used for evaluating
machining parameters. This was due to the transverse direction only being aﬀected by tool geometry, and
measurements being made across several plies. However, in other experimental investigations, the transverse
direction were found to supply more knowledge with regards to the overall roughness due to it containing
information with regards to measuring across several ﬁbre orientations. Various experimental investigations
have found the transverse roughness values to be higher in comparison to the longitudinal direction, due to
46
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 3
3.5. Conclusion on Literature Review
stylus moving through several layers leading to a larger variety of roughness values. Conﬂictingly, a few studies
obtained similar or even lower values for the transverse direction, which could be due to the measurements in
the longitudinal direction being more aﬀected the tooling marks left after machining. In terms of the eﬀect of
machining parameters, the general agreement was that an increase in feed rate lead to a higher surface rough-
ness, and the spindle speed did not appear signiﬁcant. However, as seen with the delamination study, opposing
conclusions were reached by several studies. Although, an overall increase in feed rate lead to a lower surface
quality, some studies found the intermediate feed to be the optimum setting. This was due to a decrease in
temperature when changing from lowest to medium feed rate, resulting in a lower roughness. However, any
further increase meant a higher temperature due to the more prominent eﬀect of tool wear and a large chip
thickness. Contradicting results were also found for the eﬀect of tool wear on surface roughness values. Most
researchers agreed that an increase in tool wear lead to a lower surface quality, due to higher machining forces,
thus more heat being generated resulting in the deterioration of the part. However, some studies found that
the high amount of heat being produced lead to the matrix melting, resulting in a smoother surface. Lastly,
it was found that roughness measurements were not always the best way to evaluate the quality of machined
parts. The diﬃculties mainly arises when using a contact based approach, where the placement of the stylus
has a major impact on the results. This is due to dependency of ﬁbre orientation on the surface roughness.
Furthermore, selecting the correct roughness parameters are vital in describing the surface texture correctly,
where R10z was found to be the best when considering composite materials. As seen from the conclusion,
several aspects of machining composite parts are still unknown, and contrasting observations have been made.
As such, this is still an important and exciting area of research in terms of investigating the eﬀect of machining
parameters, tool geometry and wear on the surface quality. Additionally, more knowledge is needed on the
best methods of measuring the surface roughness in terms of the equipment type and direction of measurement.
The last section focused on countersinking, and the eﬀect of surface damage sustained prior to testing
on the mechanical strength of composites. Generally, only a small amount of research has been done on
the correlation between surface damage and a deterioration of the mechanical strength. Most studies have
concentrated on sub-surface damage such as cracks, holes and delaminations existing in between plies, which
was found to have a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the reduction in compressive and tensile strength. Contrastingly,
some experimental investigations have found that damaged sustained prior to testing could result in higher
strength, which is due to a reduction in the stress concentrations caused by the hole. The delamination factor
was introduced as a way of quantifying the extent of the surface damage, where a corrected and conventional
factor was found to present the best results. The latter is found by measuring the maximum diameter, so that
the surface damage is contained within the circle. The latter is a combination of the conventional factor and a
measurement of the damaged area. The section also included a study on the eﬀect of sample geometry on the
tensile and compression strength results. For the former, the length of the specimen should have no eﬀect on
the ﬁnal results, however maintaining the same width-to-diameter and thickness-to-diameter ratio was found
to be vital. This is due to the hole acting as a stress initiator and if the ratios are too small, the damage
occurring at the edge of the hole will join with the edge of the sample at a quick rate, resulting in early
determination of the test. The same conclusion could be reached for the samples used in the compression
tests in terms of maintaining the same ratios and the length being insigniﬁcant. Additionally, the short block
compression test was found to be valid for testing smaller specimens as long as buckling and end-crushing
was not observed during or after testing, as this indicates premature failure. As shown, the general agreement
in terms of strength and delaminations is that sub-surface damage leads to a reduction in strength, however
less conclusive results have been obtained for top ply delaminations. This highlights the need for further
testing in terms of investigating and documenting the eﬀect of surface damage on the strength of composite
materials. The last part of the section concentrated on calculating the void content of the composite material.
Previous studies revealed that the an insuﬃcient amount of either matrix or ﬁbre material can lead to a
signiﬁcant reduction in the strength of CFRPs. As such, performing this test is vital in conﬁrming that if a
reduction in strength is observed during testing, a high void content is not the reason.
47
==PAGE:68==
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 4
Experimental Investigation
4.1
Slot Milling
The aim of the slot milling experiment was to investigate the eﬀect of machining parameters on the surface
quality of composite parts based on delamination depth and surface roughness. This could then be used to
evaluate the optimum spindle speed, feed rate and tooling condition for each of the tools used. The experiment
was conducted using two diﬀerent milling tools, where the former is currently used in production, a four ﬂute
end mill from Unimerco with a diameter of 10 mm. The latter was a serrated eight-ﬂute end mill from Franken
Fiber-Cut with a diameter of 10 mm, which has yet to be used in the F-35 production. Although, Terma had
already done a series of tests with their current Unimerco tool before putting it into production, this study
presented a more systematic approach to determining the signiﬁcant machining parameters when dealing
with delamination and surface roughness. Additionally, the inﬂuence of tool wear on the quality was also
inspected. The experiment introduced a new tool from Franken, which had not been tested systematically and
only on parts outside the F-35 production. Furthermore, an unstable ﬁxture was used during the experiment
providing unreliable results. Thus, this test study tested the hypothesis whether a change of machining
parameters on the current tool would eliminate surface damage or if the new tool from Franken could provide
a better surface quality.
4.1.1
Experimental Set-up
A 3-axis YCM XV1020A CNC Machine was used in combination with the Unimerco and Franken end mills,
where a design of experiments (DOE) were created for each tool. The DOE for Unimerco was based on three
diﬀerent spindle speeds at 8000 RPM, 9000 RPM and 10000 RPM and three feed rates of 800 mm/min,
1000 mm/min and 1100 mm/min. The tool from Franken was used at three spindle speeds of 5000 RPM,
6000 RPM and 7000 RPM and three feed rates of 1000 mm/min, 1200 mm/min and 1500 mm/min. The
end mills were used in two conditions, namely new and worn to test the eﬀect of tool wear. The machining
operation used was slot milling, which meant the radial depth of cut was the diameter of the tool, and a
constant axial depth was maintained at full depth. Due to the nature of slot milling, one side of the slot was
done by conventional milling, and the other by climb, as illustrated in Figure 3.3.2 in Section 3.3. Terma had
already done extensive research in this area, and found conventional milling to present the best results. As
such, measurements were only obtained for this mode of milling. The samples were machined into a total of
11 workpieces. The delaminations were studied and measured using an optical microscope, and the surface
roughness using a Surtronic 25 precision tester along with an Alicona optical measurement system .
4.1.1.1
Materials and Specimen Preparation
The sample material used was a weaved carbon ﬁbre reinforced polymer (CFRP) composite comprising of
pre-pregnated layers, where the part had a total thickness of 9 mm. The laminate contained 39 layers, where
the top ply was coated with glass. The remaining layers consisted of carbon ﬁbres with an epoxy resin matrix,
with the parts illustrated in Figure 4.1.1. Further information is not available due to conﬁdentiality.
49
==PAGE:70==
4.1. Slot Milling
Chapter 4
Figure 4.1.1: Workpieces prior to ﬁnal machining procedure
The specimens were prepared by cutting the parts seen in Figure 4.1.1 into 11 pieces. This was done using a
bench-saw with a artiﬁcial diamond coated blade. Figure 4.1.2 illustrates where the cuts were made in order
to obtain the samples, where only three pieces have been included, as the remaining two are replicates and
therefore have similar dimensions. The cuts were made in places where the original piece started to curve
slightly, which could have resulted in an unstable set-up. This lead to the ﬁnal geometry of the samples,
which can also be found in Figure 4.1.2.

4.1.1.3
Machine
The machine used was a YCM XV1020A CNC Machine, as illustrated in Figure 4.1.5, where the speciﬁcations
can also be found.
Figure 4.1.5: YCM XV1020A CNC Machine along with its speciﬁcations
Coolant
The coolant Quakercool 3750 BFF was used, which is a boron and formaldehyde free cooling lubricant. It is
a high performance micro-emulsion and suited for operations where a high surface ﬁnish quality and high
lubrication is needed. The density of the coolant is 1026 kg m−3 at 15◦C with a viscosity of 91 mm2 s−1 at
40◦C.
4.1.2
Procedure
The cutting patterns used as guidelines for the slot milling experiment had been hand drawn prior to starting.
This was done to ensure a similar length was reached for each of the machining combinations and to verify
enough material existed to complete the entire machining procedure. The cutting patterns can be seen in
Figures 4.1.6, 4.1.7 and 4.1.8, where the workpieces 1, 2, 3, 9 and 10 had a duplicate as indicated by the
two numbers on the ﬁgures. The slots had a length of (220 – 260)mm, where the slight variation in length
was due to the geometry of the workpieces. The drawings were then transferred to SolidWorks, which is a
modelling design and engineering computer program and works in combination with the CNC machine. As
52
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 4
4.1. Slot Milling
such, the program was used to draw the ﬁnal milling instructions for the machine to follow based on the
initial hand drawn lay-out.
Figure 4.1.6: Cutting patterns for workpieces 1 and 4 along with 2 and 5
Figure 4.1.7: Cutting patterns for workpieces 3 and 6 together with 9 and 13
Figure 4.1.8: Cutting patterns for workpieces 10 and 11 along with workpiece number 18
The experimental procedure was initiated by cutting the workpieces seen in Figure 4.1.1 into 11 pieces. Next,
each piece was placed separately on a large aluminium sheet to determine the position of the clamps. The
workpiece was then removed and the holes for the clamps were drilled and threaded. The workpiece was
placed back on the sheet, clamped down and point zero was found, so the CNC machine milled the pattern in
the correct area. The piece was removed and the cutting pattern corresponding to the workpiece was milled
0.5 mm into an aluminium plate using a 10 mm standard three-ﬂute aluminium end mill. The cutting pattern
was done on the aluminium sheet prior to milling the workpiece to ensure the mill was not damaged by
contact with the sheet and to simulate the production conditions at Terma. The workpiece was then placed
over the tracks, clamped down and the same cutting pattern was made in the actual workpiece. The milling
was done at full axial depth of 9 mm, and the cutting mode was both conventional and climb milling due to
the nature of slot milling. The end mill was set to rotate clockwise, which lead to conventional on the left
and climb on the right, relative to the feed direction. The experimental set-up for slot milling is illustrated
in Figure 4.1.9, and the milling sequence in Figure 4.1.10. The last image shows an overview of the diﬀerent
cutting patterns.
53
==PAGE:74==
4.1. Slot Milling
Chapter 4
Figure 4.1.9: Experimental set-up for slot milling
Figure 4.1.10: Milling sequence with sample number 4 used as an example
The experiments were conducted following the matrix seen in Tables 4.1.3 and 4.1.4 for Franken Fiber-Cut
and Unimerco, respectively. The workpiece and track number are illustrated in Figures 4.1.6, 4.1.7 and 4.1.8.
Once a slot had been milled, the spindle speed and feed rate was changed accordingly and the procedure was
repeated with the new settings. Once all the slots had been milled with the new tool, the next part of the
experiment was done with a worn tool. Wear of the Franken Fiber-Cut tool was done by milling a dynamic
pattern in a spare composite piece with the tool used for the previous experiments, reaching a length of
1700 mm. This lead to a total of 4000 mm by combining the previously milled length with the one achieved
through dynamic milling. For the Unimerco tool, wear had been done at Terma prior to the experiment by
milling three aerospace components reaching a length of approximately (4000-4500)mm.
