4.1.4
Surface Roughness Measurement
The surface roughness was measured with a Surtronic 25 Taylor/Hobson precision tester, which consisted of
a skidded pick-up with 5 µm stylus tip attached to the machine as illustrated in Figure 4.1.11. The movable
pick up was placed on the surface, where only the skidded stylus tip was touching the surface. The roughness
values Ra and R10z were determined by the vertical displacement of the stylus produced by the surface
irregularities when the tip moved along the surface. Once the data had been captured by the tester, a transfer
was made to a scanning probe imaging software (SPIP) used for analysing the measurements.
Figure 4.1.11: Surtronic 25 Taylor/Hobson precision tester
56
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 4
4.1. Slot Milling
Prior to the surface roughness being measured the samples were cleaned with pressurised air to remove any
dirt particles, which might have obscured the readings. Next, the samples were placed in special clamp with
the precision tester in front of it, where care was taken to ensure the milled tracks were parallel to the surface.
The measurements were done for conventional milling in the longitudinal and transverse direction, shown in
Figure 4.1.12, where a cut-oﬀlength of 0.8 mm and a transverse length of 4.0 mm was selected for both types
of measurements. This is based upon ISO 4287:1998 (1998), which states that for Rz > 3 µm or Ra> 0.5 µm,
these parameters can used for a stylus tip radius of 5 µm with an attached skid. Furthermore, according to
ISO 3274:1996 (1996) when employing a skid, the radius should be 50 times greater than the nominal cut-oﬀ
wavelength, which was the case.
Figure 4.1.12: Longitudinal and transverse measurement direction on a milled CFRP sample
The measurements for both directions were taken at ﬁve diﬀerent locations on the track, where a roughness
proﬁle was generated for each one. Based on these proﬁles, Ra and R10z roughness values were extracted and
an average was calculated. R10z was chosen based on previous studies indicating this was most suitable for
describing composite materials. This is due to it being based on the average height of the ﬁve highest local
maximums plus the average height of the ﬁve lowest local minimums with a mean value calculated based on
these. Ra was chosen as this is still the most common parameter used for the aerospace industry, thus could
be used to evaluate if any of the measurements were above the acceptable limit of 3.2 µm (Li et al., 2017).
Figure 4.1.13 illustrates the set-up for the transverse direction.
Figure 4.1.13: Roughness measurements in the transverse direction
For the measurements in the longitudinal direction, the stylus head was turned 90◦, as illustrated in Figure
4.1.14 with the skid on the side of the stylus, which did not have an implication on the results.
57
==PAGE:78==
4.1. Slot Milling
Chapter 4
Figure 4.1.14: Roughness measurements in the transverse direction
Due to geometry of the samples, the precision tester had to be elevated in order to reach the milled slots at
the top. Extra precautions were made to ensure a stable set-up, as illustrated in Figures 4.1.13 and 4.1.14.
4.1.5
Optical Surface Roughness Measurement
The background and theory for contact-based surface roughness measurements were discussed in Section
3.3.1.2, where certain issues with this method was highlighted. Based on this, an optical approach for mea-
suring the surface texture was considered on two samples to illustrate the disadvantages and advantages of
the former technique. Furthermore, the experiment enabled measurements to be made on samples having
experienced two diﬀerent modes of milling, namely conventional and climb, samples 1 and 2 respectively,
where results obtained with the proﬁlometer are for conventional only. This was also due to diﬃculties in
obtaining samples where the inside of the track was available for measurements. The optical assessment was
an addition to the actual study on surface roughness and the purpose was to gain an insight into future
works with regards to analysing and quantifying the surface quality of CFRP composites.
The surface roughness was measured using an Alicona Inﬁnite Focus, which is a non-contact, optical, three-
dimensional measurement system based on focus variation. Using a beam splitting mirror, emerging white
light is inserted into the optical path of the system and focused on to the specimen using an objective. The
light is reﬂected into several directions, and all the rays are captured by a light sensitive sensor, producing
the ﬁnal image used for roughness measurements. The system is illustrated in Figure 4.1.15.
Figure 4.1.15: Alicona: Optical surface roughness measurement system
Prior to testing, the sample was cleaned and placed in a holder so that the milled side of the slot was placed
58
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 4
4.1. Slot Milling
upwards, enabling measurements to be done on the inside of the track. The sample was positioned under
the microscope, and the smallest magniﬁcation was utilised to get the sample in focus. Next, the focus was
manually changed by turning the wheel containing the diﬀerent objectives. A magniﬁcation of 20x was chosen
to capture the image using a total evaluation area of 2.60 mm by 1.46 mm for sample 1 and one of 0.714 mm
by 0.542 mm for sample 2 using a magniﬁcation of 20x. The diﬀerence in evaluation area is due to sample 1
being a combination of 3x4 separate images stitched together. As such, the resolution of each image was kept
the same. The image was then transferred to a scanning probe imaging software (SPIP) used to analyse the
measurements. For the optical system, the Sa and S10z areal roughness parameters were used, rather than
the Ra and R10z as seen for the stylus based measurements. The former takes a scan over an area rather
than a proﬁle line, and calculates the roughness based on this. It is similar to the R-parameters, however
this can be more useful for the inhomogeneous composite material due to the characterisation of a larger
surface area. A cut-oﬀwavelength, an l-ﬁlter, of 0.5 mm was selected based on ISO 25178-1:2016 (2016),
which determines the amount of low-spatial frequency components or waviness of the surface proﬁle to be
removed, leaving only the roughness. This wavelength can be diﬃcult to select when the waviness is complex,
therefore, it must be adjusted with reference to the real surface, and on a case-by-case basis. Based on this, a
preliminary ﬁltering was conducted to verify the selection. At a cut-oﬀwavelength of 0.8mm some waviness
could still be detected in the image, whereas only the roughness appeared to remain at a cut-oﬀof 0.5 mm,
thus this setting was used for both samples.
The initially step in SPIP, where roughness measurements were made, was a colour equalizing procedure to
highlight the ﬁner details of the image, as illustrated in Figures 4.1.16(a) and 4.1.17(a) for sample 1 and 2,
respectively. Next, a short wavelength ﬁlter was applied, an s-ﬁlter, to obtain the primary proﬁle from the
raw proﬁle using a length of 2 µm, where after a cut-oﬀwavelength of 500 µm was selected to acquire the
roughness proﬁle, shown in Figures 4.1.16(b) 4.1.17(a) for sample 1 and 2, respectively. The individual layers
were selected by drawing a rectangle around the area of interest, which had dimensions of 1.46 mm by 0.2 mm
as found in Figure 4.1.16(c) for sample 1. For sample 2 a rectangle with a size of 0.542 mm by 0.2 mm were
selected, see Figure 4.1.17(c).
Figure 4.1.16: Sample 1: (a) Equalised image (b) Filtered image using s-ﬁlter= 2 µm and l-ﬁlter = 500 µm (c)
Selection of individual plies
59
==PAGE:80==
4.1. Slot Milling
Chapter 4
Figure 4.1.17: Sample 2: (a) Equalised image (b) Filtered image using s-ﬁlter= 2 µm and l-ﬁlter = 500 µm (c)
Selection of individual plies
Seven diﬀerent measurements were taken for sample 1 and four for sample 2, as shown Figures 4.1.18(a)
4.1.18(b). This illustrates the placement of each ply on which the surface roughness was measurement, where
each layer can be seen to have a slightly diﬀerent morphology. Based on this the Sa and S10z roughness
parameters were calculated.
Figure 4.1.18: Identiﬁcation of plies for (a) Sample 1 and (b) Sample 2
4.1.6
Delamination Measurement
The extent of surface damage in terms of delamination type and frequency were recorded using a Light Optical
Microscope (LOM). The set-up consisted of a Leica LOM, along with a charged-coupled device camera, a
60
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 4
4.1. Slot Milling
light source, Photoshop imaging software, and an adjustable stage for the samples, as illustrated in Figure
4.1.19. The lenses in the LOM magniﬁed the sample and the image was captured by the CCD, and displayed
on the computer using the imagining software.
Figure 4.1.19: Delamination measurements using a light optical microscope (LOM)
The delamination frequency was measured by placing each sample on the stage and the number of delam-
inations were recorded by following the tracks using the adjustable pins on the stage to move the sample
horizontally and vertically. Here, a 0.63x lens was used in combination with a 1.0x objective. Next, a repre-
sentative image of the sample was captured using the same magniﬁcation. The procedure was repeated for
all the samples, where any surface damage was noted. The optical microscope was also used to capture the
layered structure of one sample, where a small piece from a milled slot had broken oﬀduring machining, thus
enabling the inside of the track to be captured.
61
==PAGE:82==
4.2. Open Hole Tensile and Compression Testing
Chapter 4
4.2
Open Hole Tensile and Compression Testing
The purpose of this test was to investigate the eﬀect of surface damage sustained during countersinking on the
mechanical properties of composite parts. Two of the major structural stresses an air-plane will experience
is tension and compression forces both during ﬂight and when stationary. As such, open hole tensile and
compression tests were conducted for a variety of samples.
ASTM D-5766M-02a (2007) was used as basis for the tensile tests, where the method, machine set-up
and parameters were utilised. To verify that the Instron machining centre could induce a higher enough load
for failure, a test run was done prior to testing. Here, it was found that Instron could provide satisfying
results, thus this standard was used for testing. However, due to the specimen geometry, which is further
discussed in Section 9.2.1, some of the ratios are outside the recommendations by the standard.
For the compression test, the initial preparation was based upon ASTM D-6484M-04 (2015), which makes
use of a special ﬁxture made by Boeing. To verify the capability of the Instron providing a high enough load
for adequate compression failure, a test run was done. However, due to the geometry of the compression
samples, extra pieces, so called spacers, were needed at the end for six of the specimens, where the remaining
two needed side spacers. The initial test found this did not prove adequate for gaining the correct results as
the spacers ended up being crushed ﬁrst, and then it was simply the instron crosshead against the ﬁxture.
Furthermore, this also lead to an uneven pressure being distributed. As such, it was decided to machine
the smaller pieces down to 50.8 mm, which meant that the short block compression test could be used as a
reference Chaterjee et al. (1993). The shortness, and thickness of the samples lead to more stability. Another
test run was done for one of the longer samples, however the results obtained were inconclusive due to the
geometry of piece, which consisted of a thick honeycomb core in one end, along with ﬁxture issues. This is
further discussed in Section 9.2.1. The ratios in terms of width-to-diameter and diameter-to-thickness were
still kept within the requirements of ASTM D-6484M-04 (2015) along with use of the correct machining
parameters and preparation requirements of the room. As such, the failure modes were still based upon this
standard. Prior to the investigations, artiﬁcial damage in the form of delaminations, was introduced to the
drilled holes through a countersinking operation. This was done by experienced personnel where inadequate
machining parameters were chosen in order to induce surface damage.
4.2.1
Experimental Set-Up
The Instron 5582 series universal testing system was used for both tests, where the tensile test was done
with conical grips to tightly fasten the specimens. Hereafter, the samples were pulled apart at a speed of
1.27 mm/min, and was complete when the correct failure mode had been achieved. The machine consisted
of a moveable head, moving at the speciﬁed speed at a constant rate, where the bottom clamp remained
stationary. For the compression test, two of the samples were placed in a ﬁxture with dimensions 304.8 mm
by 38.1 mm, and the parallel plates of the Instron machine was used to transfer the force into the support
ﬁxture by shear at a test speed of 1.27 mm/min. The remaining six samples were placed between the parallel
plates of the machine and end-loaded.
The F-35 part was divided into several pieces using a diamond cutting tool, which lead to a total of 18
samples for the tensile test. The samples all had approximately the same width with varying lengths and
degree of surface damage. For the compression test a total of 8 pieces were done. Prior to the test being
conducted, the specimens were kept in an oven at 120◦C for a minimum period of 168 h. This was done
to control the moisture content of the samples. The delaminations were studied and measured using the
processing software ImageJ, and the failure mode was based upon ASTM D-5766M-02a (2007) and ASTM
D-6484M-04 (2015).
4.2.1.1
Materials and Specimen Preparation
The CFRP composite part used for both tensile and compression testing consisted of an outer mould line
(OML) layer made with glass ﬁbres, 21 uni-directional (UD) carbon plies, a tracer layer and ﬁve woven
carbon plies along with SynCore core in the centre of the part with an epoxy resin matrix. The part was

manufactured by Terma personnel and the orientation and lay-up sequence of the ﬁbres used in the composite
is illustrated in Table 4.2.1. The piece had a nominal thickness of 5.43 mm and the overall shape is illustrated
in Figure 4.2.1.
Figure 4.2.1: F-35 part prior to being machined into test pieces

4.2. Open Hole Tensile and Compression Testing
Figure 4.2.2: Tensile samples
Figure 4.2.3: Compression samples
The hole preparation was the ﬁrst step in preparing the workpiece and was a two-fold procedure, which
initially involved drilling the actual hole. This was followed by countersinking, done with a specialised tool,
and delaminations were made deliberately by drilling at a slower speed and applying a higher pressure. As
the whole point of the test was to investigate the eﬀect of delaminations on the mechanical properties of the
pieces, special care was taken to make the surface damage as extensive as possible. Next, the workpieces were
65
==PAGE:86==
4.2. Open Hole Tensile and Compression Testing
Chapter 4
machined to their ﬁnal dimensions on a Benetec platesaw using a dimond saw-cutting wafer, as illustrated in
Figures 4.2.2 and 4.2.3. After machining, the workpieces were cleaned using ethanol. Prior to being placed in a
Memmert oven at 120 ◦C for a minimum period of 168 h, the dimensions of the workpieces and delaminations
were noted down, along with a sketch of the surface damage. The samples were placed in the oven to reduce
the moisture content, thus getting the samples as close to unity as possible.
Extra pieces, inserts, were made for the two long specimens to be placed in the Boeing ﬁxture, where
the main reason for these extra pieces were to centre the hole in the middle of the ﬁxture, and not so much
for support during testing. The remaining six samples were cut to a length of 50.8 mm with a width of 38 mm
to utilize the short block compression method (Chaterjee et al., 1993).
4.2.1.2
Machine
The machine used for tensile and compression testing was an Instron 5582 universal testing system, where
the speciﬁcations are displayed in Table 4.2.4
Table 4.2.4: Speciﬁcations for Instron 5582 test system

==PAGE:87==
Chapter 4
4.2. Open Hole Tensile and Compression Testing
4.2.1.3
Fixture
The Instrom 5582 testing system was used for both tests, however the ﬁxture set-up is not the same for the
two tests due to the nature of the force applied.
Tenstile Testing
The tensile test was conducted using two conical grips, which were made of hard steel. Figure 4.2.5 illustrates
the set-up, where the specimen was placed between the conical grips and tightly secured. The gauge section
of each specimen was not made smaller in comparison to the shoulders of the piece, as is often found in
tensile testing to avoid the eﬀect of the ﬁxture force. However, due to the strength of the material, and the
limited amount of test material, the ends of the test piece was simply placed in the machine. This decision
was further validated by the test run, which was done without end-grips, and resulted in an acceptable failure
mode.
Figure 4.2.5: Illustration of tensile ﬁxture
Compression Testing
The compression test consisted of two diﬀerent procedures, due to the varying sizes of the specimens. For
the two specimens with a length of 304.8 mm, a ﬁxture specially designed by Boeing was used, as illustrated
in Figure 4.2.6, which was checked for conformity with the engineering drawing. The sample was placed
inside the ﬁxture by loosening the bolts and inserting it from the top. The bolts were then tightly secured to
ensure the sample remained within the ﬁxture, as illustrated in Figure 4.2.6. The force introduced into the
ﬁxture was transmitted by means of friction into the test specimen, as the inside of the ﬁxture consisted of a
roughened surface.
Figure 4.2.6: Fixture for the compression test
For the six remaining samples the ﬁxture was not used, these were simply end-loaded.
4.2.2
Procedure
Test runs were done prior to the actual testing for both tensile and compression to verify the Instron machine.
A high enough load was provide for both test methods, however the trials also illustrated the need for a
67
==PAGE:88==
4.2. Open Hole Tensile and Compression Testing
Chapter 4
change of test procedure for six of the samples, as already discussed. The trial run for the tensile test was done
with a specimen of width 25.82 mm and thickness 4.13 mm, which was placed between two grips ensuring
complete parallelism. Next, the speciﬁc test program was run with a speed of 1.27 mm/min. The failure
occurred at a load of 45 kN. The compression trial run was conducted by placing a standing sample on the
ﬂat surface at the bottom, and slowly bringing the movable head down until the sample was softly clamped
between the two plates. Care was taken to ensure that the two horizontal surfaces of the test sample was
parallel in order to avoid an uneven load. Next, the test program was started using a speed of 1.27 mm/min,
where failure occurred at 40 kN. The ﬁxture illustrated in Figure 4.2.6 was also tested in combination with
a shorter sample and a spacer. This set-up did not work, as the spacer was slightly thinner than the part,
which resulted in the spacer failing, but not the actual part. As a result, the six samples with a total length of
less than 304.8 mm. were machined down to a length of 50.8 mm, and then placed in between the two plates,
as described with the ﬁrst compression test run.
4.2.2.1
Open Hole Tensile Test
The test specimens were removed from the oven and placed in a desiccator allowing for acclimatisation. Prior
to testing the temperature and humidity were recorded along with the width and thickness of the samples,
which were needed as input in the test program. Next, the ﬁrst sample was placed in between the steel
conical grips, as illustrated in Figure 4.2.7(a), where special care was placed on ensuring the two grips were
parallel prior to the experiment. This was done to avoid shearing of the samples. The grip area was kept to a
minimum assuring that failure occurred at the hole. To establish the same sized area being used to hold the
specimen, markings were placed on the conical grips to highlight how far into the grips the samples should
be placed, which also ensured them being centred, as seen in Figure 4.2.7(b). Once, all the checks had been
done and the sample was tightly fastened, the experiment was initiated with a test speed of 1.27 mm/min,
where the test was set to stop once a drop of 85% of maximum load was reached. Once failure had occurred
at the hole, the next sample was placed into the gripping ﬁxture and the procedure was repeated for all 17
samples.
Figure 4.2.7: a) Conical grips used for tensile test and b) sample between grips
4.2.2.2
Open Hole Compression Test
The ﬁrst of the two 304.8 mm test pieces was placed in the ﬁxture, and as the sample was slightly thinner
than the ﬁxture, a thin piece of composite was placed below, in the longitudinal direction, to lift the sample
to the correct height. The bolts on the ﬁxture was tighten to secure it, where after the extra pieces was
removed leaving only the sample in the ﬁxture. Next, the ﬁxture was placed in the Instron, as illustrated in
68
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 4
4.2. Open Hole Tensile and Compression Testing
Figures 4.2.8(a) and (b), and the test was initiated at a speed of 1.27 mm/min. The tests was set to stop at
100 ms of 90% of maximum load.
The remaining samples were all placed between the two parallel plates of the Instron machine, as shown in
Figure 4.2.8(c). Once the samples had been positioned correctly, the top movable plate was brought down
leading to a slight load being placed on the sample, which ensured no movement prior to test start. Extra
care was taken to ensure that the samples were completely parallel with the surface of the machine. Lastly,
the test was begun using a cross-head speed of 1.27 mm/min.
Figure 4.2.8: (a) and (b) illustrating the Boeing ﬁxture for compression testing along with the short block
specimen test in (c)
In both experiments no extensometer was attached to measure the strain, however the extension measured
by the crosshead movement was recorded. The main focus during the test was to measure and record the
ultimate failure load and tensile strength. The errors associated with this will be touched upon in discussion.
To verify the importance of placing the samples in the oven at 120 ◦C for a given time period, a mois-
ture control test was conducted. Here, three samples obtained from the F-35 part seen in Figure 4.2.1, were
placed in the oven for a period of 3 days and weighed at regular intervals throughout this time.
4.2.3
Delamination Measurement
In order to quantify the extent of the delaminations sustained prior to testing an image was taken of each
sample beforehand. As a postliminary step ImageJ was used in conjunction with these images to measure
and record the maximum delamination diameter and damaged area, as described in Section 3.4.1.1. The ﬁrst
step in the procedure was to calibrate the scale, which was done based on the nominal diameter of the hole.
Next, the maximum diameter was measured by using a line tool, and noted down for further calculations.
The damaged area was measured by outlining the delaminated zone using the hand-drawing tool provided
by ImageJ, where Figure 4.2.9 illustrates how both measurements were made.
69
==PAGE:90==
4.3. Constituent Content of Composite Materials
Chapter 4
Figure 4.2.9: ImageJ used to measure Dmax and the damaged area, Ad, left and right respectively
4.3
Constituent Content of Composite Materials
The purpose of the experiment was to determine the constituent content of the composite part used in
the mechanical tests. Test method I, procedure B found in ASTM D3171-15 (2004), was used to physically
remove the matrix by acid digestion leaving the reinforcement unaﬀected allowing for calculation of matrix
and reinforcement content.
4.3.1
Experimental Set-up
A combination of concentrated sulphuric acid (H2SO4), then 30% hydrogen peroxide (H2O2) was used to
digest the matrix of the CFRP composite part, where the remaining ﬁbres were subsequently cleaned with
a combination of water and acetone using a ﬁlter crucible containing the sample and a vacuum pump. The
samples and ﬁlter crucible were weighed prior to the digestion procedure, and then the crucibles containing
the samples were weighed afterwards, which enabled the calculation of the ﬁnal weight and volume of the
ﬁbres. The experiment and ﬁnal calculations were based on ASTM D3171-15 (2004) and Terma’s revised
version of the same document.
4.3.1.1
Workpiece
Five diﬀerent samples were obtained from the F-35 part used for the tensile and compression test, as seen
in Figure 4.2.1, using a Benetec platesaw with a diamond blade. Special care was taken to ensure the whole
part was represented by getting samples from various places. A total of ﬁve test pieces were cut, all with a
weight between (0.85-0.95) g, where the speciﬁcations can be found in Table 4.3.1. As speciﬁed by ASTM
D3171-15 (2004) all the samples are within the range of (0.5-1.5) g.
Table 4.3.1: Initial weight of samples
Name
Sample 1
Sample 2
Sample 3
Sample 4
Sample 5
Weight (g)
0.8500
0.9196
0.9583
0.8863
0.9457
4.3.1.2
Chemicals
The chemicals used in this experiment are Emsure concentrated sulphuric acid 95% and 30% hydrogen
peroxide.
70
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 4
4.3. Constituent Content of Composite Materials
4.3.2
Procedure
The ﬁve samples were machined from diﬀerent places on the F-35 part using a 5000 platesaw with a diamond
blade. The samples were then placed in a Memmert oven for a minimum period of 72 h at a temperature of
200 ◦C removing any unwanted moisture. After the speciﬁed time period the samples were removed from the
oven, and their density calculated. This was done as illustrated in Figure 4.3.1, where the samples were ﬁrst
weighed in air, (a), and then in water, (b), and the density was then calculated based on the two values. Next,
the samples were placed in a beaker, which contained a ﬁlter at the bottom, referred to as a ﬁlter crucible,
where each ﬁlter crucibles had been weighed prior to this, which was used as a reference for calculating the
ﬁnal weight of the ﬁbres. The crucibles containing the samples were then placed in another glass beaker to
keep the ﬁlters and samples from being contaminated. The whole arrangement was then placed in the oven
for another 105 minutes at 95 ◦C. This was done to remove any remaining moisture on the samples.
Figure 4.3.1: Weighing samples in air and water to calculate the density
Once the samples were out of the oven, they were placed in individual, large, glass beakers, and then moved
to a fume cupboard, shown in Figure 4.3.2(a). A measurement of 70 mL sulphuric acid was added to each of
the beakers, see Figure 4.3.2(b), which are then placed on a hotplate, where a lid was placed on top to keep
the vapour inside of the glass and to minimize dangerous fumes escaping, see Figure 4.3.2(c). Once all the
samples were on the hotplate, it was turned to maximum. When the matrix started to dissolve, the mixture
was carefully stirred in case any ﬁbres were packed together. The matrix was completely dissolved after
approximately 20 min, which was further veriﬁed by the colour of the mixture remaining constant, shown in
Figure 4.3.2(d).
Figure 4.3.2: (a) Samples in fume cupboard, (b) Sulphuric acid added, (c) Samples placed on hotplate and
(d) Dissolved matrix
71
==PAGE:92==
4.3. Constituent Content of Composite Materials
Chapter 4
Prior to the samples being removed from the hotplate, hydrogen peroxide was prepared by pouring it into a
smaller beaker. All the glass beakers containing the samples were then removed from the hotplate, and left
for 7 min to cool, as seen in Figure 4.3.3(a). This was done to avoid any dangerous mixing of the sulphuric
acid and hydrogen peroxide. Small amounts of the hydrogen peroxide was added to the glass beakers one
at a time using a pipette as shown in 4.3.3(b), and stirred with a glass rod continuously throughout. The
correct amount of peroxide had been added once the solution was completely clear, indicated in 4.3.3(c). The
beakers containing the solution were placed back on the hotplate to ensure complete mixing for 5 min.
Figure 4.3.3: (a) samples removed from hotplate (b) Initial amount of hydrogen peroxide is added (c) Addition
of the ﬁnal hydrogen peroxide
The samples were left to cool for 20 min before being cleaned with de-mineralised water. This was done by
tightly securing the ﬁlter crucible on to a large conical ﬂask, which was attached to a vacuum pump, and
then slowly pouring the mixture, which contained the ﬁbres, into the ﬁlter crucible, as illustrated in Figures
4.3.4(a) and 4.3.4(b). The mixture was left to slowly run through the ﬁlter into the ﬂask without turning on
the pump, as shown in Figure 4.3.4(c). When all the ﬁbres had been transferred into the crucible, the pump
was turned on to remove any of the remaining chemical solution. Next, 300 mL water was carefully poured
into it, where after the pump was turned on to rinse the ﬁbres. This was done three times for each of the
samples, and is illustrated in Figure 4.3.4(d).
Figure 4.3.4: (a) Conical ﬂask with attached pump (b) Fibre mixture poured into crucible (c) Filtering of
mixture (d) Fibres cleaned with de-mineralised water
72
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 4
4.3. Constituent Content of Composite Materials
The beakers were then carefully cleaned on the outside before being placed back on the conical ﬂask to do
one rinse with 60 mL of acetone. Next, the samples were placed in the oven still inside the ﬁlter crucibles
for a period of 17 h at a temperature of 95 ◦C, where after they were removed and placed in a desiccator
to acclimatise. The weight of the ﬁbres and the ﬁlter crucible were then registered, where the previously
recorded weight of each empty crucible allowed the weight of the ﬁbres to be calculated.

Chapter 5
Slot Milling Results
This chapter presents the results obtained for the slot milling experiment. Initially, a brief introduction will
be given to the overall weaved structure of the sample, enabling a better understanding of the measurements
made for surface roughness and the study of delaminations. Next, the focus will be on the surface roughness
measurements acquired for Franken Fiber-Cut and Unimerco. This is followed by a visual representation of
the delamination ﬁndings in the form of light optical microscope images, and a graphical representation of
the frequency at which they occurred. As discussed in the methodology section the measurements and images
were obtained for conventional slot milling only.
5.1
Structure of Samples
Figure 5.1.1 illustrates the layered structure of the weaved composite material used for slot milling. This was
done as a preliminary study to understand the lay-up before continuing with the roughness and delamination
measurements. The slot was milled with a worn Franken Fiber-Cut end mill at a speed of 5000 RPM in
combination with a 1500 mm/min feed rate. The individual layers of the weft and warp yarns can clearly
be seen as the separation between the lighter, long grey areas in the longitudinal direction and the black
areas. The former is the cross-sectional view of the yarn, whereas the darker areas show the vertical cut of
the ﬁbres, thus seen from the end. Using the image on the right, this can be further narrowed down as being
the small, circular holes seen in the darker layers, where the remaining structure is the matrix.
Figure 5.1.1: Optical microscopy structure of slot milled composite material at 16x magniﬁcation
Figure 5.1.1 also reveal that the layers are not to be completely symmetrical in terms of the woven structure,
75
==PAGE:96==
5.2. Surface Roughness
Chapter 5
as seen by the irregular patterns of the lighter areas. There is a slight diﬀerence in the thickness of the
layers, along with varying distances between each individual ply. There are clear signs of matrix cracking, as
identiﬁed by the gaps in the darker layered structures. Furthermore, signs of delaminations, where the yarn
have separated from the matrix can also be seen. At the bottom of the image, the tracer layer can be found
and underneath are the sacriﬁcial layers, as introduced in Section 3.1.3.1.
5.2
Surface Roughness
In this section the surface roughness results for the Franken Fiber-Cut and Unimerco end mill tools will be
presented. The ﬁrst part of each section will focus on a standard roughness proﬁle obtained for each direction,
which will give a general understanding of the subsequent ﬁgures. These are a graphical representation of the
calculated roughness parameters determined from the proﬁles. Based on these results, the best machining
conditions are found, and their roughness proﬁles are examined. This can be used to visual describe the
diﬀerences seen for each machining combination. Lastly, a comparison between the tools will be drawn.
Appendix A contains the remaining roughness proﬁles obtained for each combination of feed rate and spindle
speed.
Based on the experimental investigation, ﬁve surface roughness proﬁles, as described in Section 4.1.4, were
obtained for each combination of spindle speed and feed rate for the Franken Fiber-Cut and Unimerco tool.
Using these proﬁles, the surface describing parameters, namely Ra and R10z, could be evaluated and the
average and standard deviation calculated. The values were then plotted as a function of feed rate for each
spindle speed. Although, previous studies showed that R10z is the better parameter for representing roughness
in composite materials both Ra and R10z have been included, as the former is still the preferred option for
many in the aerospace industry, thus allow for better comparison. Here, the acceptable value for roughness is
Ra ≤3.2 µm (Li et al., 2017). However, as R10z displays the largest range in roughness, the optimum tooling
conditions are based on this parameter only.
5.2.1
Franken Fiber-Cut
Figure 5.2.1 illustrates a representative roughness proﬁle for the new tool at 1000 mm/min with a speed
of 7000 RPM for the longitudinal and transverse direction. The proﬁles for each combination of machining
parameters did diﬀer in terms of the roughness, thus the frequency and size the of peaks and valleys, as to
be expected. However, the general proﬁle remained the same, and serves as a good basis for understanding
the measurements. The transverse direction is observed to illustrate are more varied and uneven proﬁle in
comparison to the longitudinal measurements, although both direction presents a similar range of values.
Furthermore, the valleys and peaks are more homogeneously spread out across the entire evaluation length
for the transverse measurements. The peaks in the longitudinal direction are initially smaller, and there is
less variation across the evaluation length. However, deeper valleys towards the end of the measurement can
be observed. Where the transverse direction displayed a similar amount of peaks and valleys, the longitudinal
direction only show the latter feature, and no high peaks are observed.
Figure 5.2.1: Representative roughness proﬁles for a new tool at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min at 7000 RPM
76
==PAGE:97==

5.2. Surface Roughness
Figure 5.2.2 shows the roughness proﬁles obtained for the worn tool at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min combined
with a speed of 7000 RPM. Here, deeper valleys and peaks can be seen for the worn tool in the transverse
direction compared to the longitudinal measurements as observed for the new tool. The pattern for the former
is more irregular with the valleys being dominant. The peaks and valleys are more pronounced for the worn
tool in comparison to the new tool for both direction, where this is most evident for the transverse direction.
Figure 5.2.2: Representative roughness proﬁles for a used tool at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min at 7000 RPM
Figure 5.2.3 illustrates the evolution of the surface roughness as a function of the feed rate at diﬀerent spindle
speeds for the new tool. From this, it can be realised that an increase in feed rate leads to a higher surface
roughness, which is the case for both Ra and R10z. An increase in spindle speed leads to a lower roughness
when assessing the R10z value at the highest feed rate, and for Ra at 1200 mm/min. For the other feed
rates, the speeds all present similar values, which is seen for both Ra and R10z values. For the worn tooling
condition the surface roughness increases with feed rate for all three spindle speeds, as illustrated in Figure
5.2.4. Furthermore, an increase in spindle speed from 5000 RPM to 6000 RPM leads to a lower surface
roughness for all three feed rates, where no obvious trend can be seen with an increase from 6000 RPM to
7000 RPM, as this varies with regards to feed rate and roughness parameter. As such, there appear to be no
clear trend in terms of spindle speed, although a lower roughness is present for the two highest spindle speeds
in comparison to the lowest. Comparing the new and worn tool it can be seen that the roughness values are
higher for the latter except in the case of 6000 RPM at the lowest feed rate, however the two values are very
close together. Generally, the observed diﬀerence between the new and worn tool is reasonably small.

the new and worn tooling condition. This is also found to be the case for the transverse direction, as seen in
Figures 5.2.5, 5.2.6, 5.2.12 and 5.2.13. Here, the roughness parameters Ra and R10z are highest for the new
Unimerco tool. Only when comparing the lowest feed rate at the smallest spindle speed for each worn tool
in the transverse direction does Unimerco display the lowest roughness values for both Ra and R10z. The
general trend for the tools are that an increase in feed rate leads to an increase in surface roughness in both
the longitudinal and transverse direction for a new and worn tool. However, for Unimerco the intermediate
feed rate presents the lowest surface roughness for the new tool.
5.3
Optical Surface Roughness
In addition to the roughness measurements obtained with a stylus based method, an experiment was con-
ducted using an optical approach for two samples using a diﬀerent evaluation area. The main purpose of the
experiment was to illustrate how optically capturing the roughness could potentially be used to obtain more
accurate results. As such, it was also an opportunity to vary diﬀerent parameters for each sample to acquire
knowledge for future studies. The same magniﬁcation and roughness ﬁlters were utilised for both samples,
where the diﬀerence stem from the evaluation area and milling mode. For sample 1, a larger area was used
for the measurements, where conventional milling had been done on the measured surface. In contrast, a
smaller area was utilised for sample 2, where climb milling had been carried out. Sample 1 consisted of 3x4
images taken at 20x, whereas sample 2 was only one image at the same magniﬁcation. Another reason for
including a sample done with climb milling was due to the diﬃculties in obtaining samples where the inside
of the track were available for measurements.
This section will show the obtained roughness measurements for individual plies on Sample 1, which was
milled with a worn Unimerco tool at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min at a speed of 9000 RPM and Sample 2,
which was done using Franken Fiber-Cut at 1500 mm/min with a speed of 5000 RPM. Sample 2 was done by
climb milling, where sample 1 was done using conventional milling as with the rest of the samples in this study.
Figures 5.3.1, 5.3.2, 5.3.3 and 5.3.4 illustrate the obtained 3D images using the Alicona optical measurement
system. The images are taken prior to any ﬁlters being applied, and is thus the raw proﬁle of the surfaces.
The ﬁrst two images were taken with a magniﬁcation of 5x, in order to get an overview of the samples,
whereas the latter presents the surface texture at a magniﬁcation of 20x. In Figures 5.3.1 and 5.3.2, the layers
can clear be distinguished, and more pits and valleys appear for sample 2. This indicates that sample 2 has
experienced a higher amount of surface damage due to machining, which is further conﬁrmed when viewing
Figures 5.3.3 and 5.3.4.

5.4
Delamination
The damaged caused by slot milling observed as surface delaminations, are presented using a qualitative
method in the form of images taken with a light optical microscope. This is followed by a quantiﬁcation of
the damage, which is presented graphically to illustrate the delamination frequency for the worn tool.
5.4.1
Franken Fiber-Cut
Figures 5.4.1, 5.4.2 and 5.4.3 provides a comparison of delaminations for a spindle speed of 5000 RPM with
increasing feed rate for a new and worn Franken milling tool. All the images are taken at the exit side, as no
delaminations or other damages were observed at the entrance. Generally, at the lowest speed there appear
to be no signiﬁcant diﬀerence when increasing the feed rate, which is the case for both new and worn tools.
However, a slight increase in ﬁbre protrusion can be observed at both 1200 mm/min and 1500 mm/min, see
Figure 5.4.2 and 5.4.3, respectively, when comparing the new and worn tool, where the latter presents more
delaminations. The most prominent delaminations occur at 1200 mm/min for both the new and worn tooling
condition, albeit not a huge diﬀerence is observed and the eﬀect is minimal. All delaminations were identiﬁed
as type II.

Changing the spindle speed, whilst keeping the feed rate ﬁxed is another interesting combination, which is

insigniﬁcant amount of delaminations, but at a speed of 10000 RPM large amounts of type II delaminations
are observed in the form of long, thick, and highly frequent uncut ﬁbres. A signiﬁcant amount of edge damage
is observed in the form of uncut ﬁbres for all three speeds at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min, however most
appear at the lowest speed of 8000 RPM. The length of the ﬁbres are observed to increase with increasing
speeds, but in terms of the frequency, there is not an obvious trend as 9000 RPM is disolaying the lowest.

delaminations, however at 1100 RPM the opposite was observed.
A common observation across both tools, wear conditions and machining parameters is that the protruding
ﬁbres are all in the same direction. The delaminations and other damages are only observed on the exit
side of the slots, where any signiﬁcant amount of damage was only obtained for the worn tool, whereas the
new end mills from both manufacturer produced a high surface quality resulting in very little delamination
and protruding ﬁbres. For the worn tools, Franken Fiber-Cut revealed to have a much lower frequency of
delaminations in comparison to Unimerco .
5.4.3
Delamination Frequency
To verify the qualitative observations made in Section 5.4, a study of the delamination frequency was done,
thus enabling a better comparison basis for the two tools. Figures 5.4.19 and 5.4.20 illustrates the Type II
delamination frequency for a worn Unimerco and Franken Fiber-Cut, respectively. Only results for the worn
tools have been obtained, as the new end mills did not display any signiﬁcant delamination, as seen in Section
5.4. The frequency count was done by tallying the occurrence of Type II delaminations observed during
conventional milling, based on Figure 3.3.4. There was a mixture of single protruding ﬁbres, and bundles,
where the latter has been counted as one protruding ﬁbre.
Figure 5.4.19 illustrates a clear correlation between an increase in feed rate and a higher delamination
frequency for the Unimerco tool. This can be observed for all three spindle speeds and is in agreement with
the observation made previously. The most extensive amount of damage is found for the lowest spindle speed
at the two highest feed rates. At the lowest feed rate of 800 mm/min an increase in speed leads to more
delaminations, however at the other feed rates the intermediate spindle speed of 9000 RPM displays the
lowest count of protruding ﬁbres.
Figure 5.4.20 demonstrates the linear relationship between an increase in feed rate leading to a raise in
delamination frequency for a worn Franken Fiber-Cut. This is observed for all three spindle speeds. Further-
more, the lowest spindle speed displays the least amount of protruding ﬁbres at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min
and 1500 mm/min. At the feed rate of 1200 mm/min an equal amount of ﬁbres can be observed for the two
lowest spindle speeds. Generally, when comparing the diﬀerent spindle speeds at each feed rate, only a small
diﬀerence can be observed with a small number of delaminations separating them.
