Chapter 6
Discussion of Slot Milling Results
The focus in this chapter is on analysing the obtained results through the slot milling experiment, and is
divided into two main sections. The ﬁrst section is a discussion of the surface roughness measurements
and mainly on the results obtained using the contact-based approach, where the last part of the section
is an evaluation of the optical roughness measurements. A statistical analysis was also made based on the
obtained roughness results with the stylus method, however this is presented in the subsequent chapter. .
The evaluation of the surface roughness is mainly based upon the R10z parameter unless stated otherwise.
This is because this value gives an better indication of the quality, as depicted in the theory section. Ra
is mainly used to indicate if there are any roughness values above the accepted value of 3.2 µm as set by
the aerospace industry (Li et al., 2017). The second part is an analysis of the delaminations based on the
microscopic images and the graphical quantiﬁcation. The analysis is concluded by summarising the optimum
tooling condition for Unimerco and Franken Fiber-Cut based on these analyses.
6.1
Surface Roughness
The Franken Fiber-Cut tool showed a clear increase in surface roughness with increasing feed rate for both
tooling conditions in the longitudinal direction, as seen in Figures 5.2.3 and 5.2.4. This is in agreement with
previous ﬁndings by Davim and Reis (2005) and Li et al. (2017), who independently studied the slot milling of
CFRP composites. An increase in feed rate leads to a larger chip thickness, and results in the chips building up
near the cutting edge of the tool. This in turn creates friction between the surface and tool edge, resulting in a
higher surface roughness. Furthermore, the larger chip thickness also results in higher cutting forces, leading to
severe impact on the cutting edge. This combined results in matrix smearing, ﬁbre pull-outs and cavities being
formed. The ﬁgures also illustrate that in some cases a higher spindle speed leads to better surface quality, as
the lowest speed of 5000 RPM displayed the largest R10z value for the worn tool, and at the highest feed rate
for the new tool However, for the new tool this trend is not pronounced at the lowest feed rates, where the
roughness values of the three speeds overlap. The contradicting observations in regard to changing the spindle
speed were also made in a study by Wang et al. (2015) who found the intermediate speed to be the best in
terms of obtaining a good surface quality. This is due to low spindle speeds producing less heat during milling,
which leads to diﬃculties cutting the matrix, resulting in a lower surface quality, however a high spindle
speed can result in excessive heat, which leads to matrix softening. This in turn can mean more protruding
ﬁbres and a higher roughness. Furthermore, even though the stylus is placed parallel to the ﬁbre direction it
might still move across a few plies or pass through several ﬁbre orientations due to the woven structure of
the composite. This is further validated in Figure 5.1.1, where a structural view of the woven composite can
be seen. Here, the individual layers are thin and close together, which conﬁrms that moving the stylus along
one ply can be diﬃcult, however not without merit as the diameter of the stylus is only 5 µm. The worn tool
displayed a slightly higher surface roughness in comparison to the new tool, and can be due to an increase in
the size of the chips generated, which in combination with the larger cutting radius of the worn tool results in
a higher surface roughness. This change in geometry will also result in higher cutting forces being generated
due to the larger cutting edge of the tool. This will lead to an increase in temperature between the machined
surface and tool, resulting in the same defects observed due to a higher feed rate (Can, 2017),(Sheikh-Ahmad
97
==PAGE:118==
6.1. Surface Roughness
Chapter 6
et al., 2017). This is further exempliﬁed in Figures 5.2.1 and 5.2.2, displaying longitudinal roughness measure-
ment for a new and worn tool, respectively. Here, similar trends could be observed in terms of the roughness
pattern, where only slightly deeper valleys could be observed for the worn tool. These could be due to the
machining damaged caused by the tool, which is exacerbated for the worn tool due to the reasons given above.
For the roughness measurements in the transverse direction for Franken Fiber-Cut, a correlation was observed
between feed rate and surface roughness, as displayed in Figures 5.2.5 and 5.2.6. Here, an increase in roughness
was found when increasing from a feed rate of 1000 mm/min to 1500 mm/min for all three spindle speeds at
both tooling conditions. This is the same as seen for the longitudinal direction, which gives a clear indication
of the transverse direction not only being aﬀected by the tool geometry, but also by machining parameters,
as indicated in recent studies by El-Hofy et al. (2011), Can (2017),Boudelier et al. (2011). For the Franken
Fiber-Cut tool, the spindle speed did not seem to follow a clear trend in the transverse direction. This is
in agreement with the results obtained for the longitudinal direction, and the same reasons are applicable
here. Additionally, further variation can stem from measuring across several plies in the laminate, where
each layer not only contained ﬁbres at 90◦to each other, but each layer was also orientated at an angle
with respect to the layer below. Similar roughness values were obtained for the new tool in the longitudinal
and transverse direction, and in some cases higher values were found for the former. This can be due to the
eﬀect of tool geometry, where the roughness in the longitudinal direction is not only determined by milling
parameters, but also by striation marks, as found by El-Hofy et al. (2018). The transverse measurements,
on the other hand, are determined by machining conditions and stacking sequence. These observations are
conﬁrmed by comparing the two graphs in Figure 5.2.1, which illustrates four standard roughness proﬁles.
Here, sharper and deeper peaks can be observed for the longitudinal measurements towards the end of the
evaluation length. This indicates a rougher surface in comparison to the transverse measurements and could
represent the tool marks left after machining, which are more prominent in this direction. The transverse
direction is also displaying peaks, and more valleys, however these appear continuously throughout the entire
measurements, and could indicate when a new layer is being measured upon. It should be noted that, ideally
measurements made in the longitudinal direction should be done in one layer, but placing the stylus in the
same place on each track was near impossible, which will have lead to some inconsistencies. For the worn
tool, a higher roughness can be observed for the transverse measurements in comparison the longitudinal
direction. This could be due to a change of tool geometry leading to more blunt serrations on the end mill,
resulting in less striations in the longitudinal direction. Furthermore, certain ﬁbre orientations might have
responded more severely to the tool wear, which will have been captured by the transverse direction, due
to the measurements being made across several ﬁbre orientations. This is further illustrated in Figure 5.2.2,
where the roughness proﬁles for a feed of 1000 mm/min at 7000 RPM are illustrated for the transverse and
longitudinal direction. Deeper and sharper valleys are clearly observed for the transverse direction, conﬁrming
the eﬀect of the worn tool being more pronounced for certain ﬁbre orientations, as these features only appear
in speciﬁc locations towards the end of the measurements.
The longitudinal roughness values for the Unimerco tool as shown in Figures 5.2.10 and 5.2.11, illustrate that
an increase in surface roughness can be observed when comparing the lowest and highest feed rate (Koplev
et al., 1983), (Caggiano, 2018). This is the case for both the new and worn tool. An increase in feed rate
leads to larger chips being generated during machining, which causes a higher surface roughness. There is a
slight decrease in surface roughness as the feed rate increases from 800 mm/min to 1000 mm/min for the two
higest spindle speeds, which is also seen for the transverse direction, albeit much more evident. The lowering
of the surface roughness due to an increase in feed rate was also observed in a study by El-Hofy et al. (2018)
as discussed in Section 3.3.1.2. At the lowest feed rate, excessive heat can be generated during milling causing
the matrix to disintegrate enough for ﬁbres to escape, leading to a higher surface roughness. Increasing the
feed rate to 1000 mm/min appears to have reduced the thermal damage, leading to a lower surface roughness,
which could be due to a decrease in surface temperature. Increasing the feed rate further resulted in a higher
surface roughness, where an explanation for this can be found in higher temperatures along with larger chips
being generated. This results in cavities and ﬁbre pull-outs being the dominating factors (Li et al., 2017),
(Koplev et al., 1983). In support of this theory, the observation was made for the two highest spindle speeds,
indicating that the material melted due to the extra heat generated by larger speeds relative to the feed
rate. At a speed of 5000 RPM, the trend is that an increase in feed rate leads to a higher surface roughness,
98
==PAGE:119==
Chapter 6
6.1. Surface Roughness
due to the reasons given above. For the new tool an increase in spindle speed at the two lowest feed rates
likewise lead to a lower surface roughness, which can also be explained in terms of the average chip thickness.
At higher speeds, smaller chips are formed, which leads to less damage on the surface (Koplev et al., 1983),
(Khairusshima et al., 2012), Can (2017). For the worn tool the trend for spindle speed is less pronounced,
where at the highest feed rate a spindle speed of 10000 RPM also resulted in the highest roughness, which
can be explained by thermal damage induced by high speeds, which results in the matrix being unable to
contain the ﬁbres causing them to protrude. However, milling with the lowest spindle speed obtained very
similar results, which can be explained in terms of a large average chip thickness, causing excessive heat to
generate between the tool and machined surface. This is further exacerbated by a worn tool being used as
found by El-Hofy et al. (2018). The inconsistent trend in spindle speed and the eﬀect on surface roughness
was also observed for Franken Fiber-Cut for a new and worn tool in both measurement directions, and is in
agreement with the observation made by Wang et al. (2015), as explained previously. The diﬀerence between
the new and worn tool in terms of surface roughness is very slight in the longitudinal direction, but the
general trend is that an increase in wear leads to a lower surface quality due to a higher cutting radius leading
to larger chips being produced (Can, 2017), Sheikh-Ahmad et al. (2017). However, the higher temperatures
generated with a worn tool also leads to the degradation of the epoxy matrix, which results in smearing
(Delahaigue et al., 2017). This in turn can lead to a smoother surface, thus a lower surface roughness. Figures
5.2.8 and 5.2.9 illustrating a standard roughness proﬁle for the longitudinal direction for a new and worn
tool, respectively, can be used to further evaluate this observation. Here, very similar roughness proﬁles can
be observed, where only slightly sharper peaks and valleys can be seen for the worn tool. However, both
patterns are quite regular indicating the eﬀect of tool wear is minimal, and the measurements appear to have
been kept within one or two plies.
For the new tool in the transverse direction, the general trend when comparing the highest and lowest
feed rate is that an increase leads to a higher roughness. However, the negative gradient found in the graph
between 800 mm/min and 1000 mm/min for the new tool at speeds of 9000 RPM and 10000 RPM, Figure
5.2.12, indicates that for Unimerco an optimum tooling condition can be achieved by selecting the intermedi-
ate feed rate of 1000 mm/min. The decrease in roughness when increasing the feed rate from 800 mm/min
to 1000 mm/min can also be seen for the longitudinal direction, which signiﬁes that the measurements made
in the transverse direction does not simply depend on tool geometry, but also on machining parameters. The
decrease in feed rate leading to a lower surface roughness can be explained in terms of thermal damage, as
discussed for the longitudinal direction (El-Hofy et al., 2018). In the case of Unimerco, the lowest feed rate
for the new tool appear to have generated enough heat during machining for protruding ﬁbres to escape
leading to a higher roughness in comparison to the mid-level feed rate. An increase in roughness is also
observed when changing from this mid-level feed to the maximum feed rate, which is according to the general
experimental observations. This can be explained by a large chip thickness being produced at higher feed
rates, as seen in Equation 3.3.1, in combination with higher thermal stresses resulting in surface degradation.
This surface degradation can often be seen in the form of matrix burning, where the matrix is essential
in binding the ﬁbres together. As such, the softer matrix will allow the ﬁbres to escape, causing a rougher
surface (El-Hofy et al., 2011). At the intermediate feed rate, the lower surface roughness could be due to
the cutting temperature decreasing, resulting in less ﬁbre pull outs and cavities, leading to a higher surface
quality. Combining this with a medium chip thickness and a reduction in contact time between the material
and tool has resulted in the smallest surface roughness. In contrast to the two highest spindle speeds, for the
lowest speed an increase in roughness was observed across all three feed rates, however the diﬀerence is most
pronounced when changing from 1000 mm/min to 1100 mm/min, and here only a slight increase between
the two lowest feed rates can be observed. The diﬀerence between the two highest speeds and the lowest
in terms of feed rate, could be due to a longer contact time at 5000 RPM, resulting in a steady increase in
surface roughness. So although more heat is generated by a higher spindle speed, a low contact time seems
to have been more eﬀective in reducing surface roughness (El-Hofy et al., 2018). The worn Unimerco tool
presented more irregular data in terms of spindle speed, however for the feed rate an increase general lead
to a higher surface roughness. For the two lowest speeds a clear pattern could be observed with a steady
increase in roughness with feed rate. However, for a spindle speed of 10000 RPM an increase in feed rate
from 1000 mm/min to 1100 mm/min saw a slight decrease in roughness, which could be a result of matrix
smearing leading to a smoother surface, as found by Delahaigue et al. (2017). In terms of the eﬀect of tool
99
==PAGE:120==
6.1. Surface Roughness
Chapter 6
wear, the worn tool presented a lower surface roughness at 800 mm/min for all three spindle speeds and at
the highest feed rate for 10000 RPM, where a similar trend was observed at a combination of 800 mm/min
and 8000 RPM along with 1100 mm/min at 9000 RPM in the longitudinal direction. This can be explained
by the high temperatures generated with a worn tool leading to surface defects such as matrix smearing and
cavities being formed. For the worn tool, the temperatures generated at the highest and lowest feed rate
in combination with a larger cutting edge due to wear, appear to have caused matrix smearing, which has
lead to the cavities and holes being evened out. This is illustrated in the form of the lower roughness values
obtained for the worn tool (Delahaigue et al., 2017). These observations are further exempliﬁed by comparing
Figures 5.2.8 and 5.2.9 illustrating a roughness proﬁle obtained in the transverse direction for a new and
worn tool, respectively. Much higher peaks and deep, sharp valleys can be observed for the new tool, which is
in contrast to the worn tool, where a very even surface proﬁle can be seen. Here, a smooth and near regular
pattern can be observed across the entire evaluation length indicating a smooth surface. The trend in terms
of spindle speed is less obvious, which was also found for the longitudinal direction. This is in agreement with
observations made by Davima et al. (2017) and Wang et al. (2015) as discussed in Section 3.3.1.2, where the
speed was found to show no clear pattern when milling composite materials. However, at the highest and
lowest feed rate the spindle speed of 1100 RPM appear to produce the largest surface roughness, which could
due to the excessive generated causing a degradation of the surface quality.
The roughness in the transverse direction for a new and worn Unimerco tool is higher in comparison to
the longitudinal direction, because measurements were made across several plies. This is more pronounced
for the new tool, as seen in Figure 5.2.8, where a vast diﬀerence is observed, which also the case when
evaluating the roughness proﬁles for optimum machining parameters, as illustrated in Figure 5.2.14. For the
transverse direction large peaks can be observed at certain locations, potentially corresponding to diﬀerent
ply orientations having been more severely aﬀected by the machining, whereas the longitudinal direction has
a very smooth and contained pattern with only small peaks. The ﬁbre orientation with respect to the cutting
direction has a major impact on the amount of surface roughness as discussed for the Franken tool. As such,
the variation in roughness is better captured by the transverse direction, and will thus be a better indicator of
the overall roughness of the composite material. This is in contrast to the longitudinal direction, which only
measures across a single ply. As such, this could result in a severe underestimation of the roughness if measure-
ments are done on the layer with highest surface quality, and therefore not representative of the entire surface.
Before the two tools are compared, the following statements should be made: The machining parameters
for both tools were intentionally kept similar in that three spindle speeds and feed rates at a low, medium
and high setting were chosen. The diﬀerence between each setting in terms of spindle speed is the same for
each of the tools. However, in comparison to other studies, the diﬀerence between each spindle speed when
assessing the tools separately, is not as pronounced. In this study a 16% increase is found between each speed,
however other experiments have used a higher percentage. This could explain the similar values seen in some
instances at the diﬀerent feed rates along with the larger standard deviations for the longitudinal direction.
The same composite material was used for both Unimerco and Franken Fiber-Cut, namely a woven structure
with a top layer consisting of a thin glass sheet along with the milling being done on the same CNC machine.
Finally, both tools were coated with a diamond like coating. This should enable the focus to lay on comparing
the geometry of the tools along with machining parameters.
The lower roughness of the Franken tool in comparison to the Unimerco was in contrast to initially studies
done on eﬀect of geometry on machining CFRP composites (Davima et al., 2017), (Caggiano, 2018). Here, a
low number of ﬂutes was found to decrease surface roughness, but in this study the eight-ﬂuted Franken tool
was found to obtain a better surface quality than the four-ﬂuted tool from Unimerco. This observation, how-
ever, is conﬁrmed by Hagino and Inoue (2013), Kili¸ckap et al. (2015) and Erkan et al. (2013) in independent
studies, as discussed in Section 3.3.1.2. Here, a high number of ﬂutes at low feed rates lead to less surface
roughness, which is also evident when comparing Figures 5.2.3 and 5.2.10. The longitudinal measurements for
Franken and Unimerco, respectively, illustrates that low feeds coupled with a higher number of ﬂutes leads
to the best surface roughness. The explanation could be that as the number of ﬂutes, and therefore teeth,
of the tool increases, the feed per tooth decreases, which leads to a lower surface roughness. Furthermore,
the segmenting of the Franken tool could lead to a better surface ﬁnish, as less damaged is being done to
100
==PAGE:121==
Chapter 6
6.1. Surface Roughness
the matrix and ﬁbres. The helix angle is another source of diﬀerence between the tool, where the Franken
Fiber-Cut has no helix angle, but Unimerco does. As such, the helix angle appears to lower the surface
roughness, which is in agreement with studies done by Hagino and Inoue (2013) along with Jenarthanan and
Jeyapaul (2015), as seen in Section 3.3.1.2. It should be noted that, although Franken Fiber-Cut generally
presented the lowest roughness, when comparing the average value obtained in the transverse direction for the
worn tool at the lowest feed rate and spindle speed, Unimerco was found to present a better surface quality.
The same could be seen for the highest spindle speed combined with the highest feed rate. However, as this
was not generally the trend, and the values do overlap when taking the standard deviation into account, this
does not appear to signiﬁcant. Especially, as Franken Fiber-Cut presented a lower roughness in all other
instances.
The measurements made in the longitudinal and transverse direction for both new tools indicated that
the feed rate is a signiﬁcant parameter, where an increase in feed rate, when comparing the highest and
lowest values, will lead to a higher surface roughness. For the Franken end mill, there is a near linear increase
in roughness as a function of feed rate, whereas for the Unimerco tool, there is a decrease in roughness at the
intermediate feed rate of 1000 mm/min, which indicates an optimum condition has been reached in terms of
machining parameters. Any further raise in feed rate will lead to an increase in surface roughness, which is
one of the major diﬀerences between the tools: Franken Fiber-Cut generally sees an increase roughness for the
longitudinal and transverse direction, when the feed rate is raised. Unimerco on the other hand, has a diﬀerent
pattern for the two highest spindle speeds, which is most pronounced for the transverse measurements. Here,
the ﬁrst increase in feed rate leads to a lower roughness, where after it increases rapidly, although an increase
in feed rate from the highest to the lowest, and ignoring the intermediate stage leads to an increase in
roughness. Generally, the roughness is found to be higher in the transverse direction for both tools, as this
means measuring across several plies with a diﬀerent ﬁbre orientation with respect to the cutting direction
leading to a higher variation in roughness. This is conﬁrmed by Figure 5.1.1 illustrating the many layers of
the composite. Similar trends were observed for measurements in the longitudinal and transverse direction
with regards to the eﬀect of varying the machining parameters on the surface roughness. This was the case
for Unimerco and Franken Fiber-Cut, which shows that the transverse direction can be used to assess the
inﬂuence of spindle speed and feed rate on the surface quality. This is in contrast to initial studies done by
Sheikh-Ahmad et al. (2012), where no clear pattern was found between transverse roughness measurements
and milling parameters. However, this was contradicted by Gara and Tsoumarev (2016a), who found the
transverse direction to be dependent on tool geometry when milling CFRP composites. Further studies on
the subject by El-Hofy et al. (2011) and Can (2017) have found the transverse direction to be eﬀective in
predicting best machining parameters, thus in agreement with the ﬁndings of this study. These observations
illustrate this is still an emerging ﬁeld, and the roughness values depend on a variety of interactions and
parameters such as ﬁbre orientation, composite structure, tool geometry, machining conditions, temperature
and coolant. Thus, when evaluating the surface quality the surface roughness can not be used as a sole indi-
cator to assess the quality. Other aspects, such as visual inspections the part and checking for inconsistencies
in the form of delaminations and other surface damages is also essential. This is discussed in Section 6.3,
where a visual representation of changing the machining parameters are illustrated in Section 5.4.
The optimal machining parameters were found for a new Franken Fiber-Cut to be a feed rate of 1000
mm/min with a speed of 7000 RPM for the transverse direction, which resulted in a R10z value of 5.37 µm.
For the longitudinal direction it was at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min combined with a spindle speed of 5000
RPM, where the R10z value was 6.70 µm. However, the roughness obtained for all three spindle speeds were
very similar. For Unimerco the best machining parameters for low surface roughness is a feed rate of 1000
mm/min combined with a spindle speed of 9000 RPM for the transverse direction, where the R10z value was
7.99 µm. For the longitudinal measurements, it was found to be at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min with a speed
of 10000 RPM, which lead to a R10z value of 6.57 µm.
A representative roughness proﬁle for the optimum machining parameters for a new Unimerco and Franken
Fiber-Cut tool is shown in Figures 5.2.7 and 5.2.14. It illustrated that the overall lowest roughness could
be found for Franken in the transverse direction, followed by the longitudinal measurements for the same
direction. This is evident in that the lowest amount of peaks and valleys was observed for the former, and
101
==PAGE:122==
6.2. Optical Surface Roughness
Chapter 6
the roughness pattern across the entire surface appear regular. The same can be seen for the longitudinal
direction for most of the evaluation length, however towards the end larger peaks are appearing, resulting
in a higher roughness. Unimerco presented a higher roughness in comparison to Franken for both directions
with regards to the new tool, which is evident when viewing the roughness proﬁles. Here, much larger peaks
and valleys can be observed for Unimerco indicating more machining damage has occurred.
Several studies have indicated that surface roughness might not be the best parameter upon which to
base the surface quality of the composite laminate (Azmi et al., 2013),(Ghidossi et al., 2004), (Gara and
Tsoumarev, 2016a). This is mainly when considering a stylus based measuring tool, as this leads to diﬃculties
when having to place the stylus in the same place for each measurements in order to get comparable results.
This diﬃculty is evident in the standard deviations found for each results for the longitudinal direction. This
indicates that there is a variation of surface roughness within each measurements, which makes it more
diﬃcult to perform any statistical analysis. Furthermore, because the surface roughness values are small in
comparison to other studies, any deviations from the mean will be more signiﬁcant However, the standard
deviation is not too inconsistent across measurements, which illustrates that the variation could be due to the
varying structure of the laminate, where the weave posses extra diﬃculties due to each ply not being in one
direction, but rather a mixture. Additionally, this is not a plain weave, but a three-harness pattern, compli-
cating the measurements further. This is validated by the cross-sectional view of the composite structure as
seen in Figure 5.1.1 illustrating the many diﬀerent layers, each varying slightly in thickness and position due
to the nature of a weave. Although, the stylus had a diameter of 5 µm, the separation distance between each
layer varies greatly making it a challenge to replicate the measurements at diﬀerent locations. The standard
deviations are more pronounced in the longitudinal direction in comparison to the transverse direction, which
exempliﬁes the individual eﬀect of ﬁbre orientation along with the roughness of the matrix. In the transverse
direction the stylus will move across several layers, so although the roughness values will be higher, the
deviation between the measurements will be less severe. In the longitudinal direction, the placement of the
stylus is of utmost importance as the stylus will move across less ﬁbre orientations, and in some cases it
will move across the same. This serves to further highlight the transverse direction as being essential when
assessing the quality of the composite in terms of surface roughness. As the highest values are found in this
direction, this should be used for setting the quality standard of the composite parts. Another challenge
with using a contact based method for measuring the surface quality is which roughness parameter to select.
As speciﬁed in the introduction to this chapter the Ra value has mainly been included due to it still being
used by the aerospace industry, where the R10z value is better for capturing the range of roughness across a
specimen. The latter is calculated by measuring the highest peak to lowest valley within the ﬁve sampling
lengths, and then an average is taken. This value only uses the ﬁve highest and deepest features, thus the
extremes have a great inﬂuence on the ﬁnal value. In contrast Ra averages all the peaks and values, and
neutralises the few extreme points, which are seen as outlines (Free, 2015). As such, these points do not have
a signiﬁcant impact on the ﬁnal result. The need to take the extreme features into account was highlighted
by the roughness proﬁles for standard and optimum machining parameters, as shown in Figures 5.2.1, 5.2.2,
5.2.7, 5.2.8, 5.2.9 and 5.2.14 for Franken and Unimerco, respectively. Even for the new tool peaks and valleys
was observed, which was made more evident in certain measurement directions and tooling conditions. If
only the Ra value is presented then some of these proﬁles will appear to have the same roughness values,
resulting in the extent of damage being diminished and inaccurately portrayed.
6.2
Optical Surface Roughness
The purpose of doing optical measurements of the areal surface roughness was to investigate the eﬀect of ply
orientation on the machined surface quality. Furthermore, it was also an opportunity to evaluate diﬀerent
approaches for measuring the roughness for future studies along with gaining an understanding of areal
roughness parameters rather than proﬁle values as obtained with a proﬁlometer. The measurements were
done at a magniﬁcation of 20x rather than 5x, at which images were also captured as illustrated in Figure
5.3.1 and 5.3.2. This was based on several studies indicating that a higher magniﬁcation provided a better
and more reliable result due to more details being captured (Green et al., 2015), (Zekavat et al., 2018). As
such, an underestimation of surface damage should be avoided as seen with lower magniﬁcations.
102
==PAGE:123==
Chapter 6
6.2. Optical Surface Roughness
The identity of each layer is not known, however the results can still be used to illustrate the signiﬁcance
of stacking sequence and ﬁbre orientation when it comes to the quality of the composite part. Figure 5.3.5
illustrates the measurements obtained for a worn Unimerco tool used in conventional milling mode with a
feed rate of 1000 mm/min and a spindle speed of 9000 RPM. Here, a large range of values were found between
the layers, which can only be due to the eﬀect of ﬁbre orientation as each one experience the same machining
parameters and tool geometry. Thus, diﬀerent cutting mechanisms occurred across each of the orientations
leading to varying roughness values. As seen in the literature review, certain orientations will experience
severe damage in the form large pits and protruding ﬁbres, where using a worn tool could signiﬁcantly increase
the observed deterioration in the form of a higher roughness (Duboust et al., 2016),(Gara and Tsoumarev,
2016b). There was found to be a variation of roughly 62 % between the smallest and largest values, as
observed for both areal roughness parameters, namely Sa and S10z. This indicates that certain orientations
are critical in determining the highest roughness, and therefore the most extensive machining damage. When
comparing the areal roughness values and the proﬁle values obtained for the transverse direction, there is
not a large diﬀerence between them. However, comparing the measurements in the longitudinal direction to
the areal parameters sees a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the obtained values, which indicates a tendency
for longitudinal measurements to underestimate the severity of machining damage. A variation among the
obtained areal roughness of each ply was also observed for the worn Franken Fiber-Cut end mill. Here, a
signiﬁcant diﬀerence was found across the plies, which was particularly evident when comparing ply 3 to ply
2 and 4. The former layer presented a 89.3% higher roughness, which could be due to the observed valleys in
Figure 5.3.4, illustrating a 3D segment of the machined surface. Here, ply 2 and 4 presented a very smooth
surface whereas ply 3 contained deep holes resulting in a markedly higher Sa and S10z value. Ply 1 presented
the second highest roughness value, due to observed valleys, however these were not as deep as for ply 4, as
shown by the resulting roughness value. This again can be explained by the diﬀerent response of the ﬁbre
orientations during machining.
The two samples should be comparable in terms of the raw information gathered as the magniﬁcation
was the same, which is vital as seen through the literature review (Zekavat et al., 2018), (Green et al.,
2015). However, it should be kept in mind that, sample 1 consisted of 3x4 images stitched together, where
each original one was the size of sample 2, and acquired at the same magniﬁcation of 20x. As such, the
evaluation area of sample 1 was three times larger than sample 2, thus more information with regards to
surface texture and roughness was obtained as seen in Figure 5.3.3. The machined pattern of sample 2
appeared uniform throughout the entire image, so it was found to be a good representation on a bigger scale
as well. However, sample 2 also presented the largest variation among the samples, which could be due to an
inaccurate representation of the overall surface, but based on Figures 5.3.1 and 5.3.2 illustrating an overall
view of the two tools, more pits do appear for sample 2, viz. Franken Fiber-Cut in comparison to Unimerco.
Although, sample 1 presented a larger overview in terms of surface texture, due to the stitching of several
images, the layers were harder to identify, as seen when comparing Figures 5.3.3 and 5.3.4. For sample 2,
the layers were easy to distinguish, which could be due to the smaller area providing an enlarge view of
the plies. A direct comparison between the images is diﬃcult due to sample 1 containing a relatively higher
information density, but it can still be observed that both Sa and S10z values were signiﬁcantly higher for
the Franken Fiber-Cut for most plies, which was expected based on the measurements stemming from the a
track milled in climb mode. Several studies have found this results in a higher roughness, which this is very
much an indication of (Richardson, 2011), (Engineering, 2010), (Rasmussen, 2018). The lower surface quality
seen for Franken is further conﬁrmed by comparing Figures 5.3.3 and 5.3.4. Although pits and valleys are
present in the surface milled by Unimerco, these are not as uniformly distributed throughout the entire length
in comparison to Franken Fiber-Cut and a lower amount are observed. Another indication of the higher
roughness found for climb milling, can be seen when comparing the proﬁle roughness values obtained for the
worn Franken Fiber-Cut to those found for the areal parameters. The latter values are overall much higher in
comparison to the transverse roughness values for the worn tool even when taking the standard deviation into
account. Although, S-values do tend to be higher due to more accurately portraying the roughness compared
to R-values based on the similar results obtained for Unimerco when comparing the Ra and R10z values in
the transverse direction to the Sa and S10z, it seems valid to draw a comparison between those for Franken
Fiber-Cut. The experiment also highlighted that even though a larger overview of the specimen, as found
103
==PAGE:124==
6.3. Delamination
Chapter 6
for sample 1 milled with Unimerco, should present a clear image of the surface, this was not the case as the
layers were more diﬃcult to identify, albeit once the various ﬁlters had been applied in SPIP this became
much easier. This indicates that in terms of measuring the areal roughness increasing the image size, albeit
maintaining the same resolution, might not always be better due to identiﬁcation of each layer being more
diﬃcult.
The measurements made on sample 1 also serves to highlight the issue with the stylus method, especially for
the longitudinal direction, where the surface roughness has a high probability of being diminished if the stylus
does not move across the roughest layer. Furthermore, the higher standard deviation between measurements
made in this direction using the proﬁlometer can also be explained by this. The stylus might have moved
across a few plies with a larger variation in terms of surface roughness causing contrasting values to be
recorded. Due to time constraints only a single measurement was done for each ply on the samples, which
meant standard deviations could not be calculated, making the results less reliable. However, as the same
observations were made for two samples having experienced diﬀerent machining conditions, this is still an
indication of the importance on deﬁning the surface quality based on several ply orientations rather than
simply one. This is because of the large variations seen across the layers. The study also highlighted that
climb milling presents a much higher roughness in comparison to conventional for most ply orientations. This
is particularly evident for ply 1 on sample 1 and ply 3 for sample 2.
The areal surface roughness parameters also presents an advantage compared to the standard proﬁle values
especially on multi-directional and woven laminates. This is due to the variation in the surface structure and
the diﬀerent amount of damage sustained by each ﬁbre orientation. As the areal parameters cover a larger
area, they are more robust to any changes in the measurement position, meaning more reliable results can
be obtained. This indicates the problems with the stylus accurately portraying the roughness of complex,
heterogeneous structures, such as a composite material. A reason for this is the diﬃculty in measuring the
surface characteristics in the third dimension, which the optical method presents a solution to. However, as
seen with the proﬁle roughness parameters used in this study, Ra and R10z, the same problems presents
itself here, with Sa only capturing the average roughness and not the extreme values. Ply 5 on sample 1 is a
good example of this, which has the second lowest Sa value, however in terms of the calculated S10z it has
the second highest. This is could be due to extreme peaks being present, which the Sa value will not take
into account. On the other hand, as S10z is calculated by measuring the lowest and highest features within
the sampling area, and then taking an average, these valleys and peaks will have a much greater inﬂuence
and give a more realistic picture of the roughness.
Some limitations were observed such as time spend on establishing a correct cut-oﬀﬁlter, as less research has
been done with regards to optical measurements, and the selected parameters are therefore often based on
the individual image. Furthermore, there is a constrained on the distance between the lens and the sample,
which makes this unsuitable for large pieces, and the set-up is less portable in comparison to the proﬁlometer.
6.3
Delamination
In this section, the ﬁrst part will focus on analysing the light microscopic images, as seen in Section 5.4, for
both tools, which is then followed by a comparative discussion. Next, the delamination frequency results will
be analysed, which was a quantiﬁcation of the microscopic observations for the worn tools.
The general trend for the Franken Fiber-Cut tool was an increase in feed rate leading to a higher de-
lamination frequency, which was seen for both the new and worn tool at all three spindle speeds. This was
more evident for the worn tool, where the new tool displays very little delamination at all combinations of
feed rate and spindle speed. The correlation between protruding ﬁbres and feed rate is due to the negative
eﬀect of increasing the feed per tooth, which leads to a higher amount of ﬁbres being cut by the end mill,
leading to a larger likelihood of surface damage occurring(Liu et al., 2018). The observed delaminations
are in the top layer (or the bottom layer), which is due to delaminations being more prone to occur in the
outermost layer, as this only receives support from one side (Hintze et al., 2011). This was also illustrated by
104
==PAGE:125==
Chapter 6
6.3. Delamination
the fact that no obvious ﬁbre protrusions appeared from inside the laminate. Initially, at the lowest feed rate
no delaminations are observed, and there are no signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the new and worn tool, further
conﬁrming that a lower feed rate is preferred. As the speed is increased to 6000 RPM the diﬀerence between
the delaminations at each feed rate is becoming more pronounced, where the largest eﬀect can be seen when
machining at 7000 RPM and increasing the feed rate, which leads to a higher frequency of delaminations,
however the length of the uncut ﬁbres are higher at the intermediate feed rate of 1200 mm/min. This is the
case for the new and worn tool. The general trend of an increase in feed rate leading to more delaminations
can also be explained in terms of the chips generated during slot milling. When the feed rate is kept low, the
chip thickness is small resulting in low delamination frequencies, however as the feed increases so does the
chip size, leading to more surface damage (Caggiano, 2018). In terms of increasing the speed, whilst keeping
the feed rate constant, the trend was less obvious as was seen with surface roughness. At the lowest feed rate
of 1000 mm/min, there was a slight increase in delaminations, whereas at 1200 mm/min, there is a clear
increase in the frequency of delaminations and severity, which is mainly seen for the worn tool, however a
slight diﬀerence can also be observed for the new tool. At the highest feed rate, there is a sharp increase
in delaminations from 5000 RPM to 6000 RPM, but the diﬀerence is less pronounced between the latter
and 7000 RPM. This trend was observed for both the new and worn tool, although more distinct for the
latter. The increase in spindle speed leading to higher amounts of delaminations can be explained a raise
of the cutting forces, and an increase in temperature between the material and tool. This in turns leads to
softening of the matrix, making it easier for the ﬁbres to debond, thus resulting in protrusions (Li et al.,
2017). In terms of the length of the protruding ﬁbres, for the new tool generally the length was the same for
all combinations of machining parameters, which was the same for the worn tool, except at the highest feed
rate of 1500 mm/min. Here, the length of the ﬁbres decreased as the speed increased. A higher cutting speed
leads to slight plastic deformation of the matrix, which results in higher cutting forces. This will force the
ﬁbres to fracture due to higher stresses being applied, thus reducing the length of the ﬁbres (Li et al., 2017).
Except, at the lowest cutting speed for the Franken tool, there is a visible increase in delaminations when
comparing a new and old tool, which becomes more pronounced as the speed increases. This is due to the
tool having a more round and larger radius, resulting in large chips being formed during milling, leading to
larger delaminations(Hintze et al., 2011). The bottom layer consists of a woven structure, which means that
the ﬁbres are orientated at an angle to each other, resulting in more pronounced delaminations in certain
areas. This observation was also made by (Li et al., 2017), where the surface integrity of milled composite
laminates were studied. Here, the trimmed edge of woven CFRP specimen were found to mainly exhibit
protruding ﬁbres at the ﬁll yarn edge, and only few was seen for the warp yarn. Although, the exact lay-up of
the composite is unknown, this could explain why the delaminations are more apparent in some areas than
others. The eﬀect of the tool geometry on delaminations will be discussed further on, when a comparison is
made between Unimerco and Franken Fiber-Cut in terms of delaminations.
For Unimerco a clear trend in terms of an increase in feed rate leading to a higher delamination rate
was seen for the worn tool, where the trend is less obvious for the new tooling condition. The latter only
displayed little delaminations, thus a comparison across the feed rates and spindle speeds is diﬃcult. The
correlation between an increase in feed rate and delamination frequency can be explained by the fact that at
higher feed rates, larger chips are produced, which leads to more surface damaged in terms of delaminations
(Caggiano, 2018). For the Unimerco tool both Type I and Type II were observed, however most commonly
ﬁbre protrusions, thus Type II, were observed. For the new tool an initial increase in feed rate leads to more
delaminations, albeit still a small amount, for all three speeds. However, for the highest feed rate, the amount
of surface damage appear to decrease slightly, but the eﬀect is very subtle. The eﬀect of increasing the feed
rate on delamination frequency is much more pronounced for the worn tool. Here, an increase in feed rate
clearly leads to a worse surface quality, which is seen for all three spindle speeds. The increase in feed rate
leading to more delaminations is due to the material removal rate increasing, which makes it easier for the
ﬁbres to avoid the cutting edge. This also promotes longer ﬁbres to be formed at a constant speed, whilst
raising the feed rate, as seen at 8000 RPM. At a spindle speed of 9000 RPM, the same trend as observed for
8000 RPM is seen. Here, the frequency and length of the protruding ﬁbres increases when raising the feed
rate, which is also the case at 10000 RPM. Comparing the new and worn tool, there is a distinct reduction
in surface quality when utilising the latter tool, which is due to the change of tool geometry. An increase
in tool wear leads to a larger cutting edge radius, thus generating more damage, as the edge is less deﬁned.
105
==PAGE:126==
6.3. Delamination
Chapter 6
Furthermore, the resultant geometry also leads to higher forces resulting in lower surface quality (Hintze
et al., 2011).
Evaluating the delaminations for the new tool in terms of constant feed rate and changing spindle speed, sees
no diﬀerence between the speeds at the lowest feed rate. Increasing the feed rate to 1000 mm/min only sees
a very insigniﬁcant increase in protruding ﬁbres as the spindle speed increases. Finally, at the highest feed
rate of 1100 mm/min initially no delaminations were observed, however at the highest cutting speed a severe
Type I delamination were detected. Generally, for the new tool only small diﬀerences were seen between
the diﬀerent spindle speeds whilst keeping the feed rate ﬁxed. This is due to the major inﬂuence of tool
geometry on delamination frequency, where a sharp cutting edge is able to cut the ﬁbres eﬀectively(Hohensee,
1992),(Rummenh¨oller, 1996). This is in stark contrast to the worn tool, where delaminations were observed at
all combinations, albeit at varying frequencies. For the worn tool at the lowest feed rate an increase in speed
leads to more delaminations, when going from 9000 RPM to 10000 RPM, but no signiﬁcant damage was
observed between the two lowest spindle speeds. Increasing the feed rate to 1000 mm/min, sees a decrease in
delamination frequency when comparing 8000 RPM to 9000 RPM, where the former displays a signiﬁcant
amount of protruding ﬁbres, however moving from 9000 RPM to 10000 RPM, whilst maintaining a feed rate
of 1000 mm/min sees the frequency increase again, although the highest amount is observed for 8000 RPM.
The trend in terms of spindle speed is less distinct for the worn tool, but at the lowest feed rate an increase in
speed leads to a more delaminations. At the two highest feed rates, an increase in speed from 9000 RPM to
1000 RPM also means an increase in delamination frequency. This could be due to the higher speed resulting
in greater temperatures, which reduces the strength of the epoxy matrix, allowing ﬁbres to debond, observed
as protruding ﬁbres. However, the highest delamination frequency was observed at 8000 RPM for the two
highest feed rates. As such, increasing the speed from 8000 RPM to 9000 RPM at 1000 mm/min and 1100
mm/min leads to a reduction in delaminations, which is in contrast to previous ﬁndings (Sheikh-Ahmad
et al., 2012) as seen in Section 3.3.1.1. However, in two separate studies by Davima et al. (2017) and Pecat
et al. (2012) the same observations were made with regards to a decrease in speed leading to higher amounts
of delaminations. The reduction in delamination frequency at the two highest feed rates when increasing the
speed, could be explained by the reduction in cutting time. The worn tool displayed a signiﬁcant amount
of delamination for the highest feed rate at all three speeds, where the highest amount was observed at the
lowest spindle speed, which is due to the ﬁbres being able to bend away from the cutting edge at low speeds.
Changing between the two highest spindle speeds of 9000 RPM and 10000 RPM, whilst keeping the feed rate
ﬁxed at 1100 mm/min, did not results in any signiﬁcant modiﬁcation of delaminations. However, a decrease
in length, which is again related to the cutting forces increasing with speed resulting in shorter protruding
ﬁbres, was seen.
Comparing the Franken and Unimerco end mill reveals that for the new tool, there is only a small dif-
ference in terms of delaminations, which can be found at the highest feed rate for both tools. Here, the
delamination frequency was slightly higher for the Franken tool, however this was only in the form of type
II delaminations. Unimerco, on the other hand produced both type I and type II, where the former is more
severe. Although, the worn condition for both tools produced worse delaminations in terms of frequency and
length in comparison the their new counterpart, this was much more pronounced for the Unimerco end mill.
This could be due to the lower number of ﬂutes, which leads to higher feed per tooth, thus resulting in large
chip thickness leading to more surface damage(Kili¸ckap et al., 2015). Furthermore, the Franken tool has a 0◦
helix angle, which studies have found results in lower surface damage(Hagino and Inoue, 2013). This eﬀect
appears more pronounced in the worn condition, whereas for the new tool both perform at an acceptable
level. It should be mentioned that although both up-milling and down-milling have been included in the
images only the up-milling sides have been analysed.
For both Unimerco and Franken, new and worn, delaminations were only found on the bottom layer, and not
on the top. This could be due to a glass layer being applied on the surface in order to protect the laminate.
This greatly aﬀects the ability of delaminations to occur as well as the bottom ply is simply the woven
structure consisting of epoxy resin and carbon ﬁbres.

==PAGE:127==

6.3. Delamination
6.3.1
Delamination Frequency
Figures 5.4.19 and 5.4.20 illustrates the quantitative analysis of the delamination frequency for a worn
Unimerco and Franken Fiber-Cut end mill, respectively, where both were found to be in agreement with
the observation made of the light microscopic images. For Unimerco Figure 5.4.19 serves to highlight the
observed trend between an increase in feed rate leading to a more signiﬁcant amount of delaminations, which
is due to larger chips being generated with increase feed rate. This in turns leads to more delaminations and
protruding ﬁbres. Furthermore, the highest delamination frequency can be observed for the lowest spindle
speed, where a signiﬁcant amount was observed for the two highest feed rates. This is in agreement with
studies by Sheikh-Ahmad et al. (2012) who found that generally delaminations increased with decreasing
spindle speed and increasing feed rate. However, an increase in delamination frequency is observed between
9000 RPM and 10000 RPM, thus in contrast to these ﬁndings, but the phenomenon has been observed by
several other researchers(Pecat et al., 2012), (Kili¸ckap et al., 2015). The explanation given was that higher
cutting speeds promotes the deformation of the matrix, resulting in ﬁbres escaping, as discussed in the previ-
ous section. In terms of optimal machining conditions for the worn Unimerco tool a number of options are
available. Both 8000 RPM and 9000 RPM presents no delaminations at 800 mm/min, thus this combination
could extent the life time of the tool. However, only a slight increase in delaminations appear when increasing
the feed rate to 1000 mm/min at 9000 RPM, so in terms of maintaining productivity level and keeping the
delamination frequency at a minimum this appears to be the best compromise.
Figure 5.4.20 illustrates the discussed correlation between a raise in feed rate leading to higher amounts of
delaminations for Franken Fiber-Cut, as seen in Section 5.4. This is in agreement with the ﬁndings for the
Unimerco tool, where the reason for this is due to a larger chip formation during machining when increasing
the feed rate (Caggiano, 2018). The graph serves to quantify the amount of delaminations observed, which
makes for an easier comparison between the diﬀerent machining combinations, where the highest spindle
speed produces the most delaminations. This was found for all three feed rates and in agreement with ﬁndings
by Liu et al. (2017). The diﬀerence between the lowest speed of 5000 RPM and the intermediate speed of 6000
RPM is less prominent at the lowest feed rate and is the same for at 1200 mm/min. Generally, an increase in
speed leads to a higher frequency count, which is in contrast to the observation made for Unimerco. This can
be explained in terms of a higher speed resulting in an accelerated rate of plastic deformation rate, resulting
in more severe surface damage, as found by (Pecat et al., 2012). The best machining conditions for the worn
Franken Fiber-Cut tool can also be achieved through a selection of spindle speeds and feed rates. The lowest
frequency is presented at 5000 RPM at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min, however increasing the spindle speed to
6000 RPM only sees a slight increase in frequency, and further increasing the feed rate to 1200 mm/min at
this spindle speed, only increases the frequency by one. Increasing the feed rate and spindle speed leads to a
higher output, however this is only beneﬁcial if the end result is a good part, which require minimum rework
- if any. Thus, changing the machining parameters needs to be done with this kept in mind. The return of
increasing the feed rate to 1200 mm/min seems to be a worthy risk, as the delamination frequency is still
low, and productivity is increased.
In comparing Figures 5.4.19 and 5.4.20 generally, the worn Unimerco tool displays a signiﬁcant higher
amount of delaminations compared Franken Fiber-Cut. This can be explained by the tool geometry, where
the latter has a higher amount of ﬂutes, meaning that the feed per tooth is signiﬁcantly lower than that of the
four-ﬂute Unimerco end mill (Hagino and Inoue, 2013), (Kalla et al., 2010). As discussed previously, a lower
feed is desirable as this leads to smaller chips being generated, thus a better surface quality. Furthermore,
the serration pattern found on the Franken Fiber-Cut end mill also leads to lower delaminations as this
increases the cutting action of the tool. However, the optimal machining parameters for the tools are both at
the intermediate feed rate and spindle speed, and here there is not a signiﬁcant amount of diﬀerence between
the frequency of delaminations. This serves to illustrate that by choosing the correct machining parameters
based on the tool used, optimal conditions can be reached at a high productivity level, avoiding scrap and
rework of damaged parts. It should be noted that although the frequency count is similar Franken is still
displaying the lowest amount.
As illustrated through the analysis of the delamination frequency for Franken Fiber-Cut and Unimerco,
107
==PAGE:128==
6.3. Delamination
Chapter 6
several contradicting observations were made in terms of spindle speed. For Franken Fiber-Cut an increase
in speed lead to more delaminations, which was in agreement with some studies, but not with others. For
Unimerco a mixture of observations were made, which indicates that several factors have to be considered
when choosing the best machining parameters. This includes evaluating the tool geometry, the structure of the
composite, the ﬁxture, temperature and the coolant used. All these factors have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the
end result. For the feed rate, a clear trend is visible in that an increase leads to a higher delamination frequency.
To adequately assess the surface damage sustained during slot milling, the surface roughness measurements
and delamination inspections should be evaluated together. For the new Franken Fiber-Cut the general
trend was that an increase in feed rate lead to more delaminations, which is in agreement with the surface
roughness measurements. However, the delaminations observed were all low in frequency and length, thus
drawing conclusive parallels between machining conditions and the damage sustained is diﬃcult. As such, the
surface quality should mainly be based upon the surface roughness measurements with a visual inspection of
the surface as a supplement when examining the new tool. This leads to the optimum conditions as being at
a speed rate of 5000 RPM with a feed rate of 1000 mm/min in the lonitudinal direction, where the transverse
has the same ideal feed rate at a speed of 7000 RPM. For the worn Franken Fiber-Cut tool, the increase in
feed rate lead to a higher delamination count, which was made more evident with a frequency graph. This
observation is also conﬁrmed by the roughness measurements in the longitudinal and transverse direction for
all feed rates and spindle speeds except one. This is at 5000 RPM for the worn tool, where an initial increase
in feed rate leads to a lower surface roughness, however the general trend is that an increase leads to a worse
surface quality. For the delamination frequency an increase in spindle speed means a higher surface roughness
at all three feed rates, where there is not a distinct trend for the surface roughness. Although, a worn tool is
not to be used for long in production as it clearly leads to a higher roughness and delaminations, through an
optimization of the machining parameters a longer tool life could be possible. For the worn Franken, the best
feed rate is at 1000 mm/min for both directions and in terms of delamination frequency. The discrepancy is
apparent in terms of speed, where the longitudinal surface roughness is lowest at 6000 RPM, for transverse
it is at 7000 RPM, and for delamination 6000 RPM achieves the best results. However, for the transverse
direction 6000 RPM and 7000 RPM presents similar results, thus the best conditions are at 1000 mm/min
with a spindle speed of 6000 RPM.
Unimerco did not present a high number of delaminations for the new tool, although generally the higher
feed rates did produce a slightly worse surface quality. As in the case of Franken Fiber-Cut, this makes a
joint conclusion diﬃcult in terms of delaminations and surface roughness, thus the quality should mainly be
evaluated based on the latter for the new tool. Here, the best conditions are at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min
for both directions, with a spindle speed of 10000 RPM for the longitudinal direction and 9000 RPM for
the transverse direction. For the worn tool, a signiﬁcant increase in delamination frequency was observed
at all spindle speeds as the feed rate increased. This is in agreement with the trend for surface roughness
measurements for the worn tool, where the same trend could be observed. In terms of delamination frequency
and spindle speed, there was not a clear trend, which was also the case for the roughness values. As such, for
the lowest delamination frequency a feed rate of 800 mm/min should be utilised at a speed of either 8000
RPM or 9000 RPM. The roughness in the longitudinal direction was smallest at 800 mm/min at 9000 RPM,
where the transverse was at 800 mm/min and a speed of 8000 RPM. As such, the best machining conditions
are with a feed rate of 800 mm/min using a speed of 8000 RPM, as this satisﬁes both the transverse direction
and delamination frequency. For the longitudinal direction all three spindle speeds presents very similar
values at the lowest feed rate, and the suggestion therefore appear valid.

Chapter 7
Statistical Analysis of Surface
Roughness in Slot Milling
To verify the observations made with respect to surface roughness in slot milling, and the subsequent dis-
cussions, which culminated in a determination of optimal machining parameters, an analysis of variance
(ANOVA) is conducted. The statistical method serves as validation that the obtained experimental data and
the conclusion reached with regards to best machining practise is reasonable.
The ANOVA analysis introduced in Section 3.3.1.3, was used to investigate the signiﬁcance of the spin-
dle speed and feed rate on the surface roughness, R10z, in the transverse direction for the new tool. The
longitudinal measurements could not be used for an ANOVA analysis due to the variation among the obtained
values for each combination of machining parameters. Generally, these roughness values were much smaller
in comparison to the ones obtained in the transverse direction, which meant a more pronounced response
when measurements were not made in the same ply for the ﬁve trials done for each machining combination.
Fortunately, the analysis of the roughness measurements, as found in Section 6.1, revealed that similar trends
could be observed for measurements made in the longitudinal and transverse direction. This showed that
transverse measurements can be used to assess the inﬂuence of machining parameters, which validates the
ANOVA analysis. As the aim of this study is to present the machining conditions resulting in the highest
surface quality the analysis will only be done for a new tool.
The levels and factors used to conduct the analysis are presented in Tables 7.0.1 for Unimerco.

Table 7.0.2 shows the results for the ANOVA test done for a new Unimerco tool for R10z values in the
transverse direction. The p-value for the feed rate and interaction are below the conﬁdence interval value of
α = 0.05, with pfeed = 4.53 · 10−9 < 0.05 and pinteraction = 0.0126 < 0.05 meaning that the null hypothesis
can be rejected. Thus, at a conﬁdence interval of 95% these two parameters present statistical and physical
signiﬁcance on the surface roughness, and their means are markedly diﬀerent. However, this is not the case
for the spindle speed, which displays a p-value of pspindle = 0.065 < 0.05. From Table 7.0.2, the feed rate
is found to explain 54.77 % of the variation in surface roughness, where the interaction accounts for 11.81
%. This is also in agreement with Figure 5.2.12, where the same observations with regards to increasing the
feed rate can be found for all three spindle speeds, but the trend for a change in speed is less predictive.

Due to the statistical signiﬁcance of the interaction term, the best practise is to interpret the factors as a
combination of each other and not their individual contribution. This is because there is a dependency of the
feed rate on the spindle speed and vice versa, which can be illustrated in an interaction plot, which is simply
the the mean value of R10z plotted against the feed rate for the three spindle speed, thus is the same found
in the results section. The plot is illustrated in Figure 7.0.1, where if a low feed rate, 1000 mm/min were
chosen, the best option in terms of spindle speed would be 8000 RPM. However, at a high feed rate the best
option is the 9000 RPM. The optimum conditions for achieving the lowest surface roughness is at a feed rate
of 1000 mm/min with a spindle speed of 9000 RPM. The interaction plot is simply
In order to validate the ANOVA model for the Unimerco tool, and ensuring a good ﬁt the R2 and ad-
justed R2 value is calculated. This represents the percentage variation in the dependent response variable,
the surface roughness, which can be explained by its relationship with the independent variables, namely the
spindle speed and feed rate and their interaction. The R2 is 0.71, and the adjusted R2 value was found as
0.75, meaning that the model ﬁts reasonably well, and is valid. Thus, the total variation explained by the
variables are 75%. The remaining 25% is most likely due to errors associated with the measuring equipment
and obtaining the results.

7.1. Validation of ANOVA
Chapter 7
In order to achieve the lowest roughness values a combination of 1000 mm/min and 7000 RPM for the feed
rate and spindle speed, respectively, should be chosen. However, the ﬁgure also illustrate that selecting a
speed of 5000 RPM at the same feed rate would produce similar results. This is in agreement with a change
in speed being less predictable. The R2 value for the Franken Fiber-Cut ANOVA is 0.77, with an adjusted
R2 value of 0.81, which indicates a ﬁtting model.
As seen in Tables 7.0.2 and 7.0.4, the feed rate has the most pronounced eﬀect on the surface roughness
for both tools, followed by the interaction. The spindle speed does not have a considerable inﬂuence, but is
statistical signiﬁcant in the case of Franken Fiber-Cut. However, as noted previously due to the interaction
term presenting a high statistical signiﬁcance for both tools, the importance of feed rate and spindle speed
as separate entities have been given less attention in favour of the interaction. This is in agreement with the
theory found in Section 3.3.1.2, where it was shown that diﬀerent combinations of feed rate and spindle speed
lead to varying surface quality, and cannot always be predicted by linear trends. Although, some studies have
found that the transverse roughness measurements are irregular and without a trend (Gara and Tsoumarev,
2016b), the results in this study revealed the transverse direction to be valid in predicting the best machining
parameters. This observation was conﬁrmed by several experimental investigations by El-Hofy et al. (2011),
Can (2017) and Boudelier et al. (2011) as shown in Section 3.3.1.2. Here, the results obtained for ANOVA in
the transverse direction was found to be in agreement with the ones acquired for the longitudinal direction,
thus the same optimum machining conditions were found for both directions.
The ANOVA results for both the Unimerco and Franken tool are in agreement with the results found
for the roughness measurement in Sections 5.2.1 and 5.2.2. Here, there was a clear trend for both tools in
terms of feed rate, where all three spindle speeds followed a similar trend in both longitudinal and transverse
directions. The eﬀect of the interaction between feed rate and spindle speed means that one cannot be
considered without the other, which is the case for both Unimerco and Franken Fiber-Cut. This is further
conﬁrmed by the roughness measurement results in Sections 5.2.1 and 5.2.2, where a clear trend in spindle
speed could not be established. This means that the diﬀerent feed rates will not give an optimum surface
quality when applying the same spindle speed. The ideal machining parameters found through the ANOVA
analysis is in general agreement with the roughness values obtained for the longitudinal direction. Thus, the
surface roughness should be based upon both direction. This is especially important when measurements are
done on a thinner composite consisting of multiple woven layers, as seen in this study. Here, the investigated
structure consisted of 39 plies, where each ply contained a woven structure of carbon ﬁbres, across a distance
of 9 mm. As such, each ply is only 0.23mm thick, where the average diameter of a carbon ﬁbre is 5 µm. This
means the stylus can easily move across a few plies in the longitudinal direction, although care was taken to
place the stylus in parallel with the measuring direction to ensure a valid measurement.
7.1
Validation of ANOVA
The adequacy of the underlying model is one of the main aspects of obtaining valid results from the ANOVA
analysis. For this purpose, residual analysis is used as one of the primary tools to check for normality and
homoscedasticity, which are two of the main assumptions to be met, as explained in Section 3.3.1.3. The
observations must also be independent of each other, which is naturally met as the measurements were done
in a diﬀerent place on the workpiece, and will therefore not be found repeated within or outside the group.
Here, a group describes the ﬁve measurements collected for each combination of feed rate and spindle speed.
Thus, in this case there are nine groups, each with ﬁve replicates. One way of assessing the homoscedasticity
and normality is to do an individual assessment of each factor, thus spindle speed and feed rate, however this
would have to be done for each group within each factor, as the mean varies across the groups (Stone et al.,
2012). As such, a better alternative is to do it for the entire model, which will also account for the residuals
from the interaction term, which was found to exist for both tools.
The normal plot for the Unimerco tool can be seen in Figure 7.1.1. This is, as with the Franken tool,
a plot of the standardised residuals against the theoretical quantiles. It takes into account all the factors in
the model, viz. the spindle speed and feed rate, along with the interaction, as illustrated by the Spindle:Feed
112
==PAGE:133==
Chapter 7
7.1. Validation of ANOVA
notation on the graph. This is important as the interaction term was signiﬁcant, as seen by the small p-value
from the ANOVA analysis. All the values lie fairly close to the line, which indicates the residuals stemmed
from a normal distribution, conﬁrming the validity of the ANOVA model assumption. A slight tail can be
observed, however as with Franken Fiber-Cut, the symmetry in the plot suggests a normal distribution, albeit
one where a few outliers might be present.
Figure 7.1.1: Normal probability plot of residuals for Unimerco
Figure 7.1.2 illustrates the residual plot versus ﬁtted values for Unimerco. Here, all the points are randomly
distributed and does not seem to follow any particular pattern. This conﬁrms the assumption that variance
is equal across all groups. To further validate this assumption a Levene’s test was done on the Unimerco tool,
where a p-value of 0.4594 was found. As this is above the signiﬁcance value of α = 0.05, the assumption of
homoscedasticity is veriﬁed.
Figure 7.1.2: Standardised residuals against ﬁtted values for Unimerco
113
==PAGE:134==
7.1. Validation of ANOVA
Chapter 7
Figure 7.1.3 illustrates the residuals plotted against the spindle speed and feed rate, left and right, respectively
for Unimerco. As with the Franken end mill each factor had three levels as indicated by the placement of the
residuals. Here, a random pattern can be seen at all levels for both speed and feed rate, indicating a valid
model and the results from the ANOVA analysis are valid.
Figure 7.1.3: Residual plots for the feed rate and spindle speed for Unimerco
Based on Figures 7.1.4 to 7.1.3, the ANOVA model used for Franken Fiber-Cut and Unimerco holds true as
the assumption of normality and homoscedasticity is fulﬁlled.
Figure 7.1.4 illustrates the normal probability plot for Franken Fiber-Cut, which shows the standardised
residuals plotted as a function of the theoretical quantiles. A reasonably good ﬁt to the linear model is
observed, which indicates that the residuals are normally distributed. The plot is slightly s-shaped, which
could indicate that the data has a few extreme values, however this alone should not pose a problem, because
the symmetry in the plot suggests that the residuals are distributed more or less symmetrically. Furthermore,
ANOVA is robust in case of any slight deviations from normality of the residuals. An alternative to accounting
for this to do a log transformation of the data, although this was not deemed necessary.
Figure 7.1.4: Normal probability plot of residuals for Franken Fiber-Cut
Figure 7.1.5 illustrates the residuals plotted against ﬁtted values for Franken Fiber-Cut. The data points
114
==PAGE:135==
Chapter 7
7.1. Validation of ANOVA
are randomly distributed and does not appear to follow a pattern, conﬁrming the residual variance is equal
everywhere, and the model is valid. In order to further verify this, a Levene’s test was performed, which
tests for equal variance amongst the groups. Here, a p value of 0.9862 was found, which is higher than the
signiﬁcance level of 0.05. This means that there is no evidence to suggest that the variance across groups is
statistically signiﬁcant and homogeneity of variances in the diﬀerent treatment groups can be safely assumed.
Figure 7.1.5: Residuals against ﬁtted values for Franken Fiber-Cut
Figure 7.1.6 illustrates the residuals plotted against the feed rate and spindle speed, respectively for Franken
Fiber-Cut. Each factor had three levels as indicated by the grouping of the residuals, and each one appear
to be randomly distributed with no signs of a pattern forming. As such, this indicates a ﬁtting model with
equal variance across the groups.
