Chapter 8
Countersinking Results
In this chapter, the ﬁrst part will focus on the results from the open hole tensile test, which is followed by the
open hole compression results. These two sections follow the same general structure, and initially the failure
mechanisms are studied through the use of optical microscopy. This is followed by the obtained strength
results, including stress-strain graphs to evaluate the response, and the last part is focused on the delamination
results. Additionally, the compression section also includes an introduction to the results obtained for the
samples containing a honeycomb core. Next, the outcome of the constituent content of composite materials
experiment will be presented, and the last section will be focused on moisture control of composite samples.
The latter test was done to verify the need for keeping the samples in the oven for an extent period of time
for both mechanical tests and the acid digestion experiment.
8.1
Open Hole Tension
In order to adequately describe the failure mechanisms seen for both tensile and compression, the layers of
the composite part had to be identiﬁed and a notation for each failure established. As this is the same for
both testing methods, an overview will be given here, which will be eﬀective throughout the rest of this chapter.
Figure 8.1.1 illustrates a standard example of compressive failure, which is ideal for identifying layers and
establish the notation for each failure mechanism. Here, the red box is ply splitting, so called matrix cracking,
seen by cracks in the matrix, white arrows are ﬁbre cracking, where the ﬁbres have broken, and blue arrows
are delaminations, and is where the plies have separated completely from each other.
Figure 8.1.1: Notation of failure modes: ; Red box: ply splitting, white arrow: ﬁbre cracking and blue arrow:
delaminations
The top ﬁve light grey segments, which are visible in the side-views of the specimens, as seen in Figure 8.1.1,
have been identiﬁed as the 0◦ply, as indicated by the black arrows. The grey segment at the bottom is the
tracer layer, as indicated by the red arrow. The latter is not visible in all of the specimens. The identiﬁcation
of the layers is based upon Table 4.2.1, and the fact that the part has been machined in the direction of the
0◦plies. Thus, the ﬁbres will be viewed in the longitudinal direction. The distinction between the 0◦plies
and the tracer layer was based upon the latter layer appearing more rough and ragged, where the plies are
117
==PAGE:138==
8.1. Open Hole Tension
Chapter 8
smooth with clear edges.
8.1.1
Failure Mechanisms
The test specimens after the tensile tests are shown in Figures 8.1.2, 8.1.3, 8.1.4, 8.1.5, 8.1.6, 8.1.7, 8.1.8, 8.1.9,
8.1.10, 8.1.11, 8.1.12, 8.1.13, 8.1.14, 8.1.15, 8.1.16, 8.1.17 and 8.1.18 where all the specimens have failed at the
hole, thus are acceptable as stated by ASTM 5766. The failure appears to occur along the line of 45◦, which
corresponds to the top ply in the stacking sequence after the OML layer, as seen in Table 4.2.1. The ultimate
failure occurred in all specimens almost instantaneously, with little warning given of the onset of failure,
except for ping noises, characteristic of ﬁbre failure, and slight tearing sounds stemming from delaminations
occurring, which was followed by sudden failure. The mode of failure according to ASTM 5766 is for sample
1 through to sample 17 Angular Lateral Mode (AGM), as illustrated in Figure 3.4.6. All failures occurred at
an angle, which originated from the hole edges, and as the loading increased the damage propagated along
the ﬁbre direction, and ﬁnally reached the entire width of the specimen at an angle to the horizontal. (failure
mechanisms in ﬁbre-reinforced composites).
Figure 8.1.2: Sample 1 after tensile testing
Figure 8.1.3: Sample 2 after tensile testing
118
==PAGE:139==
Chapter 8
8.1. Open Hole Tension
Figures 8.1.2 through to 8.1.18 also illustrate a side-view of the specimens after testing, where there are clear
signs of ﬁbre breakage, extensive splitting and delaminations at the damaged edge and through the width of
the specimen. This is indicated by the white arrow, red box, and blue arrow, respectively. The delaminations
are seen in the form of the individual layers splitting, which is evident in all of the samples. This further
indicates that this was not a clean and brittle fracture, but instead extensive damage appear to have occurred
prior to failure with evidence of ply cracking across the entire width of the sample, especially in the top
plies. All the samples also illustrate ﬁbre breakage across the width, as seen by the gaps in the middle of the
specimens, and indicated by the white arrows. The delaminations appear to have occurred when there have
been a change in the ﬁbre orientation from one layer to the next.
Samples 8 and 9 in Figures 8.1.9 and 8.1.10 show clear evidence of ﬁbre breakout and seem more extensive
in terms of damage in comparison to the others. There does, however, not seem to be any clear correlation
with regards to delamination prior to testing to the extent of damage after testing. The samples have all
failed in a very similar manner, however severe matrix cracking and ﬁbre splitting can be seen in sample 9,
which is also evident in sample 11 and 16. This is indicated by the higher amount of red boxes illustrating
where the matrix have cracked in the transverse and longitudinal direction.

8.1.2
Open Hole Tensile Strength
Figures 8.1.19 and 8.1.20 illustrate the stress-strain curve for all samples, where four diﬀerent graphs have
been made for clarity. Equations 3.4.5 and 3.4.6 have been used to calculate the stress and strain, respectively,
using the geometry of the samples found in Table 4.2.2. For the strain calculations the length, L0 was taken
as the gauge length, and the displacement of the crosshead during testing as the elongation of the sample.
This was done to minimise the dependency of length on the strain calculations, as this was not the same for
all samples, as shown in Section 4.2.1.1 Table 4.2.2. The strength, on the other hand, should not be inﬂuence
by this diﬀerence in geometry. All the samples show a linear increase before ultimate failure, where there
appear to be no change in the slope before this point, except for sample 6, where an increase in both stress
and strain is found after an initial drop, where after failure occurs, as indicated by the green line in Figure
8.1.19.
Figure 8.1.19: Tensile stress-strain curves for samples 1 to 9
There is a noticeable diﬀerence in the amount of strain each sample undergoes before failure, where the lowest
fracture strain can be found for samples 4, 5 and 14. However, these samples are also the longest as seen in
Table 4.2.2. This could indicate a correlation existing between strength and the length of the specimen or due
to other errors associated with the crosshead movement. The results should therefore be considered under
these notions, where an evaluation of the results will be carried out in the subsequent discussion section. In
eﬀort to avoid any errors associated with a connection between length and strain only samples with similar
length will be compared. This includes samples 1,2,3,9,10,11,12,15,16,17, which have a length in the range
124
==PAGE:145==
Chapter 8
8.1. Open Hole Tension
of (84.65-96.04)mm. Here, it can be seen that sample 17, with a length of 84.65 mm has the highest fracture
strain, which was also the control sample. This is followed by sample 9 and 12, where the rest of the samples
have a very similar fracture strain, and are therefore diﬃcult to distinguish. Comparing Figures 8.1.2 through
to 8.1.18, illustrating the delaminations before testing, with the stress-strain curves found in Figures 8.1.19
and 8.1.20, there does not appear to be any correlation between the amount of delamination with regards
to the fracture strain nor the ultimate tensile strength. Here, samples 7, 8, and 9 display the highest tensile
strength with values of 384.5 MPa, 389.3 MPa and 398.3 MPa, respectively, and samples 1,2, 13 and 15 have
the lowest value of 342.6 MPa, 348.1 MPa, 348.5 MPa and 348.5 MPa, respectively.
Figure 8.1.20: Tensile stress-strain curves for samples 10 to 17

the delaminated samples, where also the standard deviations have been found and plotted, with a value of
(372±15)MPa. The y-axis starts at 250 MPa, to clearly show the observed trends, which also the case for the
subsequent ﬁgures in this section.

against the maximum extension of the samples for further analysis of the results, and is illustrated in Figure

8.1.3
Delamination Factor
The conventional and corrected delamination factor was calculated for all samples seen in Figure 4.2.2 prior
to tensile testing, and was based on Equations 3.4.3 and 3.4.4, respectively. The required parameters for
the calculations were obtained using ImageJ as described in Section 3.4.1.1 the and tabulated values can be
found in Appendix B. Figure 8.1.24 illustrates the ultimate tensile strength as a function of the two factors,
where the samples have been arranged according to their delamination value, which is also imprinted on each
column representing the individual sample. The black line is the average ultimate tensile strength based on
the two control specimens, namely samples 13 and 17, where the dotted lines are the standard deviation. The
ﬁgures illustrate that all the specimens have a strength within the range of the control group. There does not
seem to be a correlation between the corrected delamination factor and the ultimate tensile strength of the
samples, as no clear pattern is emerging. Furthermore, all the samples have a higher value than sample 13,
which was one of the control specimens containing no delaminations, although samples 1, 2 and 15 do have a
similar value to this control specimen. The same is found for the delamination factor, as there appear to be
no correlation between the delamination factor and the ultimate tensile strength of the specimens. Sample 8
has one of the highest delamination factors, along with a UTS much above average, which can also be seen
for sample 3 and 6. However, sample 1 and 2 do have the highest delamination factors along with lowest
UTS.
Figure 8.1.24: Corrected delamination factor and delamination factor
In comparing the two graphs in Figure 8.1.24, a slight diﬀerence between them can be observed. However, the
three samples with the highest and lowest delamination factor, respectively, are the same for both factors.
Figure 8.1.25 illustrates the average delamination depth of each sample. This was calculated through the
use of ImageJ, where an image of the specimen prior to testing was loaded into the program. The same
measurement technique as the one described in Section 3.4.1.1 for ﬁnding Dmax was then utilized for each
of the delaminations found on the specimens and an average was calculated. Here, the depth was measured
from the edge of the hole to the end of the delamination.

Figure 8.1.25: Average delamination depth as a function of ultimate tensile strength
The black line on Figure 8.1.25 illustrates the average tensile strength for the control group, where the dotted

average delamination depth, and the second number indicates the number of delaminations. Based on the
ﬁgure there does not seem to a correlation between the average delamination depth and the ultimate tensile
strength nor does there appear to be a correlation between the number of delaminations and the ultimate
tensile strength. Although, all the damaged samples have a higher average delamination depth in comparison
to both control specimens, the tensile strength for these are still within range. Sample 8 has one of the highest
average delamination depths along with the second highest UTS, where sample 15 has one of the lowest
delamination depths along with a small UTS.
8.2
Open Hole Compression
As seen in Section 4.2, issues arose with regards to using the Boeing ﬁxture as stated by ASTM D 6484 due
to specimen geometry. As such, another approach was taken, where six of the eight samples were reduced in
length, so that a test similar to that employed by NASA’s Short Block Compression method could be used.
This lead to satisfying results, and are thus the main focus for the remainder of this study. The remaining two
samples did have the required length for the ﬁxture, which proved enough for a test to be conducted. However,
several problems were found here, which included an uneven thickness due to a core being present in both
samples, but only at one end. This lead to uneven clamping of the samples in the ﬁxture, and distribution of
the load. Although, failure was observed the test was discontinued manually, as it was diﬃcult to detect when
failure had occurred. Based on these ﬁndings, there is a whole range of parameters, which could have lead
to the failure of the specimens and as the core also inﬂuences the strength of the composite, it is diﬃcult to
investigate the inﬂuence of the delamination. As such, the results for these two ﬁndings will be presented at
the end of this chapter with minimal focus being placed on analysing the results in the subsequent chapter.
8.2.1
Failure Mechanisms
Figures 8.2.1 8.2.2, 8.2.3, 8.2.4, 8.2.5 and 8.2.6 illustrate the failure of samples C1 to C6. All the samples have
a similar failure mode when viewed from the top, which can be identiﬁed as Angular Lateral Mode (AGM),
as the failure appears to occur along the 45 ◦ply, as seen for the tensile tests. Furthermore, based on Figure
3.4.9, the failure mechanism can be seen as kink zones and fracture. The failure occurs through the hole,
which is further veriﬁed by viewing the specimens from the side where there are clear signs of fracture in the
128
==PAGE:149==
Chapter 8
8.2. Open Hole Compression
transverse direction across the width of the specimens. Additionally, the characteristic in-plane failure seen in
the form of an angled crack can be observed in all specimens. There are no signs of excessive macro-buckling
nor of end crushing.
The side-view of C1, as shown in Figure 8.2.1, illustrates that delaminations can be found in the 0◦/90◦
interface in most places, however towards the top of the specimen there are also delaminations at the 45◦/90◦
interface. There is not a lot of matrix cracking in the specimen and the damage seems to be contained to
the middle. The ﬁbre cracking is seen throughout the transverse direction of the sample, however only in a
few places.

8.2.2
Open Hole Compressive Strength
Figures 8.2.7 illustrate the stress-strain curve for samples C1 to C6. Here, a complete elastic-linear relationship
between stress and strain can be observed, where there is not a major change in the slope until the sudden
drop, which indicates immediate failure. There, is a slight drop in the stress before compression failure for
sample 6, which indicates that the sample has almost reached ultimate compressive strength, and failure is
about to occur. In the other cases, the failure occurs suddenly, as there is no drop in the slope, but continues
in a linear fashion until ultimate compressive strength has been reached. C5 shows the highest stress, where
C2 has the lowest ultimate tensile strength, as seen from the curve peaks on the graph.
Figure 8.2.7: Stress-strain curve for compression test
Sample C6 has the highest strain, whereas both C1 and C2 have a relatively low strain. However, C3 to C6 have

8.2.3
Delamination Factor
Figure 8.2.9 displays the conventional and corrected delamination factor, which was calculated based upon
Equations 3.4.2 and 3.4.4, respectively, using imageJ to obtain the geometrical parameters. The calculated
values can be found in Appendix B. C3, C5 and C6 display the highest delamination factor both conventional
and corrected. The order has changed slightly between the two factors with C3 displaying the highest Fd
value, but C5 has the largest corrected delamination factor. However, both specimens display a very similar
value in both cases. C3, C5 and C6 also display the highest compressive strength, where C2 has lowest
delamination factors, and also the smallest maximum strength value. This could indicate that there is a
positive correlation between the strength and the amount of surface damaged sustained before testing, based
upon the strength almost linearly increasing with delamination values. This appear most obvious for the
corrected delamination factor.

8.2.4
Samples with Honeycomb Core
Figures 8.2.12 and 8.2.13 illustrate samples C7 and C8 after compression testing, which have both failed
through shear. This is evident from the cross-sectional view of the samples, where a diagonal split moving
across the transverse direction can be observed. This is in contrast to the other specimens, where kink zones
and fracturing was the main failure mode, as seen in Figure 3.4.9. There are indication of delaminations, and
extensive ﬁbre breaking and matrix cracking along the shear failure crack. There are severe delaminations
for C7 at mainly the 0◦/90◦interface, where large pieces of ﬁbre appear to have been separated from the
plies completely. In C8 it is mainly matrix cracking and ﬁbre breakage, which can be observed. The damage
around the hole is more severe in case of C7, where there is a slight angling of the pattern in the 45◦direction,
which is consistent with the orientation of the top ply. The damage around the hole is less severe for C8,
which did not contain any delaminations prior to failure, however there is still a slight indication of the top
ply being orientated at 45◦.

The honeycomb core found in C7 and C8 is shown in Figure 8.2.14, where the uneven thickness of the

Figure 8.2.15 illustrates the stress-strain response of samples C7 and C8, which were both constrained in a
ﬁxture and contained a honeycomb core. For the latter, C8, an initial elastic behaviour can be seen in the form
of a straight line slope, showing little change until ultimate compressive strength is reached. Hereafter, the
stress falls approximately 20 MPa, which is accompanied by a slight increase in strain. This is then followed
by a rise in the curve until failure. The non-linearity can also be seen in C7, where the initial slope is straight
displaying an elastic response to the compressive load, however following this a sharp drop in stress can be
seen of 74 MPa, which is maintained at the same strain of 0.078 mm/mm. Following this, the stress and strain
both starts to increase, however not as steeply as the initial elastic response.
The ultimate compressive strength of C7 was found to be 295 MPa with a conventional delamination factor
of 1.094, and a corrected factor of 1.096. C8 was the control sample, thus both delamination factors were 1
and the ultimate compressive strength was found to 280 MPa.

8.4
Moisture Control
Figure 8.4.1 illustrates the weight percent change with respect to time for three diﬀerent CFRP laminates.
In this experiment the samples were placed in an oven at 190◦C for a time period of 66.7 h, and taken out
at regular intervals to be weighed. The graph shows that the initial weight percent was more than 100%
due to moisture, and then a sudden decrease occurs, which changes to a negative exponential curve , until
4000 min where the curve starts to level out. The same trend is observed for all three specimens, with only
slight changes between them.
The weight percentage change was calculated by assuming the ﬁnal weight of the samples was the cor-
rect, nominal weight, thus all moisture had been removed at this point. This assumption was based on the
fact that the graph started to level out at approximately 3800 min, where only a very small change could be
observed in terms of weight. The nominal weight was then divided by the weight obtained at regular intervals.
