Chapter 9
Discussion of Countersinking Results
This chapter presents an analysis of the countersinking results, namely the ones obtained from the tensile,
compressive and constituents content of composite materials experiments. This is also the order in which
the topics will be introduced, with the initial focus on tensile testing. Here, a discussion on the geometry of
the specimens will be done along with an analysis of the failure mechanisms, the strength and the eﬀect of
delaminations on the mechanical properties. The same structure is used for the next section on compression
testing with the addition of an analysis on the samples with a honeycomb core. This is followed by a discussion
of the results obtained in the constituent content of composite materials test, used to check the void content.
The last section is focused on moisture control of the samples.
9.1
Open Hole Tension
9.1.1
Specimen Geometry
The coupons used for the tensile tests were all made from the multi-directional laminate described in Table
4.2.1, where none of the specimens contained the core, thus should be comparable in terms of material and
stacking sequence. This is essential as it can have a pronounced eﬀect on the strength of the laminate, as
seen in a study by Garcea et al. (2017). The table also highlights the varying lengths of the specimens,
however both the width-to-diameter (W/D) ratio and the diameter-to-thickness (D/T) ratio is the same for
all specimens. In the ASTM 5766, the D/T ratio is speciﬁed as D/T= 1.5-3.0 and the W/D= 6. In this
case all the specimens fulﬁl the latter ratio, but not the former. The W/D requirement is set in place to
limit the stress eﬀect of the hole, thus the purpose is to avoid any contact between the stress concentrations
at the hole and at the edge of the specimen to obtain valid results in terms of strength. However, several
studies have indicated that if the ratio is kept above 3.3, then the results should be the same as if the
ratio had been at the required 6 (Chaterjee et al., 1993), (Khechai et al., 2018). Furthermore, the W/D
ratio is the same for all specimens, which is a key aspect of tensile testing, as investigated and concluded
by (Nilsson et al., 2009). Thus, if the strength was somewhat reduced by using samples with a W/D≈4, a
comparison can still be drawn between the damaged samples and the control as these all have the same ratios.
The length of the specimens are not the same, which is due to a real production part with a complex
geometry being used, as illustrated in Figure 4.2.1. The delaminated holes are all located around the edge
of the part, which further complicated the process of machining samples of equal lengths. However, several
studies have shown that the gauge length does not have any signiﬁcant eﬀect on the strength measurements,
where it was found that the same experimental studies results can be obtained for specimens with diﬀerent
lengths (Ata¸s et al., 2017), (Ou et al., 2017). To conﬁrm the validity of these results obtained from previous
studies, a plot was done illustrating the ultimate tensile strength as a function of length, which is shown in
Figure 8.1.22. Here, no correlation between the strength and varying length was observed, thus a comparison
of the samples can be made regardless of the diﬀerence in geometry. Furthermore, the speciﬁed length in the
(ASTM D-5766M-02a, 2007) for tensile testing is stated as ranging between 200-300 mm, indicating that a
range of lengths are allowable. Lastly, all specimens were checked after failure to ensure that this did not
139
==PAGE:160==
9.1. Open Hole Tension
Chapter 9
occur at the grips, but in the gauge section.
The length however, was shown to have an eﬀect on failure to strain, as found through the statistical
plot in Figure 8.1.23(a), where an almost linear relationship was observed. The strain was calculated based
on the crosshead movement, which provides data on the how far the sample has extended, and the original
assumption was that by using the gauge length for strain calculations any errors associated with using the ma-
chines displacement would be the same for all specimens. This would therefore enable a comparison between
the samples. However, based on Figure 8.1.23(a), the samples appear to have stretched a similar amount
irrespective of their original length, thus when calculating the strain based on this length, a vast diﬀerence can
be seen. This could be because most of the recorded displacement was due to the movement of the machine
and not the sample, which Figure 8.1.23(b) is a good indication of (Ugiansky and Payer, 1979). Here, the
strain was plotted as a function of maximum elongation, which is simply the crosshead movement, not taking
the length into account, and a correlation between the two factors was not observed. The assumption is that
longer specimens are able to stretch further, thus by using Equation 3.4.6 the data should be normalised by
taking into account the length, enabling a comparison between specimens of varying geometries. However,
if the strain recorded by the machine is mainly based on the crosshead movement, then this will lead to
calculations errors for specimens with diﬀerent lengths. Furthermore, errors associated with movement of the
samples as the breaking point is reached can also occur(Berggreen, 2018). Here, any movement of the samples
will further aﬀect the extension measured by the Instron machine, thus provide more scattering of data and
uncertainties, although the embedded displacement errors within the machine should be the same for all
samples. In terms of the samples moving within the grips as the tensile test reaches its ﬁnish, using the gauge
length instead of the actual length of the specimens, should enable a better comparison between the samples.
This is due to the assumption that each sample will have moved the same amount, as the same test machine
and grips were used, and will thus be exposed to the same errors and scattering of the data. Based on these
observations, the results section only focused on a comparison between samples with similar lengths, which
will also be the procedure in the discussion. The obtained results, however, should only be used for com-
parison and not for any ﬁnal calculations due to the errors associated with crosshead movement measurements.
The varying length of the specimens, along with the smaller-than-required W/D ratio, derive from the
fact that these were obtained from an actual F-35 part, as seen in Figure 4.2.1. The reason for using a
production part rather than a test piece, is that the former will be a better representative because a mould
has been used to obtain the unique shape of the part. This could have an inﬂuence on the lay-up, the process
of vacuum bagging prior to curing and post-treatments. Furthermore, the part has been manufactured by
Terma personnel ensuring the correct procedure has been followed. Another reason for maintaining a smaller
W/D ratio stems from the maximum loading capabilities of the Instron instrument used for the mechanical
tests. The highest possible load the machine can provide is 100 kN, and earlier experiments with larger test
samples revealed an inability to reach failure, as the strength of these specimens were too high. Thus, in
order to ensure correct and complete failure when testing, the samples used in this study were made slightly
smaller, but maintaining the same W/D and W/T ratio.
9.1.2
Failure Mechanisms
Figures 8.1.2 to 8.1.18 illustrate the samples after tensile testing, where the failure mode was identiﬁed as
AGM. This is based on the fact that the plies all failed orientated at an angle, which appear to be at 45◦,
consistent with the orientation of the top ply, after the OML coating, as seen in Table 4.2.2. The damage
appear to spread both across and down the side of the specimen, from the hole edge to the free edges,
generating a characteristic pattern. The damage started at the edge of the hole, where splits, also called
matrix cracking, have occurred in the plies, however once the loading increased and delaminations started, the
splits joined up between the layers, which has lead to delaminations, the separation of individual plies. This is
evident when looking at the cross-sectional view of the samples. Here, there is clear signs of matrix cracking
and splits having separated plies in the transverse direction, which has caused delaminations to occur. This
severe delamination continued across the entire width of the specimens and ﬁnally joined at the edges of the
piece (Wisnom, 2012), (Hallett et al., 2009a). Whilst severe damage appear in the top layers in the form of
ﬁbre and matrix cracking, there is also evidence of this occurring in the middle plies, as seen by the large gaps
140
==PAGE:161==
Chapter 9
9.1. Open Hole Tension
in the structure. These appear due to matrix cracking and ﬁbres breaking, resulting in the ply separating
across the width of the specimen, and can be due to the stacking sequence of the laminate. As found in Table
4.2.2, the plies near the core of the matrix are 90◦and 0◦orientated plies, which can act as severe stress
initiators as found by (Hallett et al., 2009a). For all samples, signs of splitting, in the form of large gaps, can
also be found near the top and bottom, and can be a result of stacking 45◦and 90◦plies together, as this can
lead to a delamination triangle forming, which coupled with matrix cracking can result in the observed gaps
(Treasurer, 2006), (Hallett et al., 2009a). The samples show clear signs of ﬁbre breakage and matrix splitting
throughout the entire width, illustrated by the white arrows and red boxes in the ﬁgures, which indicates
that this was not a clean and brittle fracture as can be found in some cases. However, as all the failures oc-
curred according to ASTM standard and no evidence of damage at the grips was observed, the results are valid.
Most of the samples have similar types of failure, where a large gap is evident in the middle due to the combined
eﬀect of all three failure mechanisms, resulting in the ﬁbres cracking under tension and leading to ultimate
failure of the specimens. Samples 2, 9,10,11,14,16 and 18 seem to present larger areas of ﬁbre breakage, which
could indicate that the ﬁbres have lower strength, thus faults are present within the ﬁbres. However, the eﬀect
is not prominent and could also be due to other factors such as the delaminations being present prior to testing.
A signiﬁcant amount of the observed failure mechanisms in sample 9 was due to matrix cracking, which could
be an indication of poor matrix/ﬁbre bonding, as a result of lower than average resin content. This can also
be observed for sample 11 along with sample 16. The reason for this only be seen in a few of the samples
could be due to an uneven spread of resin across the laminate, although some variation is to be expected as
the samples stem from various places on the part.
Comparing the samples before tensile testing, as seen in Figure 4.2.2 with afterwards, illustrated in Fig-
ures 8.1.2 to 8.1.18, there does appear to be any clear correlation between damage prior to failure and the
extent of the damage afterwards. Furthermore, all the samples have the same failure mode and similar failure
mechanisms were observed, indicating that the strongest inﬂuence on failure is the stacking sequence and
general lay-up rather than the introduced delaminations. However, samples 8 and 9 appear to have the most
extensive damage using visual inspection methods, where the appearance of ﬁbre breakage, pull-out and
general damage is more pronounced. The former had one of the highest delamination factors, however sample
9 was roughly in the middle in terms of the calculated corrected delamination factor. As such, there does not
appear to be any correlation between the observed failure mechanisms and surface damage sustained prior
to testing. .
9.1.3
Open Hole Tensile Strength
Figures 8.1.19 and 8.1.20 display the stress-strain curve for every sample. Here, the calculations of the strain
was based on Equation 3.4.6, with the length, L0, taken as the gauge length of the specimen. Due to the
previously discussed errors associated with using the crosshead movement to calculate the extension, and the
subsequent discussion found in Section 9.1.1, the strain measurements are only used for comparing samples of
similar lengths. Eﬀort was made to establish a scaling factor between the lengths, however it was diﬃcult to in-
corporate the error associated with crosshead movement, and which samples simply had a lower fracture strain.
A linear increase in the stress and strain can be found for all samples, excluding sample 6. Hereafter, a
sudden drop in can be observed, which is due to breakage of the samples resulting in ultimate failure. Both
the matrix and ﬁbres in this case are linear elastic until failure, however the matrix will fail at a lower strain
in comparison to the ﬁbre, due the epoxy resin being brittle, as found by (Pilling, 2018). The sudden failure
observed during tensile testing for composite materials is diﬀerent to metals, where plastic deformation will
occur after the elastic region, leading to a slower failure, which can more easily be controlled. For sample 6,
after the initial drop in stress and at the ﬁrst failure strain, both parameters started to increase again, as
the sample was stretched further. An explanation for this is that, although the matrix will have failed at the
initial drop in stress, the ﬁbres are still stretched by the same strain, so the stress in the ﬁbres till remain
the same, however due to the matrix failure, the overall load on the composite will continue to drop until
failure (Pilling, 2018).
141
==PAGE:162==
9.1. Open Hole Tension
Chapter 9
As discussed in Section 9.1.1, using the crosshead movement for calculating the strain has presented some
diﬃculties in that most of the recorded data appear to have been from the movement of the machine and
not the specimen. As such, following the same structure as in the results section, the discussion on strain
will only be on samples of similar lengths, namely samples 1,2,3,9,10,11,12,15,16 and 17. This should still
be a large enough sample size to draw valid conclusions from and also includes a control specimen, namely
number 17. It was found that sample 17 had the highest strain before failure, which means more energy was
absorbed by the specimen before failure. Otherwise, all the samples have a very similar value of failure strain,
which illustrates that the surface delaminations does not have an eﬀect on strain. This is further conﬁrmed
by using Figure 4.2.2 as a guideline to the extent of delaminations and comparing this to the strain rate,
there does not seem to be any correlation between the samples ability to absorb energy before failure and the
amount of delaminations. A plausible explanation is that the surface delaminations are not severe enough
to result in a reduction of strain. This illustrates that it might not be necessary for parts containing surface
damage to go through the extensive process of repair if further non-destructive tests reveals no sub-surface
damage to be present.
Figures 8.1.2 to 8.1.18 represents the samples after testing, where there were found to be extensive damage
in the form of matrix splitting and ﬁbre cracking. Furthermore, inter-laminar delaminations could also be
seen. Despite this, no change can be observed in the slope of the stress-strain graph in Figures 8.1.19 and
8.1.20, although there are clear indications of substantial damage having occurred. This could be due to the
laminate containing ﬁve 0◦plies, which adds longitudinal strength and stiﬀness and is the direction of the
tensile stretching (Callister, 2007).
Figure 8.1.21 shows that all the samples have a tensile strength within range of the average calculated
based on the two control samples, namely number 13 and 17. Further, a signiﬁcant amount of the samples
have a higher tensile strength than the average, which can be explained by the fact that the surface damage
can act as a stress relief when the geometry of the hole is changed, which leads to an ability to tolerate higher
stresses before failure. In comparing Figure 8.1.21 with Figures to 8.1.18, there does not appear to be a clear
trend between the amount and extent of damage after failure to the tensile strength of the samples.
9.1.4
Delamination Factor
Figure 8.1.24 illustrates the tensile strength as a function of the calculated corrected delamination factor
(Fda) and the conventional factor (Fd) for all specimens. There is no indication of a correlation between the
factors and the ultimate tensile strength of the samples. Furthermore, samples 10, 11, 5, 9, 12, 4, 6, 3 and 8,
arranged in ascending order in terms of damage severity, all have a relatively high Fda along with the highest
ultimate tensile strength. This can be explained by the fact that delaminations around the hole can act as a
stress relief leading to higher strength. The artiﬁcially introduced delaminations in the vicinity of the hole
for each specimen can be seen as intra-laminar damage, as separation occurred within the same ply. This
type of damage was found by O’Higgins et al. (2010) to act as a stress relief, further conﬁrming the previous
explanation. This is due to a change in the hole geometry resulting in a reduction of the stress concentration,
thus increasing the strength, where larger damage zones typically provide more stress relief and therefore
a higher strength. This is further conﬁrmed by the obtained results in Figure 8.1.24, where a considerable
amount of the samples with a high delamination factor also have a high ultimate tensile strength. However,
some samples such as 1, 2 and 15, display a relatively large delamination factor along with a low strength,
which is below the calculated average. This is particularly evident for samples 1 and 2. This could be related
to interlaminar delaminations, as this can lead to a reduction in strength due to the uncoupled plies suddenly
being independent of each other, and therefore able to fail by the fracture mode oﬀering the least resistance
(O’Higgins et al., 2010). Furthermore, the uncoupled plies does not receive any support from surrounding
layers. In order to conﬁrm such a hypothesis further testing would be needed in form of non-destructive
testing, which would be able to detect any such damage.
Samples 13 and 17 were the control specimens containing no delaminations. Sample 13 is the shortest
sample, and initially this was thought to be a signiﬁcant contribution in terms of it having the lowest ultimate
142
==PAGE:163==
Chapter 9
9.2. Open Hole Compression
tensile strength. However, as explained in Section 9.1.1, the length should not have an impact on the strength,
and further studies have indicated that a shorter length could result in a higher strength. Based on this, the
sample is not disregarded, but seen as being representative of a standard part containing no delaminations,
along with sample 17. Comparing the two delamination factors in Figure 8.1.24, there does not appear to be
any signiﬁcant diﬀerence in the order of delamination factor. However, the calculations based on Fda are higher
in comparison to the conventional factor, which could indicate the former being a better representative of the
extent of the delaminations as this takes the entire damaged area into account. Furthermore, as illustrated
in Figure 4.2.2, the patterns of delamination are irregular for most samples, which the conventional factor
does not consider and is further conﬁrmed by the lower values obtained using this factor (Davim et al., 2007).
All the samples lie within the standard deviation of the control group, which indicates the delaminations
sustained prior to testing does not aﬀect the strength of the composite and should not lead to any premature
failure.
The average delamination depth for each specimen was also calculated in case this had a signiﬁcant ef-
fect on the tensile strength and could be used as an indicator for determining premature failure. However,
as seen in Figure 8.1.25 there does not appear to be any trend between the average depth and the ultimate
tensile strength. The ascending order in terms of depth is very similar to the one seen for both delamination
factors indicating that this could also be used as a measure of delamination damage.
Common for all measurements made in terms of delaminations is that the extent of the damage was diﬃcult
to capture accurately in ImageJ. This was due to some samples containing very irregular areas, which was
more diﬃcult to trace when calculating the delaminated area as needed for the Fda. Furthermore, determining
the beginning of a delamination was also an issue, as in certain cases the top ply had not been completely
removed, resulting in the delamination depth being less deﬁned. In order to conﬁrm the observations that
there is no correlation between damaged sustained prior to testing and the strength further tests are needed.
This could be in the form of ultra-sonic tests, where sub-surface damage can be detected and rule out as a
source of failure.
9.2
Open Hole Compression
9.2.1
Specimen Geometry
The coupons used for the compression tests were made from a multi-directional laminate with the lay-up
sequence illustrated in Table 4.2.1. Two of the samples were 304.8 mm, which was based on the requirements
of the ﬁxture as seen in ASTM 6484, where the remaining six all had a length of 50.8 mm. The reason for the
diﬀerence in size was due to the available material of the part, where smaller specimens had to be made in
order to reach a reasonable sample size. Initially, the smaller samples all had slight varying lengths, but were
ﬁtted inside the ﬁxture along with extra pieces of composite material to ﬁll out the ﬁxture. However, during
testing the extra pieces inside the ﬁxture fractured ﬁrst, thus the test was incomplete. As such, it was deemed
that the best way forward was to machine the smaller parts down to a length of 50.8 mm. In validation
of doing so, a similar procedure was found with the Short Block Compression (SBC) test as introduced by
(Chaterjee et al., 1993), thus the method was deemed adequate. The shortness, and thickness of the samples
lead to more stability, and care was taken to make the surfaces completely parallel. The largest problem with
this test is end-crushing, as the specimens are simply placed between two plates without any end support.
However, this occurrence was not observed for any of the specimens after testing, as veriﬁed in the subsequent
section. Furthermore, the adequate thickness of the specimens, which was roughly 5 mm for all samples, is
another prevention measure of this happening. All the samples, both long and short, had a width-to-diameter
ratio of approximately 5, along with a diameter-to-thickness ratio of 1.5, which was the speciﬁed requirement
of ASTM D-6484M-04 (2015) and can be seen in Table 4.2.3. The former ratio requirement is actually stated
as 6 in the standard, however studies by (Lee and Soutis, 2009) have conﬁrmed that a ratio of 5 is enough.
Additionally, it was found that the width-to-diameter ratio was the most important aspect of the compression
test, where the length did not have an inﬂuence of the obtained tensile strength nor the strain. Instead
any reduction in strength was found to be due to a change in hole size rather than specimen thickness or
volume increase. The dimensions of the shorter samples as being adequate for compression testing was further
143
==PAGE:164==
9.2. Open Hole Compression
Chapter 9
conﬁrmed by Chaterjee et al. (1993).
In terms of the two long pieces, the width was not to correct dimensions, which meant that extra pieces
had to be placed inside the ﬁxture along with the specimens. After lightly tightening the ﬁxture bolts, the
extra piece were removed. However, both pieces contained some of the honeycomb core seen in the stacking
sequence in Table 4.2.1, meaning the pieces were thicker in one end. This lead to a slight movement of the
sample when removing the spacer, as the bolts could not be tighten as hard in the thinner end. This meant an
unstable set-up inside the ﬁxture, as an even pressure could not be provide from the ﬁxture onto the specimen.
Another issue which arose, was the length being slightly longer than the ﬁxture, which lead to the crosshead
not being completely parallel with the ﬁxture. This could have induced shearing instead of compression, which
is a common phenomenon. Although, failure was observed, the results were interesting, thus it was decided
to base the main conclusions on the results obtained with the shorter specimens. Furthermore, the SynCore
core will have an inﬂuence on the ﬁnal compression results, as this could stop delaminations from occurring,
which makes it diﬃcult to ﬁgure out whether the results are based on delaminations, the honeycomb core
or faults of the ﬁxture. Further tests of this kind would have to be conducted to reach a valid conclusion.
However, as the results might prove some insight into the testing method and the eﬀect of the core, they
have been included at the end of this chapter.
9.2.2
Failure Mechanisms
Figures 8.2.1, 8.2.2, 8.2.3, 8.2.4, 8.2.5 and 8.2.6 illustrate the outcome of the compression test, where the
mode of failure was through the hole at an angle of 45◦, which corresponds to the top ply after the OML
layer. Literature have shown that if the top layers consist of ±45◦plies, then arrowheads will form along the
direction of compression fracture beginning at the notch, as found by Greenhalgh (2009). However, this is
not observed in this case, which could be due to the OML layer.
All six samples displayed similar failure modes, namely ﬁbre fracture (white arrow), ply splitting (red box),
also referred to as matrix cracking, and delaminations (blue arrow) in the form of separation of individual
plies. The ﬁbre fracture occurred in the 0◦plies, which can be seen by the splitting of the grey segments
indicated by the white arrows, and is in agreement with the ﬁndings of Pinnell et al. (1993). The fracture
appears to have occurred in all of the 0◦plies for all samples, except in the case of C1, where ﬁbre fracture
have appeared in four of the plies. Fibre instability is the cause of failure in the 0◦, and is one of the reasons
for the overall compressive failure of the laminate, as seen in studies by Greenhalgh (2009) and M.R.Wisnom
and J.W.Atkinson (1997). Here, the damage is limited to a localised line along the fracture plane, thus
across the width of the specimen. The 0◦plies are load bearing, and once these failed the ±45◦plies will
follow, however these are constrained by the surrounding plies during compression, which can be seen in
the form of cracking through the ±45◦plies in Figures 8.2.1, 8.2.2, 8.2.3, 8.2.4, 8.2.5 and 8.2.6, indicated
by the red arrow. Comparing the specimens in terms of ﬁbre fracture, it was observed in Section 8.2 that
C1, C2, and C6 only had minor damage in terms of severity of fracturing, although all plies in the 0◦had
fractured leading to the ultimate failure of the specimen. C4 had the worst ﬁbre splitting, with severe and
extensive damage in the middle, where the ﬁbres have been pushed far apart in the direction parallel to
the ﬁbres due to the compression motion. This is also seen for C5 although not as severe. In comparing the
delaminations before the experiment to the severity of damage sustained after compression, there does seem
to be a slight indication that there is a correlation between them. Both C1 and C2 had the least amount
of delaminations prior to testing, and also presented a low amount of compressive damage. C3 had the
highest quantity of delaminations prior to testing, which was also the case afterwards. However, C4 did not
present severe delamination before testing, but presented severe ﬁbre fracture after compressive failure, which
indicates no strong correlation between delamination prior to testing to ﬁbre fracture afterwards. As such, the
diﬀerences between the severity of compression failure could be due to small discontinuities in the ﬁbres or
small microscopic cracks, which leads to a more harsh failure, although this might not present itself in terms of
strength. Another reason could be due to misalignment of ﬁbres, which can also change the outcome of the test.
All the samples showed signs of ply splitting, or matrix cracking, which was seen as transverse splitting
through the 90◦plies and in the ±45◦layers. Here, the cracks moved through the individual layers, but was
144
==PAGE:165==
Chapter 9
9.2. Open Hole Compression
also observed across the width of the specimen. Ply splitting can also be seen around the notch in the top
layer, as indicated by the red box. Generally, the specimens presents an equal amount of splitting both in the
transverse and longitudinal direction, which was identiﬁed based on Figure 3.4.13, showing common failure
mechanisms. In most cases, longitudinal splitting seems to be the most common, where C1 and C3 presents
the highest amount, with C2, C4 and C6 the lowest. This does not correlate to the amount of delaminations
on the specimens beforehand, as C2 had almost no surface damage prior to testing, where C3 had a signiﬁcant
amount. A reason for the observed diﬀerence in damage sustained due to compression can be related to
defects being present in the matrix and ﬁbres before testing. Failures due to matrix cracking can be explained
in terms of the adhesive strength between the ﬁbres and matrix. If the resin content is lower than expected, as
discussed in Section 3.4.3, this could lead to cracking of the layers. Uncontrolled resin bleeds can occur during
the autoclaving procedure, described in Section 3.1.3.2. During the initial stages of this process any excess
resin will be removed from the composite part, so called resin bleed, and will be captured by the bleeder fabric.
If this material is too thick, it can result in a higher amount of resin being absorbed, thus a lower content can
be found in the ﬁnal composite part. This does not occur homogeneously across the entire part, which could
explain the diﬀerence seen in terms of matrix cracking, as all the specimens stem from the same part, thus
the matrix should have a similar stiﬀness and strength along with bonding capabilities (Ganapathi et al., 2013).
Delaminations, which is the separation of plies, was observed in all of the specimens in a range of severity,
and were mainly located in the 0◦/90◦and 45◦/90◦interface. This illustrates that delaminations tend to
occur when there is a change in ﬁbre orientation between individual plies, as found by other studies seen
in Section 3.4.2.2. The delaminations are due to a change in stiﬀness because of various ﬁbre orientations
presenting diﬀerent stiﬀness. When a compressive load is applied certain orientations will fail ﬁrst, resulting
in buckling of these layers, which means a separation from the stiﬀer plies that will continue to resist the
compressive load. This can then lead to multiple delaminations, as observed in all the specimens, along with
global fracture culminating in ultimate failure of the specimen (Tsampas, 2013), (Greenhalgh, 2009). It is
hard to deduce from the images whether ply splitting induced the delaminations or occurred as a result of
other types of failure such as micro-buckling, also referred to as kinking. However, cracks can be found in
the vicinity of delaminations, which could indicate that this is the order of events (Tsampas, 2013). Under
any circumstances, the failure of the specimens will have been a result of multiple failure modes. Almost no
delaminations appeared in C1 nor C2, where only a slight separation of a few plies can be seen. Cracks are
present near the delaminations, however for C2 severe cracking of the matrix can also be observed without
delaminations. C3 and C4 show severe separation of the layers on one side, which has extended across the
entire width of the specimen. This could indicate that poor adhesion has existed between these two layers
due to low resin content resulting in the layers splitting more easily. C5 sees severe delaminations in two of
the 0◦/-45◦interfaces, and in the 0◦/90◦interface. C6 sees an almost complete separation of the -45◦/45◦
plies, where matrix cracking appear to have initiated this as indicated by the almost square red box furthest
to the right at the bottom of Figure 8.2.6. A range of observed delamination types for each of the samples
was made, however all of them exhibit the characteristic fracture in the middle as shown in Figure 3.4.14.
Lastly, there appear to be no correlation between the amount of delaminations as a result of compression,
and the surface damage introduced to the specimens before testing. Samples C3 and C4 presented the most
severe compressive delaminations, where only C3 had a signiﬁcant amount of surface damage prior to testing.
In terms of buckling of the specimens, which could have accelerated failure and lead to inconclusive re-
sults, this does not appear to have occurred, although C5 does show slight signs of bending due to the
compression. However, this sample did not present any other symptoms of early failure, such as a lower
strength or strain to failure. As such, all specimens were found to have failed according to ASTM D6484.
As seen from the above discussion, compressive failure is not due to one single failure mechanism, but
rather a combination of delamination, ﬁbre fracture and matrix cracking. Many of the individual lamina have
failed at a slight angle through the thickness and the in-plane failure, which is evidence of micro-buckling,
occurred in all cases, resulting in a correct compression failure. Delaminations were also observed in several
of the plies, along with ﬁbre cracking in multiple places and failure has led to bulging around the countersunk
hole. The surface damage on the specimens prior to testing did not appear to have any eﬀect on the failure
mechanisms nor the severity of them.
145
==PAGE:166==
9.2. Open Hole Compression
Chapter 9
9.2.3
Open Hole Compressive Strength
Figure 8.2.7 illustrates the stress-strain response of the shortest specimens, namely C1 to C6. An elastic
linear response can be seen until the onset of sudden and catastrophic failure as indicated by the fall in stress.
Just before failure there appears to be slight decrease in the curve slope, which is most prominent on C6,
and could be an indication of the beginning of severe damage culminating in termination of the test. This
is in line with the standard compressive response, as seen in Figure 3.4.8, and indicates that the strength
of the matrix/ﬁbre interface is good, as there are no signiﬁcant curvature in the stress-strain curves. This
also serves to highlight that failure appears to have occurred due to a combination of delamination, ﬁbre
cracking and ply splitting, and not prematurely ended by end-crushing as can be seen in shorter specimens
with no support ﬁxture (Chaterjee et al., 1993). This means the measured strength is indeed an indication
of the material and not as a result of sample size or set-up. The highest strength can be found for C5 and
C6, where C2 is displaying the lowest strength. C4 and C3 are roughly in the middle, although towards the
higher end in comparison to the control C1, which indicates that the severe separation of plies as seen in
Figures 8.2.3 and 8.2.4 did not have an eﬀect on the compressive test results.
The failure to strain is highest for the C6 samples, and lowest for the control C1 and the delaminated
C2, where there seems to be a noticeable gap between the two latter and the rest of the specimens. One
explanation for this could be in terms of ply thickness, as observed by both Amacher et al. (2014) and
Leopold et al. (2017). Here, it was found that thinner ply laminates exhibited higher maximum failure strains
and strength in comparison to thicker plies. Although, the specimens all stem from the same part, small
variations in the plies due to manufacturing process and the subsequent lay-up, can lead to varying thickness,
as seen in Table 4.2.3, illustrating the geometry of the specimens. If the C3,C4, C5 and C6 specimens all
contain slightly thinner plies, this can lead to an increase in maximum strength and strain before failure.
Although, the overall thickness of the specimens are actually higher for these four, this could be explained
by the fact that less of the sacriﬁcial layers were removed during machining. Sub-surface cracks could also
lead to a reduction in ultimate strain and strength, as shown by O’Higgins et al. (2010) and Liu et al. (2017).
These sub-surface damages will not have been apparent when conducting the test, but has a signiﬁcant eﬀect
on the outcome. This could also further explain the lower strength of C2 in comparison to the other specimens.
Comparing Figures 8.2.2, 8.2.3, 8.2.4, 8.2.6, and 8.2.7, there does not appear to be a correlation between
the surface damage sustained prior to testing and the ultimate stress and strain. This is based on C3 and
C5 having sustained the highest amount of surface damage prior to testing, however C5 also presents the
highest strength and strain, followed by C6, which had the third highest amount of delamination prior to
testing. It was shown by Clarke (1997) and stated in ASTM D-6484M-04 (2015), that delaminations can
blunt the stress concentration caused by the hole, thus increasing the force carrying capacity of the specimens
and the resultant stress and strain. This does not appear to be in line with the observations made through
compression testing, although the three samples with the highest strength also contained the largest amount
of surface delaminations sustained prior to the test.
In comparing the ultimate tensile and compression strength a signiﬁcant diﬀerence can be found, where
the lowest strength obtained through tensile testing is 27% higher in comparison to the lowest strength for
compression. Analysing the highest strength for both cases a reduction of 28% can be found for compressive
strength. This is in agreement with the several studies, and is mainly due to the diﬀerent failure modes
observed for each method, with ﬁbre failure during tension being the most common versus micro-buckling
for compression Clarke (1997), Treasurer (2006). Based on these observations, the compressive strength is
the most important parameter in terms of strength-design safety, ensuring that the part can withstand the
required loads during ﬂight (Ahn et al., 2013).
9.2.4
Delamination Factor
Figure 8.2.9 illustrates the calculated conventional and corrected delamination factor. For the former, there
appears to be an increase in strength as the delamination damage increases until reaching C5, where after
the strength falls with an increase in delamination factor. When solely evaluating the delaminated samples,
there does seem to be a positive correlation between strength and the delamination factor. However, when
146
==PAGE:167==
Chapter 9
9.2. Open Hole Compression
taking the standard deviation of the delaminated samples into account, the values all fall within the range of
the control strength as determined by C1, which indicates that the trend is simply due to normal scattering
of the data. This was conﬁrmed in the scatter plot, where the ﬁt was extremely poor, and serves to highlight
that there is not a correlation between strength and delaminations sustained prior to testing. A possible
explanation for the surface damage not causing a reduction in strength, quite the contrary, could be due to
one delamination dominating in the compression failure process, which is highly inﬂuenced by this mode of
failure. If a delamination close to the centre of the specimen buckles out the tendency for a delamination to
occur at the surface is reduced. This observation is conﬁrmed by the visual analysis in Section 8.2, where
signiﬁcant and large amounts of delaminations could be found in the middle ply for specimens C3 to C6, where
smaller amounts were found for C2, albeit still near the centre. As such, the growth of delaminations under
compressive loading will play a signiﬁcant role in determining the ultimate compressive strength (Clarke,
1997). Furthermore, as described in (ASTM D-6484M-04, 2015), delaminations and ply splitting can result
in diminishing the stress concentration caused by the presence of a hole, thus increasing the strength of the
specimen. Studies have also indicated that sub-surface delaminations and other defects not visible thorough
optical inspections, are mainly the cause of a reduction in strength, rather than surface damage, as discovered
by O’Higgins et al. (2010) and Liu et al. (2017). The depth of damage also plays a role in determining
how quickly delaminations grow, where specimens with delaminations closer to the surface, and therefore
a thinner layer to buckle under compressive pressure, will grow more quickly. As the surface damage was
in the uppermost ply in all cases, there will not have been any layers above to initiate the quick growth of
delaminations in this ply (Clarke, 1997).
C2 is displaying the lowest amount of compressive strength, but also had the least amount of surface damage
prior to testing. Although, delaminations in the top ply could lead to a reduction in strength, this does
not coincide with the results obtained for the other delaminated samples. As such, this could indicate that
low impact damage in the form of less than proper countersinking has lead to subsurface damage such as
delaminations or cracks, which could explain the low strength. Thus, further non-destructive testing would
be needed.
9.2.5
Samples with Honeycomb Core
Figures 8.2.12 and 8.2.13 illustrates the failure mode of both specimens to be shearing, which is in contrast
to the formation of kink zones and fractures observed in the other specimens, however both failure modes
are valid. Studies have found that the latter, as seen in C7 and C8, is actually also associated with ﬁbre
buckling along with kinking and referred to as shear crippling (de Paiva et al., 2003). The two are diﬃcult
to distinguish as macroscopically they look very similar. Microscopic inspection, however indicates shear
crippling is frequently the result of kink band formation. This is also in agreement with previous studies
investigating the initiation of compressive failure and found micro-buckling to be the main failure mechanism,
as found in Section 3.4.2.2. One such study was conducted by Prabhakar et al. (2015), where failure modes
during compression testing of CFRP specimens were investigated. It was found that ﬁbre misalignment due
to compression coupled with a slight shear lead to the formation of a kink band. The mechanisms associated
with this failure are very dependent upon the geometry and volume of the surrounding material. In both
samples, the fracture have occurred at an angle, and propagated down through the specimen until ﬁnal failure.
However, in both cases, the failure was not sudden and it was hard to detect when maximum stress and
strain had been reached. Upon inspection of the samples, both appeared to have failed correctly, as shearing
has occurred across the width of the samples.
In terms of ultimate compressive strength and strain, as shown in Figure 8.2.15, C7 presents the high-
est ultimate compressive strength and failure to strain, where C8 was the control. This could indicate that the
surface delaminations do indeed blunt the stress concentration at the hole and provide stress relief, however
more testing would be required to state anything conclusive, as the values are not too far apart. Furthermore,
a variation in the strength was also observed for the shorter samples, where no correlation was found between
delaminations and strength. This indicates a range of strength values are to be expected. Observing the
stress-strain curves for C7 and C8 in Figure 8.2.15, initially a linear response can be seen, however this is
followed by a non-linear drop for both specimens. Here, C8 presents a gradual decrease, whereas the curve
147
==PAGE:168==
9.3. Constituents Content of Composite Materials
Chapter 9
for C7 shows a very small drop and then a sudden drop maintained at the same strain. This can be explained
based on Figure 3.4.8(b) illustrating a stress-strain curve for a laminate containing an insert (Lemanski
et al., 2013). After the peak load, micro-buckling starts to occur in the ﬁbres contained in the epoxy matrix
closest to the honeycomb core, and leads to a further reduction of the load. The micro-buckling results in
a separation of the honeycomb core from the laminate, thus delaminations are encountered. This causes
micro-buckling of the other layers further away from the core, eventually leading to ultimate failure. The
waviness of the curves could potentially be due to how the layers are separating from the core, whether this
occurs suddenly, as in C7, or more gradually as seen in C8.
Comparing C8 and C7 to the shorter specimens, a higher compressive strength can be found for the former,
and as both groups displayed a correct type of failure mode, this must be related to either the delaminations
or the structure of the material. In both groups, no clear correlation was observed between delaminations
obtained prior to testing and strength, which is harder to deduce from C7 and C8 as there are only two
specimens here. However, as length nor ﬁxture should have an inﬂuence on the strength of the composite, the
conclusions with regards to delamination and strength correlation, can be based on samples C1 to C6 for both
groups, and there is found to be no dependency. As such, the higher strength must stem from the core seen in
C7 and C8, which is further conﬁrmed by the formation of kink bands and the subsequent compressive failure
being dependent upon the surrounding materials. C7 and C8 had extra support in the form of a honeycomb
core, which provides extra strength during compression and bending, as described by Henkel (2017). The
core resists the shear loads and provides support to the face sheets to increase their critical buckling stresses
under bending or axial compression. Although, CFRP composites have exceptional strength on their own,
adding a core seems to provide even further strengthening.
9.3
Constituents Content of Composite Materials
Table 8.3.1 illustrates that the data obtained in the experiment is very reliable as there is not much variation
across the specimens for each data group. The samples weight prior to testing is slightly diﬀerent, which is
to be expected as the samples had to be cut into small pieces using a large saw-tooth diamond blade, thus
accuracy was diﬃcult to obtain at such a small scale. This, however, does not appear to have aﬀected the
results. The calculated values for the density of the composites were made more accurate by taking into
account the temperature of the water in which they were weighed, which does make a slight diﬀerence to the
overall calculations particular at such small values as the void content was found to be. Some variation can
be found in the ﬁbre weight for each sample, which perfectly correlates with the specimens weight prior to
testing, and further indicates that the test results are valid.
Figure 8.3.1 illustrates the ﬁbre and matrix volume percent content, which was calculated based upon
the values in Table 8.3.1. The resin content is lower than the ﬁbre content, which is to be expected for all
samples based on the manufacturing process, where the ﬁbres provide the strength of the composite, and
the matrix is needed to bind the ﬁbres together. As such, a high carbon ﬁbre content is desirable in order to
make the composite as strong and stiﬀas possible, as this is not provided by the matrix. The variation across
the specimens were found to be low, indicating that the resin and ﬁbres are spread evenly throughout the
composite, ensuring that the strength and stiﬀness are the same everywhere. This is essential as any large
areas containing only one constituent lead to a severe reduction in the mechanical properties, although the
void content is a better indication of this, which can be found in Figure 8.3.2. All the values are below the 1%
requirement of the aerospace industry, which illustrates that there is an even distribution of ﬁbre and matrix
content across the entire part, as the samples were taken at diﬀerent placements to try and capture any voids,
which might be present only in some areas (Liu et al., 2006). The low values also indicate that any surface
delaminations observed during machining, such as slot milling and countersinking, should not stem from a
high void content. If a composite part contains a signiﬁcant amount of voids, this could demonstrate that the
resin has ”bleed out” during manufacturing, essentially leaving the composite, and resulting in poor binding
capabilities, as there are less material available to hold the ﬁbres together (Ganapathi et al., 2013). This
in turn results in easier splitting of the plies, seen in the form of delaminations, and matrix cracking. The
low values also signiﬁes that any reduction in strength during mechanical testing should not stem from void
148
==PAGE:169==
Chapter 9
9.4. Moisture Control
content. Generally, for delaminated structures which are subjected to compressive forces a higher amount of
voids will lead to more delaminations resulting in early termination of the test. A severe reduction in stiﬀness
has also been linked to a high void content.
Sample 4 has the highest void content percentage of 0.29%, along with the lowest ﬁbre volume content.
This could be due to slight misalignment of the ﬁbres, meaning that in certain areas the content will be
slightly lower, and if the matrix volume remains the same, this will mean more voids. However, sample 4 also
has the highest matrix volume, so a more likely explanation is that during testing some of the ﬁbres were
lost due to errors. During testing, after the hydrogen peroxide had been added and the beakers placed back
on the hotplate, a reaction occurred between the condensed water on the stirring rod and the mixture in the
glass beaker. The samples were quickly removed from the hotplate, and no mixture appeared to have escaped
during the small eruption. However, the ﬁbres can be hard to detect, and this could explain the lower ﬁbre
volume and the higher void content of sample 4. The values are all still within range, and some variation is
to be expected in the samples as these were taking from diﬀerent places on the part. Furthermore, all values
are well below the requirements of void content as set by the aerospace industry to ensure a good quality part.
Some studies have indicated that the void content by itself is inadequate to explain the eﬀect of voids.
Instead, factors such as size and shape of voids, as well as their location, must be considered. In fact the
eﬀect of voids and other defects such as ﬁbre waviness and irregular ﬁbre distribution in initiating damage
and the interaction. As such, it is important to consider various aspects of a manufacturing process and how
diﬀerent errors might lead to faults in the part.
9.4
Moisture Control
To validate the need for keeping the samples in an oven at 120◦C for an extent period of time a moisture
control test was done, as seen in Figure 8.4.1. Here, it was found that there is a need for keeping the samples
in the oven, as a reduction in weight did occur for the time period shown. After 168 h the graph levelled
out for all three specimens indicating that only an insigniﬁcant amount of moisture is left in the specimens.
In terms of moisture and its eﬀect on mechanical properties, several studies have noted a degradation in
performance for samples containing high moisture contents, which is due to its negative eﬀect on the chemical
properties of the CFRP composite. Here, a change in moisture content can induce swelling, which modiﬁes
the residual stresses in the material in addition to the thermal stresses introduced during curing. This all
leads to a reduction of strength. Had the samples not been placed in the oven for an extent period of time,
this could have lead to incorrect results, although the test also indicates that this is something, which should
be investigated further.
149
==PAGE:170==
==PAGE:171==
Chapter 10
Conclusion and Future Work
10.1
Conclusion
10.1.1
Slot milling
The aim of the slot milling experiment was to investigate the eﬀect of machining parameters and tool wear
on the surface quality of CFRP composites. Furthermore, by utilising a four-ﬂute end mill from Unimerco
and an eight-ﬂute serrated end mill from Franken Fiber-Cut the eﬀect of tool geometry was also considered.
The obtained results could then eﬀectively be used to ﬁnd the optimum machining parameters. To evaluate
the surface quality, two parameters were calculated based on the roughness measurements, namely Ra and
R10z, where the literature study showed the latter to be superior when evaluating composites. This is due to
Ra showing limited variation with respect to ﬁbre orientation, thus will not give an accurate representation
of the roughness. Because of this, the discussions on surface quality and the optimum tooling conditions
were mainly based upon the R10z value. Ra was included as this can be used to evaluate whether the
roughness values fall below the accepted limit of 3.2 µm set by the aerospace industry. The literature review
showed the feed rate to be a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on surface roughness, where the eﬀect of spindle speed was
less conclusive. This is in agreement with the obtained results for both Franken Fiber-Cut and Unimerco,
where generally an overall increase in roughness with feed rate was found for a new and worn tool. This
was explained as being due to larger chips being produced per tooth, resulting in higher cutting forces, thus
a raise in surface roughness being observed. However, for Unimerco the intermediate feed rate resulted in
the best surface quality, which was also found to be the case for a few previous studies. The higher surface
roughness observed at the lowest feed rate for the two highest spindle speeds could be due to thermal damage.
Studies have found that excessive heat can be generated at signiﬁcantly low feed rates resulting in surface
damage such as ﬁbre pull-outs and cavities. Based on the observed roughness, this appears to have been the
case. Changing the feed rate from mid-level to maximum also saw an increase in roughness. A high feed
rate promotes the generation of heat between the tool and surface causing accelerated tool wear along with
the formation of a larger chip thickness. This in turn will lead to severe surface damage in the form of pits,
matrix burning and ﬁbre pull-outs. In line with the observed increase in roughness, this seems to be a valid
explanation. The low roughness observed at the intermediate feed rate could be a result of a lower generation
of heat, resulting in less surface damage coupled with a smaller contact time between tool and material. The
spindle speed did not show a clear trend for neither tools. Franken Fibre-Cut revealed a higher roughness
for the worn tool in comparison to the new, which was also the general conclusion reached in the literature
review, due to the worn tool presenting higher cutting forces resulting in a lower surface quality. Unimerco
displayed a higher roughness for the worn tool in the longitudinal direction, however for the transverse the
opposite was observed. The literature studies revealed several experimental investigations reaching the same
conclusion, due to the excessive heat generated by the worn tool leading to matrix smearing resulting in
a smoother surface. The literature study also revealed the transverse direction generally displayed higher
roughness values, due to the measurements being made across several plies, which is in agreement with the
experimental outcome for a new and worn Unimerco end mill, and with the worn Franken Fibre-Cut. On
the contrary, the transverse direction displayed a lower roughness for the new Franken Fibre-Cut, which was
151
==PAGE:172==
10.1. Conclusion
Chapter 10
also highlighted in the literature review, and is because of the more prominent eﬀect of tooling marks in the
longitudinal direction. The signiﬁcance of tool geometry can be found when comparing the roughness results
for Franken Fibre-Cut with Unimerco, where the former presented the lowest roughness. This was found to be
in line with the more recent studies conducted on the eﬀect of tool geometry, where a higher number of ﬂutes
resulted in a better surface quality. As Franken has a higher number of ﬂutes in comparison to Unimerco,
this means a lower feed per tooth, resulting in lower surface roughness. Furthermore, the neutral helix angle
of the Franken Fiber-Cut end mill in combination with the serration marks on the ﬂutes also had a positive
inﬂuence on the surface quality.
The results obtained for the delamination study further conﬁrms the observations made with regards to
surface roughness and are in line with the literature review. A low delamination frequency was observed for a
new Franken Fibre-Cut and Unimerco, which made it diﬃcult to assess the machining parameters. This is in
slight disagreement with the ﬁndings from the literature review, where it was found that an increase in feed
rate lead to higher surface damage. However, this trend was observed for the worn tool for both tools, where
an increase in feed rate lead to a higher delamination frequency for all three spindle speeds. The eﬀect of tool
geometry was further conﬁrmed by the visual examination of the surface damage, where Franken Fiber-Cut
presented the lowest frequency. This was also found to be the case for the surface roughness, and the same
reasons apply here. However, in certain cases the surface roughness values were found to be lower for the
worn Unimerco tool, which is not evident when studying delamination frequency. This observation was also
discussed in the literature review, and indicates the importance of an overall assessment being made, and the
surface quality being based on both surface roughness and visual inspections.
As seen through the literature review, the subsequent experimental investigations and analysis of obtained
results, the surface roughness varies depending on the tool geometry, machining parameters and wear. This
validates the importance of assessing the surface quality based on the tool, the roughness and other quality
measurements such as a visual inspection of the machined parts. The variation observed between individual
roughness measurements, due to the diﬃculties associated with using a proﬁlometer, further conﬁrms this
statement. Placing the stylus in exactly the same place each time was diﬃcult, which is important as the
material is inhomogeneous and diﬀerent values might be obtained depending on where the measurements
are done. This is mainly due to the eﬀect of ﬁbre orientation on the surface roughness. Furthermore, the
measurements were made on slots with a nominal thickness of 9 mm. This distance consisted of 39 individual
plies, where each one contained carbon ﬁbres with a diameter of 10 µm, which combined with a stylus radius of
2.5 µm made measurements a challenge. The issues with a stylus based approach and measuring the variation
in roughness among the individual layers was supported by a preliminary study on optical roughness mea-
surements. Although, the experiment was only conducted on two samples, it highlighted the vast diﬀerence
of observed roughness values between the individual plies, which was due to each ﬁbre orientation responding
diﬀerently to the machining mechanisms. It emphasises the roughness measurements made in the longitudinal
direction could severely undermine the severity of the surface damage if a ply with a high roughness is not
measured upon. This is also the case for the transverse measurements, but this direction captures a wide
range of roughness values, thus appears more likely to include the layers with the highest roughness. A
ﬁnal point to make with regards to surface measurements is in the selection of roughness parameters. As
found in the literature review, the Ra value can lead to a severe underestimation of the surface damage, due
to not taking the extreme features, such as deep valleys, into account. This issue was highlighted with the
obtained roughness proﬁles, where deep valleys and high peaks could be observed in some of the graphs. These
clearly need to be taken into account to get a representative picture of the extent of the machining damage.
Further support of using the R10z value to represent the roughness was found in the optical measurements,
where a vast diﬀerence could be observed in the obtained Sa and S10z value for ply 5 on sample 1. Here, a
low roughness was found using the former parameter, whereas a high roughness value was obtained when
calculating R10z, which indicates that peaks and valleys are present, and needs to be reported. The optical
measurements also highlighted the signiﬁcantly higher roughness obtained with climb milling in comparison
the conventional milling, although further studies are needed as a comparison between to diﬀerent tools used
with varying machining parameters are diﬃcult to compare.
The surface roughness was used to validate the surface quality of machined CFRP with two diﬀerent tools,
152
==PAGE:173==
Chapter 10
10.1. Conclusion
namely Franken Fiber-Cut and Unimerco. Here, optimum parameters were found for both tools, and sta-
tistical analysis revealed that the interaction between feed rate and spindle speed was signiﬁcant. Franken
Fiber-Cut generally presented the lowest surface roughness, however both tools showed Ra values well below
the aerospace requirement of 3.2 µm for both the worn and new tool.
The following points serve as a recommendation for best machining practise for Franken Fiber-Cut and
Unimerco along with the best tool to utilise when slot milling. The results are based on the roughness and
delamination measurements along with the subsequent ANOVA analysis.
 As both new tools presented almost no delaminations at all combinations of spindle speed and feed
rate, the surface quality assessment should be made based mainly on the surface roughness, with
delaminations evaluated visually only
 Optimum machining conditions for Franken Fiber-Cut: feed rate of 1000 mm/min with a speed of 7000
RPM for transverse direction and one of 5000 RPM for the longitudinal direction.
 Optimum machining parameters for Unimerco are: Feed rate of 1000 mm/min for both directions with
a speed of 9000 RPM for transverse direction and one of 10000 RPM for longitudinal
 Franken presented the lowest roughness for both tooling conditions in the longitudinal direction
 Franken presented the lowest surface roughness in the transverse direction for the new tool
 Franken Fiber-Cut displayed the lowest roughness for the worn tool in the transverse direction except
at the lowest spindle speed and feed rate, where Unimerco were found to have the lowest roughness
value
 Roughness Surface roughness is best based on transverse direction as this gives an indication of the
entire surface and not a few plies. Furthermore, more consistent and higher results were obtained in
this direction for both Unimerco and Franken Fiber-Cut.
 For the worn tool a signiﬁcant amount of delaminations were presented. Thus, if a worn tool is being
used in production, the best machining parameters should be found through a combination of surface
roughness values and a quantitative evaluation of the delamination. By continuously assessing the eﬀect
of tool wear on surface roughness and delaminations, optimizations could be made by selecting diﬀerent
machining parameters to achieve the best quality.
 Worn Franken Fiber-Cut lowest roughness value (R10z) can be found for a feed rate of 1000 mm/min
at a speed of 6000 RPM. For the transverse direction is found to be at the same feed rate but at a speed
of 7000 RPM. In terms of delaminations for the worn Franken, the best conditions were found to be at
either 1000 mm/min or 1200 mm/min at a speed of 6000 RPM. As such, the best machining condition
appear to be at a feed rate of 1000 mm/min at a speed of 6000 RPM, as the diﬀerence observed between
7000 RPM and 6000 RPM for the transverse direction is small enough for it to be valid
 For the worn Unimerco tool the lowest roughness values (R10z) are displayed at 9000 RPM at a feed
rate of 800 mm/min for the longitudinal direction and for the transverse direction this is found to be
at 8000 RPM at a feed of 800 mm/min. In terms of minimum delaminations this was observed to be
at 8000 at a feed rate of 800 mm/min. As such, the best feed rate is at 800 mm/min with a speed of
8000 RPM. Although, this is not the case for the longitudinal direction, the values at the lowest feed
rate are very close for all three spindle speeds making the suggestion valid and in line with keeping the
surface quality high.
10.1.2
Countersinking
The aim of this study was to investigate the eﬀect of surface delaminations sustained prior to testing on the
mechanical strength of CFRP composite parts. This was done based on two testing methods, namely open hole
tensile and compression tests. For the tensile testing, the literature review showed that the length of the spec-
imens should have no inﬂuence on the strength, where it was found to be more vital to keep the width-to-hole
153
==PAGE:174==
10.1. Conclusion
Chapter 10
diameter (D/W) ratio the same across all samples. Although, the recommendations by the ASTM standard
for tensile testing was to keep the ratio at 6, studies have found that ratios of 3.3, should lead to correct failure,
thus conclusive results. This is in line with the ﬁndings obtained through tensile testing, where a correct
failure occurred in every instance and there were found to be no correlation between specimen length and
strength. The experiment also revealed that surface damage sustained prior to testing did not have a negative
eﬀect on the ultimate tensile strength, however a signiﬁcant amount of the delaminated samples displayed a
higher strength when compared to the control group. This could indicate delaminations leading to a reduction
in the stress caused by the presence of the hole, as reviewed in the literature study. There was found to be an
interdependence of strain and specimen strength, where the longer samples displayed a lower strain. This is in
disagreement with most previous ﬁndings, which have found that by using the gauge length, the strain should
be normalised, enabling a comparison across of a range of geometries. A reason for this could be because most
of the recorded displacement was due to the movement of the machine and not the sample, along with errors
associated with movement of the samples when testing. As such, a comparative analysis was only done for
specimens of similar lengths. Here, the control specimen was found to have a larger strain to failure, indicating
a better ability to absorb energy before failure. Otherwise no correlation was found between the extent of
surface damage and strain. These observations were further conﬁrmed by reviewing the results obtained from
the delamination factor - both conventional and corrected values. Here, no correlation was found between the
delamination factor and the strength. Based on these observations the delaminations present no signiﬁcant in-
ﬂuence on the tensile strength, however in some cases a higher strength were found for the damaged specimen.
The geometry of the compression test samples were comparatively based on the Short Block Compres-
sion (SBC) test due to a change of testing method. This was due to incorrect failure with the original Boeing
ﬁxture, where extra pieces had to be inserted in order for the samples to be compressed. The literature review
revealed that the former testing method was valid for short, thicker specimens, and obtained results could
be used if no end-crushing or buckling of specimens occurred. As none of these phenomenons were observed,
correct failure of the specimens occurred and based on this the tests results were deemed valid. The failure
mode was observed to be AGM, which is where the laminate fails in compression at the hole with remnants
angle plies across the hole in a 45◦orientation. As seen in the literature review, previous studies have found a
reduction in strength when delaminations are introduced, although this was mainly in the form of sub-surface
damage. Contrastingly, in this study the strength was not found to depend on the amount of delaminations
sustained prior to failure, leading to the conclusion that surface damage has an insigniﬁcant impact on
mechanical strength in comparison to sub-surface damage. An initially evaluation of the correlation between
the calculated delamination factor and strength even lead to an alternative hypothesis being introduced,
namely that the strength increased with surface damage. However, a statistical analysis revealed this not to
be the case. The test could have been aﬀected by not using a ﬁxture. Although, the visual inspection and
the obtained results revealed no indication of a wrongful test, further tests could be done with a strain gauge
to valid the observations. In addition to the standard SBC test, another was conducted with the Boeing
ﬁxture in accordance with ASTM. Here, the samples contained a honeycomb core, and due to the unknown
eﬀect of combining a honeycomb core with the surface delaminations, made a comparison with the shorter
specimens diﬃcult. As such, only limited attention were given to the theory, and the subsequent results,
although is an interesting study for further investigations. A range of problems presented itself, however
based on the results correct failure occurred, where both specimens failed through shear failure. C7 contained
delaminations sustained prior to testing and presented the highest compressive strength and failure to strain.
This is in comparison to the control specimen, C8 and indicated that the delamination acted as a stress relief,
resulting in higher strength. However, due to the small sample size, this was not conclusive. A comparison
between C7 and C8 to the shorter specimens tested through the use of the SBC test method, revealed that
the longer specimens presented a higher strength. As all specimens failed according to speciﬁcations, the
higher strength could have been an indicated of the extra support, stiﬀness and strength provided by the
honeycomb core. In conjunction with the mechanical tests, a moisture control assessment was made of the
CFRP composite part. This was found to be necessary, as initially all specimens contained enough moisture
for the weight to be aﬀected. This could have lead to inconclusive results as moisture content has an eﬀect
on the strength of CFRP composites.
Through the visual analysis of the open hole tensile and compression test results, there were found to
154
==PAGE:175==
Chapter 10
10.2. Future Work
be a range in the extent of matrix and ﬁbre cracking across the samples. This indicated that the resin-to-ﬁbre
content was not ideal and voids being present in the composite as found through the literature review. How-
ever, the acid digestion test conﬁrmed that the void content was acceptably low with values ranging between
0.13-0.29. All the specimen fell below the accepted limit of 1% as set by the aerospace industry. Based on
the low values obtained for the void content, this has not aﬀected the strength.
Generally, the delaminated samples presented a slightly higher strength, which could have been due to
the damage acting as a stress relief caused by the hole. This observation was based upon ASTM D-6484M-04
(2015) and ASTM D-5766M-02a (2007) eﬀect. The diﬀerence between the samples however, did not appear
to be signiﬁcant enough for it to be a ﬁnal conclusion.
10.2
Future Work
Investigating the surface quality of CFRP composite parts through the use of surface roughness measure-
ments, microscopic observations and mechanical testing has highlighted the complex and intrinsic nature of
the material. This was particular evident through the contact based surface roughness measurements. When
data was obtained in the longitudinal direction ensuring the stylus was placed in the same layer was found
to be essential as the obtained values varied signiﬁcantly otherwise and this proved diﬃcult. The transverse
direction was also measured, which was a better representative of the overall roughness of the part. However,
only a proﬁle was drawn across the machined surface, which might miss severe damage caused by the milling.
As such, an optical method is a good alternative to the contact based measurements, which enables the
areal roughness for each ply to be captured accurately. Using this technique means using a larger area of
assessing the roughness, which can be done accurately for each individual ply, ensuring the quality of the
part is accurately captured. In this study only two samples were measured upon using the optical method,
so future studies could include a new investigation of the experiment in order to conﬁrm the obtained results
with the stylus based approach. Further studies could also include an investigation on the cutting forces
observed during slot milling along with the temperatures generated as this has a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the
resulting quality.
Further studies in terms of mechanical testing of composite parts would include using a ﬁxture for new
compression tests to validate the results obtained using the short block compression method. Furthermore,
in order to accurately capture the strain an extensometer could be employed during testing, which would
allow for the stiﬀness of the samples to be calculated. The same approach applies to further work with tensile
testing with regards to strain, however samples of varying lengths should be used again to conﬁrm whether
a correlation exists between length and strain as observed in this study. There appeared to be no correlation
between surface damage and strength, thus additional studies could involve investigations of the correlation
between sub-surface damage and strength. Furthermore, experiments on whether delamination introduced
by countersinking results in sub-surface damage could also be done.

==PAGE:187==
Appendix A
Slot Milling
This chapter includes all the raw data obtained during surface roughness measurements in the form of
roughness proﬁles. The raw data in tabulated form of Ra and R10z values have been attached as a separate
Excel sheet, where the tool, condition, and measurement direction have been clearly stated in bold writing.
Please notice that a comma is used to separate decimals. Furthermore, the values used for the ANOVA test
are also included in the Excel Sheet named ”Raw Data Roughness Measurements Slot Milling”, which are
the values obtained in the transverse direction for a new tool.
A.1
Surface Roughness Data in Transverse Direction for Franken
Fiber-Cut
In this section, the obtained roughness proﬁles for the new and worn tool in the transverse direction will be
presented. Five measurements were done for each combination of spindle speed and feed rate, which have
been combined in one graph, where the average value for R10z and Ra can be found in the caption along
with the machining parameters.

Appendix B
Countersinking
The raw data obtained for the tensile and compression test have been attached as a separate Excel Sheet
named ”Raw Data Tensile Countersinking” and ”Raw Data Compression Countersinking”. As such, in
this chapter the obtained values used for calculating the delamination factor are presented along with the
calculations for void content.
B.1
Delamination Factor
Tables B.1.1 and B.1.2 illustrates the obtained values used to calculate the delamination factor Fd and Fda
for tensile and compression testing. The Adel value is the delaminated area found with imageJ, Amax was
found based on Dmax, thus Amax = D2
max
π
and Anom is simply the nominal area found as Anom = D2
0
π . Here,
D0 is 6.9367 mm
